Response to Objection on Divine Creation of Sin and Punishment
Author responds to Mu'tazili objection about God creating theft and then punishing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response turns the Mu'tazili objection about divine predestination and punishment back on itself, exposing an inconsistency in their own framework.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili objection against predestination ultimately undermines their own position.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection from the Mu'tazila: if God creates the theft, how can He punish the thief? The response is both subtle and decisive, showing that the Mu'tazili framework itself makes punishment impossible. By denying human power over the very attributes that define actions, the Mu'tazila cannot explain human responsibility, forcing them into a dilemma that supports the Maturidi position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The passage begins with the Mu'tazili question. Notice the examples: theft, adultery, disbelief. They argue that if God creates the act, punishing seems unjust.
This objection is still common today in discussions of predestination.
2
Identify the author's counter-argument
The author responds by examining the Mu'tazili view of human power. According to them, human power does not extend to attributes like 'thing-ness' or 'accident-ness'. So what exactly did the human do?
The author is using their own premises against them.
3
Understand the dilemma
The author argues that if humans have no power over those attributes, then they did nothing. So punishment is even more problematic on their view than on the Maturidi view.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author says whatever the Mu'tazila answer, it defends the Maturidi position. Because any answer will concede that humans do create something, which the Maturidis affirm.
This shows the strength of the Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الجواب عن قولهم : كيف يجوز أن يقال يخلق الله تعالى في يد إنسان سرقه ثم يأمر بقطعها ؟ وكذا هذا في الزنا وكذا يخلق فعلاً ثم يعاقب عليه؟ قيل: وإذا لم يفعل السارق شيئًا ولا الزاني ولا الكافر ، وكان الزنا والسرقة والكفر في العدم أشياء وأعراضاً وأعيانا ووجودها أعيانها أو ليس معنى وراءها ولم تتعلق قدرة العبد بالشيئية ولا العرضية ولا العينية، ولم تتعلق بمعنى وراءها فماذا فعله، وعلى أي فعل يقطع ويحد ويعاقب ؟ فما أجابوا فهو جواب لهم.
And likewise the answer to their statement: 'How can it be said that Allah creates in the hand of a man a theft, then commands that it be cut off?' And likewise this in the case of adultery, and likewise He creates an action then punishes for it? It is said: And if the thief did nothing, nor the adulterer, nor the disbeliever, and the adultery, theft, and disbelief in non-existence were things, accidents, and entities, and their existence is their entities or not a meaning beyond them, and the power of the servant is not attached to thing-ness, nor accident-ness, nor entity-ness, and is not attached to a meaning beyond them, then what did he do? And for which action is his hand cut, and he is punished with ḥadd, and punished? So whatever they answer, it is an answer for them.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses another Mu'tazili objection: 'How can God create theft in a hand and then command cutting it off?' The author argues that according to the Mu'tazila's denial of human power over attributes like thing-ness, accident-ness, etc., the thief did nothing. Since theft, adultery, and disbelief are merely attributes of actions in non-existence, and existence is the same as the entity, and the servant's power is not attached to those attributes, then what exactly did the human do to deserve punishment? The same dilemma applies: whatever the Mu'tazila answer, it defends the Maturidi position.

Key Terms
السرقة
الزنا
الكفر
العدم
القدرة
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine predestination makes punishment unjust because God creates the sin.
Correction: The author demonstrates that even without predestination, the Mu'tazili framework cannot explain human responsibility, so the objection boomerangs.

Checkpoint
In the response to the objection about God creating theft, what does the author argue regarding the attributes of theft in non-existence?
A) The servant has power over those attributes.
B) The servant's power is not attached to those attributes, so the servant did nothing.
C) The servant's power is attached to the entity of theft.
D) The servant creates the attribute of theft.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili objection against divine predestination fails because their own denial of human power over attributes makes punishment inexplicable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When I sin, do I ever catch myself thinking, 'But Allah created this action in me' as an excuse? How does this passage expose the weakness of that excuse and call me to own my sin fully before Him?
?
The Mu'tazila denied human power over attributes to preserve God's transcendence, yet their position made punishment unjust. Am I willing to accept the full weight of divine command and human responsibility, even when it challenges my understanding of fairness?
?
If I truly believed that my choices are entirely my own under Allah's creation (as Maturidi teaches), how would that change the way I repent and strive to obey? What would I need to change today to live with that conviction?

The Punishment Dilemma: Maturidi Response
Author responds to Mu'tazila objection about punishment for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response to the Mu'tazila objection about punishment for existence turns the objection back on itself and leads to the doctrine that the servant has genuine action.

Core Idea
The punishment dilemma is no worse for Sunnis than for Mu'tazila.

The Mu'tazila raised a clever objection: if only God and creatures exist, is punishment for God's existence or the creature's? The Maturidi response flips the question, showing the Mu'tazila cannot escape the same dilemma. More importantly, the author explains that this argument is a gratuity; the real foundation is that the servant indeed has an action, even if its modality is unknown. Let's examine the text directly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila ask whether punishment is for God's existence or the creature's. This is a dilemma they think refutes predestination.
2
See the Maturidi retort
The author responds by asking a parallel question: if a thing exists that no one did, is punishment for that thing? This leaves the Mu'tazila without an answer.
The retort uses analogy to show the objection is not specific to Sunnism.
3
Note the author's disclaimer
The author calls this dialectical answer a 'gratuity' (تبرع) — it's not the core proof. The real proof is coming next.
This shows the author values substantive theology over mere polemics.
4
Grasp the real proof
Even though the servant is not a creator or inventor, he still has an action (فعل) — even if we don't know its modality. This grounds punishment.
The action is real but its 'how' is beyond our imagination.
5
Conclude the lesson
Punishment is justified because the servant's action is real. The dilemma is resolved without denying divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الجواب عن قولهم: إذا لم يكن وجود إلا للخالق ولمخلوقه أفيعاقب على وجود الخالق أم على وجود المخلوق؟ قيل: وإذا لم يوجد إلا شيء لم يفعله أحد أفيعاقب على الشيء؟ فما أجابوا فهو لهم جواب، وهذا كله تبرع منا فإنا إذا أقمنا الدلالة على أن العبد وإن لم يكن مخترعا خالقا فله فعل وإن كان لا يقع كيفية ذلك في أوهامنا، فنقول: بأنه يعاقب
And likewise the answer to their statement: 'If there is no existence except that of the Creator and of His creature, is he punished for the existence of the Creator or for the existence of the creature?' It is said: And if there exists nothing except a thing that no one did, is he punished for the thing? So whatever they answer, it is an answer for them. And all of this is a gratuity from us, for when we establish the proof that the servant, though not an inventor or creator, still has an action, even if the modality of that does not occur in our imaginations, we say: He is punished...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage responds to the objection: 'If existence is only from God and the creature, is punishment for God's existence or the creature's?' The author retorts that if existence is simply a thing that no one created, then punishment would be for that thing. The Mu'tazila cannot escape this dilemma. The author then notes that these answers are a gratuity; the real proof is that the servant indeed has an action even if its modality is unknown. The passage ends mid-sentence but establishes that punishment is justified.

Key Terms
الوجود
الخالق
المخلوق
التبرع
الدلالة
الفعل
الكيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The punishment dilemma is unique to predestinarian views and cannot be answered.
Correction: The author shows that the same dilemma applies to the Mu'tazila, and the actual solution lies in the servant's genuine action.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the objection regarding punishment on the existence of the Creator vs. creature?
A) It is a valid objection that requires a new proof.
B) The answer is that punishment is for the creature's existence.
C) Whatever the Mu'tazila answer, it defends the Maturidi position.
D) The objection is irrelevant because punishment is only for actions.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi response to the punishment objection reveals that the real foundation is the servant's genuine action, not a mere logical trick.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a dilemma about God's justice and human accountability, do you actually believe that your actions are genuinely yours, even if the details are beyond your understanding? What difference would that conviction make in how you approach your deeds tomorrow?
?
If the modality of your actions is beyond your imagination, how much do you rely on your own reasoning versus submitting to what God has revealed about accountability? Are you willing to accept mystery in matters of faith?
?
The passage calls the dialectical response a 'gratuity' — do you ever try to defend your faith with arguments that are merely gracious concessions, while neglecting the solid foundation of revealed truth? Where in your life have you substituted clever reasoning for humble submission to Allah's decree?

The Batiniyya Heresy on Divine Existence
Nasafi condemns the Batiniyya's claim about God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Batiniyya's heretical claim that God is neither existent nor non-existent and why it leads to atheism and sophistry.

Core Idea
Denying God's existence is atheism; denying His non-existence is sophistry.

The Batiniyya sect proposed a radical negation of both existence and non-existence for God. This passage from Nasafi identifies this claim as a folly that leads to intellectual ruin. Let's examine it closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once, then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi frames the claim as a 'folly' and immediately gives its consequence.
The phrase 'wallāh al-muwaffiq' is a prayer, not part of the argument.
2
Identify the heretical claim
The Batiniyya assert that the Creator is neither existent (موجود) nor non-existent (معدوم). Understand why this is a direct violation of the law of excluded middle.
In logic, every entity must be either existent or non-existent; denying both is impossible.
3
Understand the consequences
Nasafi says this leads to 'ilḥād' (atheism) and 'safsaṭa' (sophistry). Discuss how denying God's existence is atheism, and denying His non-existence is a sophistical rejection of reality.
This shows that the Batiniyya position is not a higher insight but a logical error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من جهالات الباطنية فإنهم يزعمون أن الباري عز وجل ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، وهذا قول يؤدي إلى الإلحاد والسفسطة، والله الموفق.
From the follies of the Batiniyya: they claim that the Creator, mighty and majestic, is neither existent nor non-existent. This statement leads to atheism and sophistry. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a concise condemnation of a heretical position held by the Batiniyya sect. Nasafi states that they claim God is neither existent (موجود) nor non-existent (معدوم). He declares that this statement leads to 'ilḥād' (atheism/heresy) and 'safsaṭa' (sophistry), implying it is a denial of reason and reality. The phrase 'wallāh al-muwaffiq' closes with a prayer for divine guidance.

Key Terms
الباطنية
موجود
معدوم
الإلحاد
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Batiniyya's claim that God is neither existent nor non-existent is a sophisticated metaphysical insight beyond ordinary categories.
Correction: Nasafi clearly labels it a 'folly' that leads to atheism and sophistry. It contradicts the necessary rational principle that everything must be either existent or non-existent.

Checkpoint
What do the Batiniyya claim about Allah according to this passage?
A) He is both existent and non-existent.
B) He is neither existent nor non-existent.
C) He is existent only.
D) He is non-existent only.

Key Takeaway
God's existence is rationally necessary; denying it leads to absurdity and heresy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to avoid the clear truth of God's existence by resorting to vague, indefinite descriptions? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of such attempts?
?
If someone told you that God is beyond existence and non-existence, would you be able to identify the logical error? How would you respond using the reasoning in this passage?
?
This lesson calls the Batiniyya claim 'follies.' Is there any area of your own belief where you have entertained a comfortable ambiguity about God rather than embracing the decisive truth? What step will you take today to affirm His necessary existence?

Mu'tazila and Dualist Principles: Agreement and Modification
Describes Mu'tazila adoption and modification of Dualist principles on evil and benefit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila adopted two key principles from the Dualists and modified them regarding the nature of evil and benefit.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila adopted Dualist principles but rejected their application to created entities.

This passage examines the historical and theological relationship between the Mu'tazila and the Dualists (Zoroastrians). It reveals that the Mu'tazila built their school upon two fundamental principles borrowed from the Dualists: that creating evil is evil, and that action devoid of benefit is foolishness. However, they differed significantly in how they applied these principles, particularly regarding which things are considered evil and the scope of benefit. The author sets up the stage for a refutation by highlighting these agreements and disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument without analysis. Then reread with attention to the structure.
Notice the author's accusatory tone toward the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the two shared principles
Find the two principles that both groups agree on: (1) creating evil is evil, (2) an action without benefit is foolishness.
The passage states this explicitly.
3
Understand the Dualist position on evil
Dualists limit evil to things with no benefit for the doer, thus positing a separate creator for harmful entities like impure bodies.
This leads to the dualist doctrine of two creators.
4
Compare the Mu'tazila modification
Mu'tazila deny that created entities (apes, pigs, Iblis) are evil; they restrict evil to actions. They also expand benefit to include benefit for others, not just the doer.
They rely on scripture but reinterpret it.
5
Reflect on the method
Both groups base these principles on analogy from the witnessed world (al-shahid). This will be critiqued later.
This sets up the author's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما تلقنوا هذا من إخوانهم الثنوية فبنوا المذهب على قواعدهم ونسجوا على منوالهم، فزعموا أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن ما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه وتعلقوا بالشاهد، غير أن الثنوية يزعمون أن ما لا منفعة فيه للفاعل فهو قبيح، ولهذا زعموا أن كل فعل للنور فهو في نفع نفسه هو وخلاصه عن وثائق الظلمة، فصوبت المعتزلة الثنوية في هاتين القاعدتين وزعمت أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وهو سفه، غير أنهم خالفوا الثنوية في تخليق الأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة والأعيان الضارة، فزعمت الثنوية أنها لما كانت قبيحة كان إيجادها قبيحا وهي محدثة فلابد لها من صانع آخر، وأنكرت المعتزلة قبحها مع شهادة الله تعالى بخبثها وتشبيه الكفر الخبيث بها بقوله: "وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ" وزعمت أن القردة والخنازير وإبليس - عليهم اللعنة - ومردة الشياطين كلهم أشياء مستحسنة لا خبث فيها ولا قبح، وإنما القبح في الأفعال، فأنكروا أن يكون الله تعالى خالقًا لها، ولهذا أنكروا حرمة الأعيان النازلة من حرمة الأفعال منزلة الحفظ من الحماية لما لا حرمة إلا بوصف القبح ولا قبح في الأعيان لما أنها لو كانت قبيحة لم يكن إيجادها حكمة، وكذا خالفوا الثنوية في تعليق الحكمة بنفع الفاعل نفسه بل علقوها بمطلق النفع إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وتمسك الفريقان - أعني المعتزلة والثنوية - في الأمرين جميعًا أعني - أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن الفعل الخالي عن النفع سفه – بالشاهد
They only learned this from their dualist brothers, so they built the school upon their principles and wove upon their pattern. They claimed that creating the evil (al-qabih) is evil, and that what is devoid of benefit is foolishness, and they clung to the witnessed (al-shahid). However, the dualists claim that what has no benefit for the doer is evil, and for this they claimed that every act of light is for its own benefit and its deliverance from the bonds of darkness. The Mu'tazila approved the dualists in these two principles and claimed that creating the evil is evil and is foolishness, but they disagreed with the dualists regarding the creation of abominable, filthy bodies and harmful entities. The dualists claimed that since they are evil, their creation is evil, and they are originated, so they must have another maker. The Mu'tazila denied their evil despite God's testimony to their foulness and His likening foul unbelief to it by His saying: 'And the example of a foul word.' They claimed that apes, pigs, Iblis—may curses be upon them—and the rebellious devils are all commendable things, with no foulness or evil in them, and that evil is only in actions. Thus they denied that God is the Creator of them, and for this they denied the prohibition of entities, descending the prohibition of entities from the prohibition of actions, like preservation from protection, since there is no prohibition except by the description of evil, and no evil in entities, because if they were evil, their creation would not be wisdom. Similarly, they disagreed with the dualists in attaching wisdom to the benefit of the doer himself, but rather attached it to absolute benefit, either for the doer or for others. And the two groups—I mean the Mu'tazila and the dualists—clung in both matters—namely, that creating the evil is evil and that the action devoid of benefit is foolishness—to the witnessed (al-shahid).
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage examines the historical relationship between Mu'tazila and Zoroastrian Dualists (al-thunawiyya). It begins by stating that the Mu'tazila took from the Dualists the two principles that (1) creating evil is evil, and (2) an action devoid of benefit is foolishness. However, the author notes a split: the Dualists restricted evil to what has no benefit for the doer, whereas the Mu'tazila, in approval, applied the same principle but disagreed on which things count as evil. The Dualists considered certain created bodies (like impure animals) to be evil, requiring a separate creator (the principle of two creators). The Mu'tazila denied that these entities are evil, arguing that God's own description of them as 'foul' does not entail moral evil in the entities themselves; rather, evil is in actions, not in created essences. Consequently, they denied that God creates such things as evil acts, and they reinterpreted the prohibition of entities as derived from actions. On benefit, the Dualists tied wisdom to benefit for the doer, while the Mu'tazila extended it to benefit for others. Both groups, however, based these principles on analogy with the witnessed world (al-shahid). The author is setting up the refutation that follows.

Key Terms
الثنوية
المعتزلة
القبيح
الشاهد
النفع
السفه
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Dualists agreed on everything regarding the two principles.
Correction: They disagreed on which things are evil (entities vs actions) and on the scope of benefit (doer only vs absolute).

Checkpoint
On which point did the Mu'tazila disagree with the Dualists regarding the two principles?
A) The principle that creating evil is evil.
B) The principle that action devoid of benefit is foolishness.
C) The classification of certain created objects as evil and the attribution of benefit.
D) The use of analogy from the witnessed world.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila adopted two principles from the Dualists but rejected their application to created entities, restricting evil to actions and expanding benefit to include others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Mu'tazila refused to call apes, pigs, and Iblis 'evil' even though God declared them foul, do you see any area in your own life where you have redefined good and evil based on your own reasoning rather than submitting to God's judgment? Will you repent of that today, asking Allah to give you a true understanding of His names and attributes?
?
The Dualists believed that harm and evil come from a separate creator, not from God. Have you ever, even subconsciously, attributed the bad things in your life to something other than Allah—like bad luck, people, or even yourself, as if God has no control? How does this lesson confront that hidden shirk? Will you make a determined effort to say 'Qadr Allah wa ma sha'a fa'al' (This is the decree of Allah; He does what He wills) even in hardship?
?
Both the Mu'tazila and Dualists relied on analogy from the witnessed world to judge what God should do. In your daily decisions—like worship, work, or relationships—are you using the same flawed reasoning: judging what is 'wise' by your own limited standards rather than trusting that Allah's commands are full of wisdom even when you don't see the benefit? What specific command or prohibition do you struggle to accept as wise? Will you commit to obeying it without demanding to see the benefit first?

The Dilemma of Analogy with the Witnessed
Objection to Mu'tazila: dilemma from shahid to divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection based on the dualist conception of benefit forces the Mu'tazila into a dilemma that undermines their reliance on analogy with the witnessed world.

Core Idea
If God is judged by witnessed standards, He must be either unwise or needy—both impossible.

This passage presents a powerful objection to the Mu'tazila method of drawing analogies from the witnessed world (al-shahid) to God. The objector, using the dualist view that an act is only good if it benefits the doer, argues that since God does not benefit from His actions, the Mu'tazila face an impossible choice. Either they deny God's wisdom or attribute need to Him, both of which are against Islam. The objection thus turns the Mu'tazila's own method against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
2
Identify the dualist premise
Notice that the objection starts by adopting the dualist criterion: an act is good only if it benefits the doer. This is the standard being applied.
This premise is crucial for the dilemma.
3
Trace the dilemma
See what happens when this standard is applied to God: either His actions lack benefit so He is not wise, or if He is wise, He must benefit and thus be needy. Both are heretical.
The final sentence nullifies the entire method if both options are rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل لهم : ما تنكرون على من يقول لا يحسن الفعل في الشاهد إلا لنفع نفسه كما زعمت الثنوية، ومن نفع غيره إنما حسن فعله لأن من نفع غيره نفع نفسه في الحقيقة إنما بنيل الثواب، وإما باكتساب الجاه، وإما بإحراز المحمدة والثناء الجميل حتى إن من نفع غيره بلا حمد يستحق ولا أجر ينال لا يكون ذلك حكمة، فإن تمسكتم بالشاهد فقولوا: إن الله تعالى ليس بحكيم لما أنه لا ينفع بفعله، وإن قلتم: إنه حكيم فقولوا: إنه منتفع محل للحاجات دافع للضرورات، فبأي أمر تمسكوا خرجوا عن الدين، وإن أبوهما أبطلوا استشهادهم بالشاهد
And it was said to them: What do you say about one who says that an action in the witnessed (al-shahid) is only good if it benefits oneself, as the dualists claimed? And benefiting others is only good because the one who benefits others actually benefits himself, either by obtaining reward, or by acquiring status, or by achieving commendation and fine praise, such that one who benefits others without deserved praise or obtained reward is not wisdom. If you cling to the witnessed, then say that God is not wise because He does not benefit by His action. And if you say He is wise, then say that He is benefited, subject to needs, repeller of necessities. By whichever matter you cling, you exit from the religion. And if they reject both, their inference by the witnessed is nullified.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is an objection posed to the Mu'tazila from the perspective of a Dualist or a critic. It argues that if the Mu'tazila insist on basing their moral judgments on analogy with the witnessed world (al-shahid), they must accept the consequences. In the witnessed world, an act is only considered good (hasan) if it benefits the doctor himself, even when benefiting others—because ultimately the doer gains reward, status, or praise. If an action benefits others without any such return, it is not considered wisdom. Applying this to God: God does not benefit from His actions (since He is self-sufficient), so if the Mu'tazila cling to the witnessed standard, they must either deny that God is wise (since His actions lack benefit) or attribute need and benefit to God (which is anthropomorphic and heretical). The conclusion is that whichever path they take, they leave the religion. If they reject both options, then their entire method of inference (istishhad) from the witnessed is nullified. Thus the objection forces the Mu'tazila into a dilemma.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الحكمة
النفع
الاستشهاد
الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this objection only targets the Dualist position.
Correction: In fact, it turns the Mu'tazila's own method against them, showing that their reliance on shahid leads to heretical conclusions.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the objection present to the Mu'tazila?
A) Either accept Dualist cosmology or abandon Islam.
B) Either deny that God is wise or attribute need to Him.
C) Either reject the witnessed world or accept that God creates evil.
D) Either affirm divine omnipotence or deny human free will.

Key Takeaway
The objection exposes a flaw in the Mu'tazila method: judging God by witnessed standards leads to absurd conclusions that contradict Islamic teachings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's actions in your life, do you ever measure them by what seems beneficial to you? How does this lesson expose the flaw in that approach?
?
The objection shows that applying human standards to God leads to absurdity. Where in your own thinking about God have you assumed He operates like a human? Will you repent from that and submit to His transcendence?
?
If you were to defend God's wisdom without using analogy with this world, what would you say? Are you willing to let go of every analogy that makes God seem like a larger version of a human being?

Refuting the Dualists' Analogy: The Distinction Between Shahid and Gha'ib
Imam al-Nasafi refutes the dualists by distinguishing between shahid and gha'ib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi uses the distinction between the witnessed (shahid) and unseen (gha'ib) to refute the dualists' argument that God's actions must be judged by the same moral standards as human actions.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by the moral categories of the created world.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi addresses a key dualist objection: if God gives power to a disbeliever knowing he will commit evil, how can God be good? By showing the essential difference between the witnessed realm (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib), Nasafi demonstrates that the dualists' analogy is false. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist's claim
The dualists analogize God's actions to human actions, arguing that what is evil in human interactions must also be evil for God.
This is a common argument against divine actions that appear unjust.
2
Examine the specific case
God gives power to a disbeliever who will insult and transgress against Him, even though God knows the outcome. Note that God creates the power and strength for the act.
3
See the conclusion
In the witnessed world, such an act is evil (qabih), but from God it is good (hasan). This establishes a fundamental distinction between shahid and gha'ib, invalidating the dualists' analogy.
The distinction is crucial: what is evil in the created realm may not be evil from the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على المنع، ولا يكتفى بذلك بل يخلق قدرة ذلك الفعل والشدة في الآلة، ويعطي الكافر قدرة مع علمه أنه يشتمه بها ويعتدي عليه ومثل هذا في الشاهد قبيح، وقد حسن ذلك منه ووقعت المفارقة بين الشاهد والغائب، فبطل بذلك استشهادهم وتسويتهم بين الشاهد والغائب.
Concerning prevention, He is not content with that, but He creates the power of that act and the strength in the instrument, and He gives the disbeliever power while knowing that he will insult Him by it and transgress against Him. The likes of this in the witnessed (shahid) is evil (qabih), and that has been good from Him, and the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen (gha'ib) occurs. Thus their analogy and equation between the witnessed and the unseen is invalidated.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is part of a refutation of the dualists (thunawiyya) who argue that God's actions must be judged by the same standards as human actions. The author presents a specific case: God gives a disbeliever the power to insult and attack Him, knowing he will use it. In the witnessed world, such an act would be evil (qabih). Yet from God, this same act is good (hasan), proving that there is a fundamental difference between the witnessed and the unseen. Therefore, the dualists' analogy is invalid.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
القبيح
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an act is evil when done by a human, it must also be evil when done by God.
Correction: The passage shows that God's actions are not subject to the same moral categories as human actions because of the fundamental distinction between shahid (witnessed) and gha'ib (unseen).

Checkpoint

A) Because the disbeliever has free will.
B) Because it is beneficial for the disbeliever.
C) Because the standards of the witnessed world do not apply to the unseen.
D) Because God does not know the outcome.

Key Takeaway
The dualists' analogy fails because God's actions are evaluated by a different standard than human actions, as shown by the example of giving power to the disbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Dualists' Criterion of Benefit
Imam al-Nasafi refutes the dualists' claim that acts are only good if they benefit the doer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dualists' claim that acts are good only if beneficial leads to an impossible dilemma about God's wisdom.

Core Idea
If benefit is the standard for goodness, God cannot be wise without being needy.

The dualists (thunawiyya) argued that an act is only good if it brings benefit to the doer. Imam al-Nasafi uses their own logic against them: even in human acts, benefiting others ultimately benefits oneself. When applied to Allah, this criterion forces a stark choice—either Allah lacks wisdom because He does not benefit from His acts, or He is subject to needs. Both are impossible. This passage exposes the internal contradiction of their position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualists' claim
Read the opening sentence: they claim acts in the witnessed world are only good if they benefit the doer. Notice how this is a criterion drawn from human experience.
2
Notice the analysis of 'benefiting others'
The author argues that even when we benefit others, it is ultimately self-benefit—through reward, status, or praise. This shows that their criterion actually admits hidden self-interest.
This refutes the purity of altruism in the dualists' framework.
3
Apply to God
If the same standard is applied to Allah, then since He does not benefit from His acts, He would not be 'wise' by their definition. Alternatively, if they insist He is wise, they must admit He is benefited, which implies need.
This is the dilemma that destroys their position.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The author concludes that whichever horn they choose, they depart from religion. If they reject the analogy, they undercut their own method of reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل لهم : ما تنكرون على من يقول لا يحسن الفعل في الشاهد إلا لنفع نفسه كما زعمت الثنوية، ومن نفع غيره إنما حسن فعله لأن من نفع غيره نفع نفسه في الحقيقة إنما بنيل الثواب، وإما باكتساب الجاه، وإما بإحراز المحمدة والثناء الجميل حتى إن من نفع غيره بلا حمد يستحق ولا أجر ينال لا يكون ذلك حكمة، فإن تمسكتم بالشاهد فقولوا: إن الله تعالى ليس بحكيم لما أنه لا ينفع بفعله، وإن قلتم: إنه حكيم فقولوا: إنه منتفع محل للحاجات دافع للضرورات، فبأي أمر تمسكوا خرجوا عن الدين، وإن أبوهما أبطلوا استشهادهم بالشاهد
And it was said to them: What do you deny against the one who says that an act in the witnessed (shahid) is only good (hasan) if it benefits himself, as the dualists (thunawiyya) claimed? And one who benefits others, his act is only good because benefiting others is actually benefiting himself, either by attaining reward, or by acquiring status, or by earning praise and commendation, to the extent that one who benefits others without deserving praise or receiving no reward—that is not wisdom. So if you adhere to the witnessed (analogy), then say that God is not wise because He does not benefit by His act; and if you say He is wise, then say that He is benefited, subject to needs, repeller of necessities. By whichever matter they hold, they depart from religion. And if they refuse, they invalidate their analogy with the witnessed.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of the dualists' claim that an act is only good if it benefits the doer. The author argues that even in human affairs, benefiting others ultimately benefits oneself through reward, status, or praise. If that standard is applied to God, then since God does not benefit from His acts, He would not be wise according to their logic. Alternatively, if they insist God is wise, they must admit He is in need of benefits—which is impossible. Thus, whichever horn of the dilemma they choose, their position collapses.

Key Terms
الثنوية
الحسن
الحكمة
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions can be judged by the same standards of benefit as human actions.
Correction: Applying human criteria of benefit to God leads to either denying His wisdom or attributing need to Him, both inconceivable.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem the author identifies with the dualists' claim that acts are good only if they benefit the doer?
A) It is contradicted by the fact that people do benefit others.
B) It forces either denying God's wisdom or attributing need to Him.
C) It is an innovation not found in the Qur'an.
D) It confuses divine and human actions.

Key Takeaway
The dualists' criterion of benefit cannot be applied to Allah without leading to an impossible contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Objection to Allah Creating Disbelief
Objection and answer about calling Allah 'Creator of disbelief'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a classical theological objection against Allah creating disbelief is structurally identical to a dualist argument and how the answer exposes this borrowing.

Core Idea
The objection is not original; it is recycled from dualist polemics.

This passage presents an objection that seems strong at first: if Allah created disbelief, then calling Him 'Creator of disbelief' would be true and permissible, but since we do not say such things, He must not have created it. The answer, however, reveals that this very logic was used by dualists to argue against Allah creating impure things like apes. By recognizing this borrowed argument, we see the weakness of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note how the objector frames his argument: if Allah created disbelief, then calling Him 'Creator of disbelief' would be truthful, and truth cannot be forbidden.
The objector assumes that truthfulness alone makes a phrase permissible.
2
Identify the answer's strategy
The answer does not engage the logic directly. Instead, it points out that this argument is borrowed from the dualists. Notice the phrase 'this is a question you have received from your brothers the dualists.'
The answer uses a historical-genetic argument to undermine the objection.
3
Understand the dualist parallel
The dualists used the same reasoning: if Allah created impure bodies, then calling Him 'Creator of apes' would be permissible. The objection against created disbelief is structurally identical.
This shows that the objector is unknowingly using a heretical framework.
4
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The passage cuts off, but the implication is clear: the objection is invalid because it originates from false premises. Reflect on why tracing an argument to its source can refute it.
The answer would likely continue by showing the difference between creating and approving.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو كان الله تعالى هو الذي تولى تخليق الكفر والمعاصي لكان يجوز أن يقال يا خالق الكفر والمعاصي إذ هو يكون صدقًا، والصدق لا يمنع عنه دل أنه لم يخلق ذلك.
قيل لهم: هذا سؤال تلقيتموه من إخوانكم الثنوية حيث يزعمون أنه تعالى لو كان خالقًا للأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة الكان يجوز أن يقال يا خالق القردة
For if it is said: If Allah Most High is the one who undertook the creation of disbelief and sins, then it would be permissible to say 'O Creator of disbelief and sins', since that would be true, and truth is not prevented from Him; this indicates that He did not create that. It is said to them: This is a question you have received from your brothers the dualists, where they claim that if He Most High were the creator of impure and filthy bodies, then it would be permissible to say 'O Creator of apes...'
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of its answer. The objection states that if Allah created disbelief and sins, then calling Him 'Creator of disbelief and sins' would be true and permissible, but since that is forbidden, He must not have created them. The answer refutes by pointing out that this objection is borrowed from the dualists (al-thunawiyya) who used a similar argument against Allah creating impure bodies like apes. The implication is that the objection is flawed and derivative; the full refutation likely continues beyond the available text.

Key Terms

الكفر
المعاصي
خالق
الثنوية
القردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection against Allah creating disbelief is an original, strong argument.
Correction: The objection is actually borrowed from dualist arguments about impure bodies, losing its force.

Checkpoint

A) The Mu'tazila.
B) The Jahmiyya.

D) The philosophers.

Key Takeaway
An objection may be refuted not by defeating its logic but by exposing its heretical origins.

Refuting the Objection about Calling God Creator of Kufr
Imam Nasafi refutes the objection by comparing it to the dualist argument about foul bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God cannot be called 'Creator of disbelief' and Imam Nasafi's refutation by showing its origin from Dualist arguments.

Core Idea
An argument borrowed from heretics cannot disprove God's creation of all things.

Imam Nasafi addresses a common objection against the Ash'ari position that God creates all actions, including disbelief. Critics argue that if God created disbelief, one could address Him as 'Creator of disbelief,' but since this is not said, it cannot be true. Nasafi reveals the source of this objection and refutes it by analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the refutation.
The refutation uses a parallel argument from the Dualists.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors claim that if God created kufr, it would be true to call Him 'Creator of kufr'. Since this is not said, God must not have created it.
This is a common Ash'ari theological objection from some Muslim groups.
3
Understand Nasafi's counter
Nasafi points out that the objectors borrowed this argument from the Dualists (Manichaeans). The Dualists used the same reasoning about foul bodies (monkeys, pigs, etc.) to argue that God did not create them because we do not call Him 'Creator of filth'.
The Dualists were a heretical group, so borrowing from them weakens the argument.
4
Grasp the parallel
If the objectors' reasoning is valid, then the Dualists' reasoning is also valid. But the Muslims reject the Dualists' conclusion, so the objectors must reject their own logic.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the method
This refutation shows that the objection is neither new nor sound. It is a borrowed argument that leads to unacceptable consequences.
Nasafi defends the orthodox position that God is the Creator of all things, including good and evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو كان الله تعالى هو الذي تولى تخليق الكفر والمعاصي لكان يجوز أن يقال يا خالق الكفر والمعاصي إذ هو يكون صدقًا، والصدق لا يمنع عنه دل أنه لم يخلق ذلك. قيل لهم: هذا سؤال تلقيتموه من إخوانكم الثنوية حيث يزعمون أنه تعالى لو كان خالقًا للأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة لكان يجوز أن يقال يا خالق القردة والخنازير والخنافس والجعلان والأقذار والأنتان، وحيث لم يجز دل أنه لم يخلقها، فإن كان ما سألتم لازمًا فهذا لازم، وإن كان ما سألوا باطلا فسؤالكم باطل.
If it is said: If Allah Himself had undertaken the creation of disbelief and acts of disobedience, then it would be permissible to say, 'O Creator of disbelief and acts of disobedience,' since it would be true, and truth is not prevented from being said. This indicates that He did not create that. It is said to them: This question you have taken from your brethren the Dualists, who claim that if the Exalted were the Creator of foul and filthy bodies, it would be permissible to say, 'O Creator of monkeys, pigs, beetles, dung beetles, filth, and stench.' Since it is not permissible, this indicates that He did not create them. So if what you asked is necessary, then this is necessary, and if what they asked is invalid, then your question is invalid.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objectors claim that if God created kufr, one could legitimately address Him as 'Creator of kufr,' but since this is not said, it shows He didn't create it. The author responds by pointing out that this argument is borrowed from the Dualists (Manichaeans), who argued similarly about foul bodies. If the objectors' reasoning holds, then the Dualists' reasoning would also hold, leading to absurd conclusions like denying God's creation of foul bodies. But since the Dualists' argument is invalid, so is the objectors'. This is a refutation by parallel argument.

Key Terms
الثنوية (Dualists)
خالق الكفر (Creator of disbelief)
الأجسام المستخبثة (foul bodies)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the objection that one cannot call God 'Creator of disbelief' is a novel, valid argument against Ash'ari theology.
Correction: The objection is not original but borrowed from the Dualists, a heretical group. Its logic, if applied consistently, would also deny God's creation of foul bodies, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Nasafi refute the objection that one cannot call God 'Creator of kufr'?
A) By quoting scripture
B) By showing that the same logic applied to foul bodies is used by Dualists and is invalid
C) By admitting that God does not create kufr
D) By saying that all creation is good

Key Takeaway
An argument that would equally apply to God's creation of foul bodies cannot be used to deny His creation of disbelief, especially when it originates from heretical sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Najjariya Concept of Aspects of Actions
Najjariya claim about aspects of actions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Najjariya concept of 'aspects of actions' (jihāt al-fiʿl) and how they apply it to examples like disbelief, lying, and blackness.

Core Idea
Actions can be both good and evil, depending on which aspect you consider.

The Najjariya sect proposed a nuanced view on moral judgments: actions are not inherently good or evil but have multiple aspects (jihāt). In this passage, the author presents their claim and illustrates it with examples of disbelief, lying, and blackness, showing how the same thing can be evaluated differently based on the aspect considered.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim about aspects
Read the opening claim: actions have aspects, and the same action can be ugly from one aspect and not from another. Note that from God's aspect it's not ugly, from the servant's aspect it is ugly.
This is the core idea of the Najjariya position.
2
Example of disbelief (kufr)
Analyze how disbelief is examined under different aspects: as a thing, an accident, a belief, and a proof. The text argues it is not ugly from these aspects because that would make faith ugly.
3
Example of lying (kadhib)
Consider how disbelief is a lie, yet it truthfully indicates the disbeliever's foolishness. So from the aspect of truthfulness it is not ugly; only from the aspect of being a lie is it ugly.
4
Example of blackness (sawād)
Notice that blackness is a thing, accident, and color, but it is not blackness because of being any of these generic categories. This shows that the specific attribute (blackness) is not reducible to its generic aspects.
5
Distinguish from physical aspects
The author clarifies that by 'aspects' they do not mean physical directions (above, below, right) but rather conceptual aspects of consideration.
This prevents confusion with the term in other contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم النجارية على هذا يزعمون أن الفعل له جهات، فمن حيث إنه فعل الله تعالى ليس بقبيح، ومن حيث إنه فعل العبد قبيح، ويجوز أن يكون للفعل جهات تقبح وتكون معصية ببعض الجهات ولا يكون كذلك ببعض الجهات، ألا ترى أن الكفر شيء وهو عرض وهو اعتقاد، وهو حجة الله تعالى على الكافر بالتعذيب وليس بقبيح، فمن حيث إنه شيء إذ لو كان قبيحًا من حيث إنه شيء لكان كل شيء قبيحًا فكان الإيمان قبيحًا سببًا للعقاب، وكذا كل طاعة، وكذا ليس بقبيح من حيث إنه عرض، ولا من حيث إنه اعتقاد لما يلزم أن يكون الإيمان قبيحا منهيًا عنه، وكذا الكفر كذب وهو دليل سفه الكافر، ودلالته على سفهه صدق فكان الكذب صدقا في الدلالة على سفه الكاذب وليس بقبيح ولا سفه لاستحقاق العقاب من حيث إنه صدق بل من حيث إنه كذب. وكذا السواد شيء وعرض ولون، وليس بسواد لأنه شيء ولا لأنه عرض ولا لأنه لون لوجودنا أشياء و أعراضا وألوانا ليست بسواد، ولا يعنون بهذه الجهات كما هو جهات الأجسام من حيث فوق وتحت وعن يمين
Then the Najjariya, upon this, claim that the action has aspects. So in terms of being the act of God Almighty, it is not ugly, and in terms of being the act of the servant, it is ugly. And it is possible that an action has aspects by which it is ugly and is disobedience in some aspects, and is not so in other aspects. Do you not see that disbelief is a thing, and it is an accident, and it is a belief, and it is a proof of God Almighty against the disbeliever for punishment, and it is not ugly? So in terms of being a thing: if it were ugly in terms of being a thing, then everything would be ugly, so faith would be ugly and a cause for punishment, and likewise every act of obedience. And likewise, it is not ugly in terms of being an accident, nor in terms of being a belief, because it would necessitate that faith be ugly and prohibited. And similarly, disbelief is a lie, and it is an indication of the disbeliever's foolishness, and its indication of his foolishness is truthful, so the lie becomes truthful in indicating the foolishness of the liar, and it is not ugly nor foolish in terms of deserving punishment in terms of being truthful, but rather in terms of being a lie. And similarly, blackness is a thing, an accident, and a color, and it is not blackness because it is a thing, nor because it is an accident, nor because it is a color, because we find things, accidents, and colors that are not blackness. And they do not mean by these aspects the aspects of bodies such as above, below, and to the right.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage explains the Najjariya position on jihāt al-fi'l. The author reports that the Najjariya claim that actions have aspects (jihāt). From the aspect of being God's act, it is not ugly; from the aspect of being the servant's act, it is ugly. An action can have aspects that make it ugly and disobedient in some respects and not in others. The author illustrates with several examples. First, disbelief (kufr) is a thing, an accident, and a belief, and it is a proof (hujja) from God against the disbeliever for punishment, so it is not ugly. If it were ugly from the aspect of being a thing, then everything would be ugly, including faith. Similarly, it is not ugly from the aspect of being an accident or a belief, because that would make faith ugly and prohibited. Second, disbelief is a lie (kadhib) and is evidence of the disbeliever's foolishness; yet its evidence of foolishness is truthful (sidq), so the lie becomes truthful in indicating the liar's foolishness, so it is not ugly or foolish from that aspect; the ugliness is from the aspect of being a lie. Third, blackness (sawād) is a thing, an accident, and a color, but it is not blackness because it is a thing, etc., because we find things that are not blackness. The author notes that they do not mean by these aspects the physical directions like above, below, and right, but rather conceptual aspects.

Key Terms
النجارية
جهات الفعل
القبيح
الكفر
الكذب
السواد
العرض
الاعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The same action is both good and evil from the same perspective.
Correction: The Najjariya argue that aspects are distinct; an action can be good from one aspect and evil from another, not simultaneously from the same aspect.

Checkpoint
According to the Najjariya as presented, why is disbelief not considered ugly from the aspect of being a thing?
A) Because everything that exists is good.
B) Because if it were ugly, then every thing would be ugly, including faith.
C) Because disbelief is a proof against the disbeliever.
D) Because it is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The Najjariya hold that actions have multiple conceptual aspects, and an action's moral value (good/evil) depends on which aspect is considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent action you performed that you are ashamed of. Now, consider it from a different aspect—perhaps as a necessary mistake or as a step toward growth. Does the Najjariya teaching about aspects allow you to see it differently? What aspect should you focus on to turn this action into a means of drawing closer to Allah? Commit to one concrete step to change your perspective.
?
The example of disbelief being both a lie and a truthful indication of foolishness shows that the same reality can be described truthfully and falsely from different aspects. When you judge others, which aspect do you usually focus on—their sin or the underlying truth about their state? How can you train yourself to see both aspects without excusing sin? Write down one person you have judged harshly and what aspect you could now consider.
?
If every action has multiple aspects, how does this affect your personal accountability before Allah? Are you careful to consider your actions from the aspect of how they appear to Allah (as obedience or disobedience) rather than just from the aspect of social approval? What is one action you do regularly that you have not examined from the aspect of divine command? Pray for guidance and resolve to examine it today.

Jihāt: Conceptual Aspects, Not Spatial Directions
Clarifying that jihāt are conceptual, not spatial, and refuting Mu'tazila's misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term jihāt in theological discussions refers to conceptual aspects of actions (expressed through words and meanings) rather than physical directions, and how the Mu'tazila's misunderstanding of this led to their refutation.

Core Idea
Jihāt are about expression and meaning, not spatial dimensions.

The term jihāt can be confusing. When theologians like the Najjariyya speak of 'aspects' of actions, they do not mean up, down, left, or right. This lesson clarifies that distinction and then explains why the Mu'tazila's criticism of jihāt al-fi'l is based on a misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two main parts: definition of jihāt and refutation of Mu'tazila.
The passage has a clear contrast between spatial and conceptual jihāt.
2
Identify the correct meaning of jihāt
Find where the author says jihāt are not like the aspects of bodies (above, below, etc.) but rather in terms of expression (ʿibāra) and meaning (maʿnā).
This is the core positive definition.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's error
The Mu'tazila thought jihāt are like spatial dimensions and argued an accident cannot have multiple distinct aspects. The author names al-Iskāfī and Jaʿfar b. Ḥarb as critics.
Their mistake is misunderstanding the technical term.
4
Reflect on the author's dismissal
The author says their criticism stems from ignorance of reality and of the opponents' doctrines. Note that Maturidis themselves do not need this theory.
This shows the author's stance is not fully committed to jihāt al-fiʿl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعنون بهذه الجهات كما هو جهات الأجسام من حيث فوق وتحت وعن يمين وعن شمال وخلف وقدام ، بل يعنون ما بينا من وجود الجهات من حيث العبارة والمعنى دون الجهات التي يصح منها محاسة ما يلاقيها وبمعرفة تفسير الجهات تظهر جهالة المعتزلة بإنكارهم ذلك، وزعمهم أن الجهات كون للأجسام وكل جهة تغاير صاحبتها، والعرض شيء واحد فلا وجه لجعله أشياء متغايرة مع أنه في نفسه شيء واحد كما شنع الإسكافي وجعفر بن حرب وغيرهما حيث صنفوا تصانيف لإبطال جهات الفعل وكل ذلك لإنكارهم الحقيقة وجهلهم بحقائق مذاهب الخصوم، هذا تقرير مذهبهم، وإن كنا لا نحتاج إلى القول بجهات الفعل على ما قررنا.
And they do not mean by these 'aspects' (jihāt) what is meant by the aspects of bodies - like above, below, right, left, behind, and front. Rather, they mean what we have explained: the existence of aspects in terms of expression (ʿibāra) and meaning (maʿnā), not the aspects that allow physical sensation of what contacts them. And by knowing the interpretation (tafsīr) of aspects, the ignorance of the Muʿtazila becomes apparent in their denial of them, and their claim that aspects are a being (kawn) for bodies and that every aspect differs from its counterpart, and that an accident (ʿaraḍ) is a single thing, so there is no basis for making it multiple distinct things while it is in itself a single thing - as al-Iskāfī and Jaʿfar b. Ḥarb and others have harangued, composing works to invalidate the aspects of action. All that is due to their denial of reality (ḥaqīqa) and their ignorance of the realities of the doctrines of opponents. This is the exposition of their doctrine, though we have no need to speak of aspects of action as we have established.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage clarifies the intended meaning of 'aspects' (jihāt) in the context of actions. The author explains that the Najjariyya (and by extension those who use jihāt) do not mean spatial directions like up, down, left, right, etc. Rather, they mean aspects in terms of expression and meaning - i.e., relational or conceptual dimensions, not physical ones. The author then points out that the Muʿtazila's denial of jihāt stems from their ignorance of this technical meaning. The Muʿtazila mistakenly think jihāt are like the spatial aspects of bodies, and they argue that an accident (ʿaraḍ) cannot have multiple distinct aspects when it is one thing. The author cites specific Muʿtazilī figures - al-Iskāfī and Jaʿfar b. Ḥarb - who wrote refutations of jihāt. The author dismisses their criticism as stemming from ignorance of the true nature of the concept and of the opponents' doctrines. He then concludes that this is the exposition of their (the Najjariyya's) doctrine, while reiterating that the Maturidis themselves do not need to adopt the theory of jihāt, as established earlier.

عبارة
معنى
محاسة

جعفر بن حرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila thought jihāt refer to spatial directions like up and down.
Correction: Jihāt refer to conceptual aspects of actions, expressed through words and meanings, not physical dimensions.

Checkpoint
What mistake does the author attribute to the Mu'tazila regarding jihāt al-fiʿl?
A) They thought jihāt refer to spatial directions
B) They denied that God creates actions
C) They claimed jihāt are physical parts of actions
D) They agreed with the Najjariyya but misunderstood the terminology

Key Takeaway
Jihāt in theology are conceptual aspects of actions, not spatial directions; the Mu'tazila's refutation is based on a misunderstanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's actions in the world, do you unintentionally picture them as if they happen in a certain direction or location? How might this subtle anthropomorphism distort your tawhīd? Will you consciously remind yourself that God's attributes are beyond spatial constraints starting today?
?
The Mu'tazila refused to accept the concept of jihāt because it clashed with their rigid framework. Have you ever dismissed a valid Islamic concept because it didn't fit your preconceived categories? Would you be willing to re-examine that dismissal with humility and seek deeper understanding?
?
This passage shows that theological disputes often arise from misunderstandings of terms. In your own life, have you ever disagreed with someone simply because you used the same word with different meanings? Can you think of a relationship in need of clarification today?

The Mu'tazila's Denial of Aspects: Rhetoric vs. Reality
Mu'tazila's frantic denial of aspects; truth forces acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila resorted to emotional rhetoric and intimidation when faced with the concept of aspects of action, and how the truth compels rational acceptance.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's loud denial reveals their doctrine's weakness, not its strength.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi describes the Mu'tazila's agitated response to the concept of 'aspects of action' (جهات الفعل). While they present themselves as rational defenders of tawheed, their behavior—calling proponents foolish, showing vexation, and engaging in sophistry—betrays a deeper insecurity. The author then contrasts this with the calm confidence of one who knows the truth, showing that the concept is inescapable even for the Mu'tazila themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the strong language used to describe the Mu'tazila's reaction.
Pay attention to words like 'تضايق الكلام' and 'وهاء المذهب'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's tactics
List the specific behaviors attributed to the Mu'tazila: strong denial, calling proponents foolish, showing vexation, engaging in defamation and sophistry, obscuring the truth, and concealing the weakness of their doctrine.
These are not arguments but emotional responses.
3
Understand the author's contrast
The author contrasts the Mu'tazila's reaction with the attitude of one who truly understands the aspects. Such a person does not shy away and can compel rational people to affirm the concept.
The author implies that the truth is self-evident to rational minds.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misconception that the Mu'tazila had a strong rational case. The passage shows that their behavior indicates weakness, not strength.
The phrase 'سترا لوهاء مذهبهم' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة لمعرفتهم بتضايق الكلام عليهم من هذا الوجه ينكرون القول بجهات الفعل جدًا، وينسبون قائلة إلى الحمق، ويظهرون الضجر عند سماعه، ويشتغلون بالتشنيع والمشاغبة تنصيراً للضعفاء، وتمويها عليهم ، وسترا لوهاء مذهبهم. وقف على ما بينا من الوجوه عرف الحقيقة ولم يجبن عن إثبات ذلك عند تهويل المعتزلة، ويبين لهم أنه يقول ما يقولون هم، ويضطر جميع العقلاء إلى القول به.
And the Mu'tazila, due to their knowledge of the tightness of speech upon them from this angle, strongly deny the statement of aspects of action, and attribute its speaker to foolishness, and show vexation upon hearing it, and occupy themselves with defamation and sophistry to assist the weak ones, and to obscure (the truth) upon them, and to conceal the weakness of their doctrine. Whoever stops at the aspects we have explained knows the reality and does not shy away from affirming that despite the intimidation of the Mu'tazila, and he makes clear to them that he says what they themselves say, and he compels all rational people to affirm it.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage describes the behavior of the Mu'tazila regarding the concept of 'aspects of action' (جهات الفعل). The author notes that the Mu'tazila, aware that this concept creates difficulty for their own position, strongly deny it. They go so far as to call its proponents foolish, show irritation when it is mentioned, and resort to rhetorical tricks like defamation and sophistry (التشنيع والمشاغبة) to deceive the weak-minded and conceal the fragility (وهاء) of their own doctrine. The author then contrasts this with the correct approach: whoever understands the points made earlier (the arguments about aspects) will recognize the truth and will not be intimidated by the Mu'tazila's bluster. Such a person can demonstrate to them that the concept of aspects is something they themselves implicitly affirm, and that all rational people are forced to accept it. The implication is that the Mu'tazila's denial is not based on reason but on a desire to avoid the consequences of their own principles.

The Definition of Shirk and Its Two Categories
Definition and classification of shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic definition of shirk (partnership) and the two distinct types recognized by Muslims.

Core Idea
Shirk is only meaningful if each partner has exclusive power that the other lacks.

The author begins by clarifying a potential misunderstanding: that God creating human actions would make humans partners with God. To answer this, he first defines what true partnership means and then categorizes the two forms of shirk recognized in Islam: shirk in creation and shirk in worship. This foundational understanding is crucial for the subsequent arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of partnership
Carefully read the first few lines where the author states that the reality of partnership is that each partner has something the other does not have in what they share.
This definition is key to the entire argument.
2
Identify the two types of shirk
Find the two categories: shirk in creation (the Magians) and shirk in worship (idol worshippers). Note the differences.
The Magians assign creation of good and evil to two different sources.
3
Observe the description of idol worshippers
Notice that even idol worshippers acknowledge God as the Creator of the heavens and the earth, yet they worship idols alongside Him.
This shows their shirk is not in creation but in worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بكون أفعال الخلق مخلوقة لله تعالى لا يُلزم أن يكون العباد شركاء الله تعالى في الفعل؛ وذلك لأن حقيقة الشركة أن يكون لكل واحد من الشريكين ما ليس لصاحبه فيما يشتركان فيه، فإن الشرك عند أهل الإسلام نوعان: أحدهما: مشرك يثبت الله تعالى شريكا في تخليق العالم وهم المجوس، فيكون ما هو مخلوق الله تعالى من الخيرات غير ما هو مخلوق شريكه وما هو مخلوق شريكه من الشرور والقبائح غير ما هو مخلوق الله تعالى. والآخر من يثبت الله تعالى شريكا في استحقاق العبادة دون التخليق وهم عبدة الأصنام، فإنك إن سألتهم من خلق السماوات والأرض؟ ليقولن الله غير أنهم يعبدون الأصنام كما يعبدون الصانع، ويجلعونها مستحقة للعبادة كما استحقها الصانع ثم ما يوجد من عبادتهم للأصنام لا يكون عبادة الله تعالى وما يوجد من عبادتهم الله تعالى لا يكون عبادة للأصنام، وفي العرف عند أرباب اللسان الشركاء في القرية والمحلة قوم يكون كل واحد منهم اختص بملك شيء من القرية لا يملكه غيره من الشركاء، ولم يعقل كون شيء لذات من وجه ولذات من وجه شركة بينهما بإجماع العقلاء، فإن ما هو ملك العبد هو بعينه ملك لله تعالى ملك تخليق لم يزل شيء من المخلوقات عن ملكه بتملك العباد ذلك، ولم يقل أحد أن العبد شريك الله تعالى فمن ما يملكه من العقار أو الحيوان أو الثياب والفرش والأواني وغير ذلك، وإن كان ذلك ملك العبد وعين ما هو ملك العبد ملك الله تعالى، وعين ما ملك الله تعالى ملك العبد ، ثم العالم أعراض وأعيان وما أوجده الله تعالى غير ما هو أوجده غيره، وما أوجده غيره غير ما أوجده الله تعالى فيكون العالم له ولأغياره، وهذا هو حقيقة الشركة ، وكذا من كل فعل كأن العبد شريك الله، فإنا إذا رأينا أن زيدًا مع عمر إذا اشتركا في بناء الدار وما يقدر عليه هذا من أجزاء الدار لا يقدر عليه ذاك، وما يقدر عليه ذاك لا يقدر عليه هذا فجعل كل واحد منهما ما في قدرته حتى تم بناء الدار بهما لم يمتنع أحد من أرباب اللسان والعقلاء من القول زيدًا وعمر اشتركا في بناء الدار ثم إن الفعل من العبد لن يحصل إلا بوجود قدرة يخترعها الله تعالى لا قدرة للعبد عليها
Then, the fact that the actions of creatures are created by God Almighty does not necessitate that servants are partners with God in the action. That is because the reality of partnership is that each of the two partners has something that the other does not have in what they share. Indeed, according to the people of Islam, shirk is of two types: One: one who affirms a partner to God in the creation of the world, and they are the Magians. So what is created by God from good things is other than what is created by His partner, and what is created by His partner from evils and abominations is other than what is created by God. The other: one who affirms a partner to God in deserving worship without creation, and they are idol worshippers. For if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will say God, but they worship idols as they worship the Maker, and they make them deserving of worship as the Maker deserves it. Then what is found of their worship of idols is not worship of God, and what is found of their worship of God is not worship of idols. And in custom among speakers of the language, partners in a village or district are people each of whom has exclusive ownership of something of the village that no other partner owns. And it is not intellectually understood that a thing belonging to one entity in one respect and to another entity in another respect constitutes partnership between them, by consensus of the rational people. For what is the possession of the servant is precisely the possession of God as possession of creation; nothing of the created things has left His possession by the servant's taking possession of it. And no one has said that the servant is a partner of God in what he owns of real estate, animals, clothes, bedding, vessels, and other than that, even though that is the servant's possession and precisely what is the servant's possession is God's possession, and precisely what God possesses is the servant's possession. Then the world consists of accidents and substances, and what God brought into existence is other than what other than Him brought into existence, and what other than Him brought into existence is other than what God brought into existence, so the world would belong to Him and to others, and that is the reality of partnership. And likewise, from every action it would be as if the servant is a partner of God. For we see that when Zayd and Umar share in building a house, and what this one can do of the parts of the house, that one cannot do, and what that one can do, this one cannot do, so each of them puts forth what is in his power until the building of the house is completed by both, no one among speakers of the language and rational people refrains from saying 'Zayd and Umar shared in building the house.' Then indeed, the action of the servant is not obtained except by the existence of a power that God invents; the servant has no power over it.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. The author states that the fact that God creates human actions does not imply that humans are partners with God in that action. He defines partnership as each partner having exclusive ownership in something they share. Then he classifies shirk into two types: shirk in creation (like the Magians) and shirk in worship (like idol worshippers). He explains that even idol worshippers acknowledge God as creator. He then uses linguistic and rational arguments: in common language, partners in property each have exclusive rights to parts; God's ownership of everything is ownership of creation, and humans merely possess things in a different sense. He argues that if God created all, then if humans were partners, they would have to have exclusive creative power over some things, which is not the case. He gives an analogy of two people sharing in building a house: each contributes his own power, but in the case of God and humans, humans cannot create any part independently; their action only occurs through a power that God invents. Therefore, true partnership is impossible. The passage ends mid-sentence, apparently continuing to emphasize that humans have no power over the action itself.

Key Terms
شركة
شرك
تخليق
عبادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates human actions, then humans are partners with God in the action.
Correction: The author explains that partnership requires each partner to have exclusive power over something, which is not the case with God and humans.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the reality of partnership (shirk)?
A) Both partners contribute equally
B) Each partner has something the other does not have in what they share
C) Partnership requires mutual consent
D) Partnership only exists in worship

Key Takeaway
Shirk requires each partner to have exclusive power, which does not apply to God and humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that idol worshippers acknowledged God as Creator yet still worshipped idols. Examine your own life: are there areas where you acknowledge God's role in theory but practically give your ultimate trust, fear, or devotion to created things—like money, status, relationships, or your own abilities? What is one concrete step you will take today to redirect exclusive worship back to Allah alone?
?
True partnership requires exclusive control. In your heart, have you granted anything or anyone 'exclusive control' over your happiness, security, or identity? What would it mean for you to recognize that only Allah has exclusive power over these, and how would that change your daily reactions?
?
The author defines shirk as requiring each partner to have something the other lacks. When you commit a sin, do you sometimes act as if that sin has power over you that Allah does not have—like an addiction or a habit you think you cannot overcome? How can this lesson's definition of partnership reshape your understanding of reliance on Allah in overcoming sin?
