Why Divine Ownership Precludes Partnership
Linguistic and rational argument against partnership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's ownership of creation is fundamentally different from human possession, making partnership impossible.

Core Idea
God's ownership is creative; human possession is derivative and does not exclude God's ownership.

Having defined shirk, the author now offers a deeper argument based on the nature of ownership. He points to linguistic custom and rational consensus to show that true partnership involves each partner having exclusive rights over some part. He then applies this to the relationship between God and humans, arguing that God's ownership of creation is absolute and humans merely possess things in a secondary sense, without any exclusive domain. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Examine the linguistic custom example
Read how partners in a village each have exclusive ownership of something the other does not own. Understand that this exclusivity is essential.
This sets up the rational standard.
2
Apply the standard to God and humans
Notice the author's claim: God's possession of everything is possession of creation; humans possess things but God still owns them as Creator.
There is no exclusive human domain.
3
Consider the consequence
If humans were true partners, they would have exclusive creative power over something, which they do not.
The author argues this would lead to multiple creators, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بكون أفعال الخلق مخلوقة لله تعالى لا يُلزم أن يكون العباد شركاء الله تعالى في الفعل؛ وذلك لأن حقيقة الشركة أن يكون لكل واحد من الشريكين ما ليس لصاحبه فيما يشتركان فيه، فإن الشرك عند أهل الإسلام نوعان: أحدهما: مشرك يثبت الله تعالى شريكا في تخليق العالم وهم المجوس، فيكون ما هو مخلوق الله تعالى من الخيرات غير ما هو مخلوق شريكه وما هو مخلوق شريكه من الشرور والقبائح غير ما هو مخلوق الله تعالى. والآخر من يثبت الله تعالى شريكا في استحقاق العبادة دون التخليق وهم عبدة الأصنام، فإنك إن سألتهم من خلق السماوات والأرض؟ ليقولن الله غير أنهم يعبدون الأصنام كما يعبدون الصانع، ويجلعونها مستحقة للعبادة كما استحقها الصانع ثم ما يوجد من عبادتهم للأصنام لا يكون عبادة الله تعالى وما يوجد من عبادتهم الله تعالى لا يكون عبادة للأصنام، وفي العرف عند أرباب اللسان الشركاء في القرية والمحلة قوم يكون كل واحد منهم اختص بملك شيء من القرية لا يملكه غيره من الشركاء، ولم يعقل كون شيء لذات من وجه ولذات من وجه شركة بينهما بإجماع العقلاء، فإن ما هو ملك العبد هو بعينه ملك لله تعالى ملك تخليق لم يزل شيء من المخلوقات عن ملكه بتملك العباد ذلك، ولم يقل أحد أن العبد شريك الله تعالى فمن ما يملكه من العقار أو الحيوان أو الثياب والفرش والأواني وغير ذلك، وإن كان ذلك ملك العبد وعين ما هو ملك العبد ملك الله تعالى، وعين ما ملك الله تعالى ملك العبد ، ثم العالم أعراض وأعيان وما أوجده الله تعالى غير ما هو أوجده غيره، وما أوجده غيره غير ما أوجده الله تعالى فيكون العالم له ولأغياره، وهذا هو حقيقة الشركة ، وكذا من كل فعل كأن العبد شريك الله، فإنا إذا رأينا أن زيدًا مع عمر إذا اشتركا في بناء الدار وما يقدر عليه هذا من أجزاء الدار لا يقدر عليه ذاك، وما يقدر عليه ذاك لا يقدر عليه هذا فجعل كل واحد منهما ما في قدرته حتى تم بناء الدار بهما لم يمتنع أحد من أرباب اللسان والعقلاء من القول زيدًا وعمر اشتركا في بناء الدار ثم إن الفعل من العبد لن يحصل إلا بوجود قدرة يخترعها الله تعالى لا قدرة للعبد عليها
Then, the fact that the actions of creatures are created by God Almighty does not necessitate that servants are partners with God in the action. That is because the reality of partnership is that each of the two partners has something that the other does not have in what they share. Indeed, according to the people of Islam, shirk is of two types: One: one who affirms a partner to God in the creation of the world, and they are the Magians. So what is created by God from good things is other than what is created by His partner, and what is created by His partner from evils and abominations is other than what is created by God. The other: one who affirms a partner to God in deserving worship without creation, and they are idol worshippers. For if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will say God, but they worship idols as they worship the Maker, and they make them deserving of worship as the Maker deserves it. Then what is found of their worship of idols is not worship of God, and what is found of their worship of God is not worship of idols. And in custom among speakers of the language, partners in a village or district are people each of whom has exclusive ownership of something of the village that no other partner owns. And it is not intellectually understood that a thing belonging to one entity in one respect and to another entity in another respect constitutes partnership between them, by consensus of the rational people. For what is the possession of the servant is precisely the possession of God as possession of creation; nothing of the created things has left His possession by the servant's taking possession of it. And no one has said that the servant is a partner of God in what he owns of real estate, animals, clothes, bedding, vessels, and other than that, even though that is the servant's possession and precisely what is the servant's possession is God's possession, and precisely what God possesses is the servant's possession. Then the world consists of accidents and substances, and what God brought into existence is other than what other than Him brought into existence, and what other than Him brought into existence is other than what God brought into existence, so the world would belong to Him and to others, and that is the reality of partnership. And likewise, from every action it would be as if the servant is a partner of God. For we see that when Zayd and Umar share in building a house, and what this one can do of the parts of the house, that one cannot do, and what that one can do, this one cannot do, so each of them puts forth what is in his power until the building of the house is completed by both, no one among speakers of the language and rational people refrains from saying 'Zayd and Umar shared in building the house.' Then indeed, the action of the servant is not obtained except by the existence of a power that God invents; the servant has no power over it.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. The author states that the fact that God creates human actions does not imply that humans are partners with God in that action. He defines partnership as each partner having exclusive ownership in something they share. Then he classifies shirk into two types: shirk in creation (like the Magians) and shirk in worship (like idol worshippers). He explains that even idol worshippers acknowledge God as creator. He then uses linguistic and rational arguments: in common language, partners in property each have exclusive rights to parts; God's ownership of everything is ownership of creation, and humans merely possess things in a different sense. He argues that if God created all, then if humans were partners, they would have to have exclusive creative power over some things, which is not the case. He gives an analogy of two people sharing in building a house: each contributes his own power, but in the case of God and humans, humans cannot create any part independently; their action only occurs through a power that God invents. Therefore, true partnership is impossible. The passage ends mid-sentence, apparently continuing to emphasize that humans have no power over the action itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if humans own property, they are partners with God in ownership.
Correction: The author explains that God's ownership is creative and all-encompassing; human possession is a trust and does not give exclusive rights.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the linguistic custom of partners in a village?
A) They share all property equally
B) Each has exclusive ownership of something the others do not own
C) They have no individual ownership
D) They are considered partners in everything

Key Takeaway
God's ownership is absolute and creative, leaving no room for humans to be partners with exclusive power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your possessions—your home, your car, your phone—do you feel a sense of ownership that makes you forget they are ultimately God's? What would change in your heart if you truly lived knowing your possession is only a loan that never leaves His ownership?
?
The passage says no one claims that a servant is God's partner in property. But in your daily life, do you ever act as if you have independent control, making decisions without consulting God? Where do you need to acknowledge that He is the true Owner and you are merely a trustee?
?
If you were stripped of everything you own tomorrow, would your faith in God's ownership remain unshaken? Or does your sense of security depend on what you possess? Let this passage challenge you to find your identity in being owned by God, not in owning things.

The Example of Shared Building and Human Inability
Analogical argument refuting partnership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy of two people sharing in building a house and how it contrasts with the relationship between God and humans, showing that humans lack independent creative power.

Core Idea
True partnership requires each partner to contribute from their own power; humans have no power to create any part of their actions.

To drive his point home, the author provides a concrete example from everyday life: two men share in building a house. Each contributes his own ability to different parts. In contrast, when it comes to human actions, the servant has no power to create any part of the action; the entire action depends on a power that God invents. This lesson examines the analogy and its implications, concluding that genuine partnership is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the building analogy
Consider the example of Zayd and Umar sharing in building a house. Note that each can do something the other cannot, and they combine their abilities.
This is a clear case of partnership.
2
Contrast with the divine-human case
Notice that in the case of God and humans, the human cannot create any part of the action independently. The action only occurs through a power God invents.
No shared creative power.
3
Conclude the argument
Understand that if partnership required each to have exclusive power, and humans have none, then partnership is impossible.
The author ends mid-sentence but the implication is clear.

?

?

How does that affect your repentance? Are you willing to take full responsibility while simultaneously acknowledging your utter dependence?

Nasafi's Accusation: Mu'tazila as Greater Polytheists than the Majus
Nasafi compares the Mu'tazila unfavorably to Zoroastrians in two ways.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the Mu'tazila commit a worse form of shirk than the Zoroastrians by attributing good actions to creatures rather than to Allah.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila commit a greater shirk than the Majus by making all living beings partners in goodness.

This passage presents the climax of Nasafi's polemic against the Mu'tazila. After establishing the true meaning of partnership (shirk), he boldly reverses the accusation, claiming that the Mu'tazila themselves are the real polytheists—and even worse than the Zoroastrians. He then specifies two ways in which their shirk exceeds that of the Majus, focusing on the number of partners and the quality of deeds attributed to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the strong language Nasafi uses.
Notice how Nasafi begins with an accusation and then supports it with a comparison.
2
Identify the two comparisons
The passage lists two ways the Mu'tazila are worse than the Majus (Zoroastrians). Find these two points in the text.
The text says 'min wajhayn' (from two aspects).
3
Understand the logic of shirk
Nasafi argues that attributing good deeds to creatures is a graver sin than attributing evil to a partner god. Reflect on why this is so.
This is because goodness belongs to Allah alone.
4
Consider the rhetorical purpose
Ask yourself: why does Nasafi make this comparison? How does it strengthen his refutation of the Mu'tazila?
This is a classic polemical move: reversing the accusation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة يثبتون شركاء الله بقولهم وبالوقوف على حقيقة الشركة علم أن المعتزلة هم الذين أثبتوا الله تعالى في العالم شركاء ومن كل فعل اختياري شريكا، ونحن بحمد الله تعالى برآء عن ذلك، فكانت نسبة المعتزلة إيانا إلى القول مما يوجب الشركة ووصف أنفسهم بالبراءة من ذلك وقاحة عظيمة أو جهلاً بحقيقة الشركة المعقولة، وصاروا بإثبات الشركاء الله تعالى في العالم وفي كل فعل مساعدين للمجوس بل كانوا شرا من المجوس من وجهين أحدهما أن المجوس ما أثبتوا الله إلا شريكاً واحداً، وهؤلاء جعلوا كل ما دب ودرج من ذوي الأرواح والمهج شركاء الله تعالى. والثاني: أن المجوس أرادوا بإثبات الشريك تنزيه الله تعالى عما هو عندهم سفه، وما أضافوا إلى الشريك إلا الشرور والقبائح، والمعتزلة أضافوا كل ما هو حسن على الحقيقة إلى غير الله تعالى، وجعلوا ما فعله غيره أحسن مما فعله الله تعالى على ما قررنا والله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة، وفي المسألة دلائل كثيرة ذكرها من تقدم من ائمتنا الماضين - قدس الله أرواحهم – ومن
And the Mu'tazila establish partners for Allah by their statement. And by understanding the reality of partnership, we know that the Mu'tazila are the ones who have established partners for Allah in the world and a partner from every voluntary action. And we, praise be to Allah, are free from that. So, the Mu'tazila's attribution to us of a saying that necessitates partnership and their description of themselves as being free from that is either great impudence or ignorance of the intellectually sound reality of partnership. And they, by establishing partners for Allah in the world and in every action, become helpers of the Majus. Rather, they are worse than the Majus in two respects: first, the Majus only established one partner for Allah, while these have made every creeping thing with soul and blood a partner of Allah. Second, the Majus intended by establishing a partner to exalt Allah above what they consider foolishness, and they only attributed evil and ugly things to the partner, while the Mu'tazila attributed everything that is truly good to other than Allah, and they made what His other has done more excellent than what Allah has done, as we have established. And Allah is the protector and savior from every error and innovation. And on this issue there are many proofs mentioned by our past imams - may Allah sanctify their souls - and from...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the argument. After defining true partnership, Nasafi accuses the Mu'tazila of being the actual polytheists because they attribute the creation of actions to humans, making them partners with God in voluntary acts. He rejects their accusation against the Maturidis as either insolence or ignorance. Then he compares them unfavorably to the Majus (Zoroastrians), giving two reasons: the Majus only have one partner (Ahriman) and only attribute evil to him, while the Mu'tazila attribute good acts to humans, making them partners and even surpassing God's acts in goodness. This is a strong polemical conclusion.

Key Terms
معتزلة
مجوس
شريك
حسن
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Maturidis are the ones accused of shirk by the Mu'tazila.
Correction: Nasafi argues the opposite: the Mu'tazila themselves are the true polytheists, worse than the Zoroastrians.

Checkpoint
In what two ways are the Mu'tazila worse than the Majus according to Nasafi?
A) The Majus have no partner; the Mu'tazila have one
B) The Majus only one partner; the Mu'tazila make all living beings partners; the Majus only attribute evil to the partner; the Mu'tazila attribute good to partners
C) The Majus worship fire; the Mu'tazila worship idols
D) The Majus deny creation; the Mu'tazila affirm it

Key Takeaway
Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila commit a greater shirk than the Zoroastrians by multiplying partners and attributing genuine good to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something praiseworthy, do you feel a sense of pride as if you are the creator of that success? What would it mean for your daily gratitude to explicitly attribute every good act and outcome solely to Allah, as the Maturidi creed demands?
?
Nasafi says the Majus at least preserved the idea that only evil comes from their partner. But you might claim to believe in tawhid while practically attributing evil to others and good to yourself. In what specific situation this week have you committed this subtle shirk, and how will you repent and reorient your heart?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's justice by making humans responsible, yet Nasafi calls them worse polytheists. How might your own efforts to justify yourself or defend your ‘righteousness’ actually be a form of hidden shirk, making you the source of your own goodness rather than Allah?

Mu'tazila Are Worse Than Magians
The author argues that the Mu'tazila affirm partners for Allah and are worse than Magians in two ways.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the Mu'tazila to affirm partners for Allah and be worse than the Magians in two specific ways.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila inadvertently affirm partners for Allah and are worse than the Magians.

This passage presents a sharp critique of the Mu'tazila. The author argues that despite their claims of tawhid, the Mu'tazila actually affirm partners for Allah by their theology. Moreover, they exceed the Magians (Zoroastrians) in deviance in two specific ways. Let's examine the author's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and its translation carefully. Note the author's strong language.
2
Identify the two accusations
Find the two respects in which the Mu'tazila are worse than the Magians. Write them down.
3
Understand the comparison with Magians
Reflect on why the author compares them to Magians and what the difference is.
The Magians are dualists.
4
Consider the broader context
The author mentions many proofs but does not list them. Why might that be?

المعتزلة يثبتون شركاء الله بقولهم وبالوقوف على حقيقة الشركة علم أن المعتزلة هم الذين أثبتوا الله تعالى في العالم شركاء ومن كل فعل اختياري شريكا، ونحن بحمد الله تعالى برآء عن ذلك، فكانت نسبة المعتزلة إيانا إلى القول مما يوجب الشركة ووصف أنفسهم بالبراءة من ذلك وقاحة عظيمة أو جهلاً بحقيقة الشركة المعقولة، وصاروا بإثبات الشركاء الله تعالى في العالم وفي كل فعل مساعدين للمجوس بل كانوا شرا من المجوس من وجهين أحدهما أن المجوس ما أثبتوا الله إلا شريكاً واحداً، وهؤلاء جعلوا كل ما دب ودرج من ذوي الأرواح والمهج شركاء الله تعالى. والثاني: أن المجوس أرادوا بإثبات الشريك تنزيه الله تعالى عما هو عندهم سفه، وما أضافوا إلى الشريك إلا الشرور والقبائح، والمعتزلة أضافوا كل ما هو حسن على الحقيقة إلى غير الله تعالى، وجعلوا ما فعله غيره أحسن مما فعله الله تعالى على ما قررنا والله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة، وفي المسألة دلائل كثيرة ذكرها من تقدم من ائمتنا الماضين قدس الله أرواحهم ومن ساعدنا في هذه المسألة من أرباب المذاهب وغيرهم لا وجه إلى ذكر عشرها فضلاً عن كلها لما يقتضي حصرها وذكرها بما للخصوم عليها من الشبه ودفعها، والكشف عن بطلانها كتابًا مفردًا يربو على هذا الكتاب الذي نحن بصدده، ولو رحمه الله كشفنا عما للإمام أبي منصور الماتريدي من الإشارات اللفظية والعبارات الوجيزة الجارية مجرى التوفيقات في هذا الباب لطال الكلام وملت عن ضبطها الأفهام.
The Mu'tazila affirm partners for Allah by their statement. And by understanding the reality of partnership, it is known that the Mu'tazila are the ones who affirmed for Allah Almighty partners in the world and from every voluntary deed a partner, and we, praise be to Allah, are innocent of that. So the Mu'tazila's attribution of the saying that necessitates partnership to us and their description of themselves as innocent of that is great insolence or ignorance of the intelligible reality of partnership. And they, by affirming partners for Allah Almighty in the world and in every deed, became assistants to the Magians; rather they were worse than the Magians in two respects: one is that the Magians only affirmed for Allah one partner, while these made every creeping and walking thing among the possessors of spirits and souls partners of Allah Almighty. The second: that the Magians intended by affirming a partner to exonerate Allah Almighty from what they consider foolishness, and they only attributed evils and ugly things to the partner, while the Mu'tazila attributed everything that is truly good to other than Allah Almighty, and they made what others did better than what Allah Almighty did, as we have established. And Allah is the protection and safety from every error and innovation. In this issue there are many proofs that those who preceded among our past imams mentioned—may Allah sanctify their spirits—and those who assisted us in this issue from the adherents of schools and others. There is no way to mention even a tenth of them, let alone all of them, because their enumeration and mention along with the doubts of the opponents against them and their refutation and the exposure of their falsehood would require a separate book exceeding this book we are engaged in. And if we had mercy, we would have uncovered what Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi indicated with verbal indications and concise expressions that run in the vein of reconciliations in this chapter, but the discussion would become lengthy and the minds would deviate from grasping them.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage exposes the Mu'tazila as the real proponents of shirk. The author argues that by examining the true meaning of partnership, it is clear that the Mu'tazila have affirmed partners for Allah in the world and in every voluntary deed, while the Maturidites are innocent. In fact, the Mu'tazila are worse than the Magians (Zoroastrians) in two ways: first, the Magians only affirmed one partner for Allah, but the Mu'tazila make every living being a partner; second, the Magians aimed to exonerate Allah by attributing only evil to the partner, but the Mu'tazila attribute all true goodness to other than Allah and consider the acts of others better than Allah's acts. The author then notes that there are many proofs from earlier imams and that it would require a separate book to list them all, mentioning the contributions of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.

Misconception: The Mu'tazila are purely monotheistic and uphold tawhid.
Correction: The author argues that their theology actually affirms partners for Allah, making them worse than the Magians.

Key Takeaway

Refutation of Thumama's Innovation: Acts Need a Creator
Refutation of Thumama's view that acts occur without a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Thumama's view that acts occur without a creator leads to ta'til and undermines the proof for God's existence.

Core Idea
Denying a creator for acts is pure ta'til.

Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite Thumama, who claimed that well-ordered acts can come into existence without a specific agent. The author argues that this 'innovation' destroys the very proof for the Creator and amounts to absolute denial of divine agency (ta'til). Let's examine this critical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify Thumama's bid'ah
Read the Arabic and translation to see what Thumama's view is: acts come into existence without a specifier or existentiator.
2
Understand the consequence: ta'til
Notice that the author immediately links this view to ta'til mahd (pure denial). Ask: why is this the logical outcome?
Ta'til means denying God's attributes or agency.
3
See the destruction of the proof
The author says Thumama 'nullified for himself the way of proving the Maker.' Reflect on why, if acts can happen without a creator, the argument from design collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لها (١) ، وأبطل بهذه البدعة على نفسه طريق إثبات الصانع (۲) حيث جوز أن كثيرًا (١) من الأفعال المحكمة المتقنة تخرج من العدم إلى الوجود وتختص بالوجود بعد العدم من غير تخصيص مخصص (۳) وإيجاد موجد، وهو التعطيل المحض – نسأل الله العصمة (٤) عن القول بمثله.
By this innovation he nullified for himself the way of proving the Maker, for he allowed that many well-ordered, precise acts come out of non-existence into existence, and become specified with existence after non-existence without the specification of a specifier and the bringing into existence of an existentiator—and that is pure ta'til (denial of God's attributes/agency). We ask Allah for protection from saying something like it.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Mu'tazilite position attributed to Thumama. The author states that this bid'ah (innovation) destroys the proof for the existence of the Creator because it claims that many perfectly ordered acts can come into being without a specific agent to bring them about. This is described as 'ta'til mahd' (absolute denial of divine agency). The author concludes by seeking Allah's protection from such a statement.

Key Terms

إثبات الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for well-ordered acts to come into existence without a Creator.
Correction: This would nullify the proof for the Creator and lead to ta'til, which is denying Allah's attributes and agency.

Checkpoint

A) A new proof for God
B) Pure ta'til
C) The acquisition of acts
D) The will of the agent

Key Takeaway
Thumama's claim that acts occur without a creator is pure ta'til and destroys the proof for God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even subtly, attributed a well-ordered blessing in your life—your health, a solved problem, a provision—to luck or mere coincidence rather than to Allah's direct specification and creation? Write down one such instance and, in light of this lesson, consciously replace that thought with the acknowledgment that Allah is the Mukhasis and Mujid.
?
If you truly believe that every precise act in your life requires a Creator, how does that reshape your gratitude and reliance on Allah? List three specific things today that you will verbally attribute to Allah's creation, saying 'Allah brought this into existence,' and commit to doing so daily for the next week.

Mu'tazilite View on Generated Acts (Tawallud)
Explanation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of generated acts from reality and ruling perspectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the majority of Mu'tazilites attribute generated effects to human agents and why they hold humans morally responsible for those effects.

Core Idea
Generated acts are morally attributed to the agent of their cause.

The Mu'tazilite school is known for its rationalist approach to theology, including a distinctive theory of causality called tawallud (generation). In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents the majority Mu'tazilite position on how certain effects are produced and who is accountable for them. The discussion is divided into two perspectives: the metaphysical reality (haqiqa) of how generated acts occur, and the legal-moral ruling (hukm) that determines blameworthiness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the gist. Notice the two distinct sections introduced by 'this is from the perspective of reality' and 'from the perspective of ruling'.
2
Understand the reality (haqiqa) of generation
Focus on how Mu'tazilites say generated effects come about: they exist according to the will and intention of the agent of the cause, just like his direct acts. Example: pushing a body to move it.
3
Understand the ruling (hukm) of blame
Now see how the same agent is blamed and punished for the generated effects (e.g., wounding another). The reasoning: if it were not his act, blame would be impossible.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: why is this distinction important? It establishes that humans are morally responsible for the consequences of their actions, even indirect ones.
This contrasts with other schools that may limit human agency to direct acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما جمهور المعتزلة فإنهم يزعمون أن تلك المعاني في محالها توجد على وفق إرادة فاعل السبب وقصده كما توجد أفعاله القائمة به على وفق إرادته وقصده فإنه إذا أراد أن يتحرك الجسم حركة يسيرة دفعه دفعة خفيفة، ومتى أراد أن يتحرك حركة قوية دفعه دفعة قوية، وكذلك الحال في الألم وذهاب السهم وأشباه ذلك(٥)، هذا من حيث الحقيقة، ومن حيث الحكم أن فاعل أسبابها يلام عليها ويعاقب ويعذب عليه ويعاقب فإن الإنسان يعاتب ويعاقب على جرح الغير وإيلامه، ولو لم يكن ذلك فعله لما توجهت عليه اللائمة ولا استحق عليه العقوبة المتلفة أو الناهكة إذ استيجاب ذلك واستحقاقه على فعل الغير محال.
As for the majority of the Mu'tazilites, they claim that those meanings (effects) in their loci come into existence in accordance with the will and intention of the agent of the cause, just as his own actions that subsist in him come into existence in accordance with his will and intention. For if he wants the body to move a small movement, he pushes it with a light push; and if he wants it to move a strong movement, he pushes it with a strong push. Likewise with regard to pain, the flight of an arrow, and the like (⁵). This is from the perspective of reality. And from the perspective of ruling, the agent of their causes is blamed for them, punished, and tortured. For a person is reproached and punished for wounding another and causing him pain; were it not his act, he would not be subject to blame, nor would he deserve destructive or exhaustive punishment, because deserving and meriting that due to another's act is impossible.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite doctrine of tawallud (generation of acts). The author explains that the majority of Mu'tazilites believe that the generated effects (like movement, pain) occur in the substrates (e.g., the pushed body) according to the will and intention of the agent of the cause (e.g., the pusher), just like his own direct acts. They provide examples: a light push for a small movement, a strong push for a big movement. This is the 'haqiqa' (reality). As for the 'hukm' (ruling or legal-moral status), the agent of the causes is blameworthy and punishable for the generated effects (like wounding or pain). If it were not his act, blame would not be deserved, because one cannot be punished for another's act. This establishes the Mu'tazilite position that generated acts are attributable to the human agent and he is morally responsible for them.

Key Terms
تولد
جمهور المعتزلة
إرادة
سبب
حقيقة
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Generated effects are not attributable to the human agent and thus humans should not be blamed for them.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue that generated effects are indeed attributable to the agent of the cause, and he is blameworthy for them, as the passage states.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do Mu'tazilites claim about generated effects?
A) They occur by God's direct creation
B) They occur according to the will of the agent of the cause
C) They occur by chance
D) They are not attributable to any agent

Key Takeaway
The majority of Mu'tazilites hold that generated acts are morally imputable to the human agent who initiated the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you cause harm to someone indirectly—through your words, actions, or negligence—do you truly accept that you are accountable before Allah for that harm? Or do you internally minimize your role, telling yourself 'it wasn't really my fault'? How does the Mu'tazilite argument about generated acts challenge your excuses?
?
Imagine a situation where your action led to someone's injury or spiritual downfall, even unintentionally. Do you feel the weight of that consequence in your prayer and repentance? Or do you brush it off because the harm was 'just a side effect'? This lesson insists that generated effects are morally yours. Are you prepared to face Allah for every consequence you set in motion?
?
If you truly believed that every generated effect of your actions would be accounted for, what would you change in your daily life—your driving, your joking, your advising? Name one specific behavior you will start or stop today because of this truth.

The Mu'tazili-Sunni Exchange on Divine Will and Command
A marginal note records a dialogue and argument between a Mu'tazili and a Sunni scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that divine will must conform to divine command and the Sunni response affirming God's absolute will over all things.

Core Idea
God's will governs all that occurs, even if His command differs from His will.

This marginal note records a succinct theological exchange that captures the core disagreement between Mu'tazilism and Sunni Islam regarding divine will. The Mu'tazili asserts God's transcendence from evil, while the Sunni affirms God's sovereignty over all events. The note then presents a summary of the Mu'tazili argument that God's will must align with His commands, with refutations of the contrary view.

Guided Path
1
Read the exchange
Read the opening dialogue between the Mu'tazili and the Sunni. Notice how each statement reflects a different theological commitment.
The Mu'tazili focuses on God's purity; the Sunni on God's sovereignty.
2
Identify the speakers
The commentary identifies the Mu'tazili as 'Abd al-Jabbar and the Sunni as Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. Consider why these figures are representative of their schools.
3
Understand the Mu'tazili argument
The passage details 'Abd al-Jabbar's proof that command implies will. Follow the logical steps: a command is not a command by its form but by the will of the commander.
The argument uses the analogy of human command.
4
Consider the consequences
The argument warns that if God could will evil, it would lead to impossible consequences such as God lying, breaking promises, or commanding evil.
These consequences are presented as absurd to refute the contrary view.
5
Reflect on the significance
This exchange highlights the Sunni position that God's will is above all, while the Mu'tazili seeks to align will with justice to avoid attributing evil to God.
Contrast the two theological approaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال معتزلي لسني : سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء. فقال السني مجيبا سبحان من لا يكون في ملكه خلاف ما شاء. وقد قال السعد في شرح العقائد ص 96 المعتزلي هو عبد الجبار والسني هو أبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني. ويقدم القاضي عبد الجبار أكثر من عشر أدلة على موافقة الأمر للإرادة من ذلك مثلاً: أنه قد ثبت أن الله تعالى أمر بالعبادات ورغب في فعلها والأمر لا يكون أمرًا لصورته ولا لسائر أحواله، وإنما يكون أمرًا لإرادة الأمر من المأمور ما أمره به ولذلك يساويه في الصيغة وسائر الأحوال ما ليس يأمر به. والأمر لا يكون آمرًا لإرادة إحداثه ولا لإرادة كونه خطابًا لمن هو خطاب له ولا لعلم العالم به ولا لمتعلقه فيجب أن يكون إنما يكون صار آمرًا لإرادة المأمور به، وذلك لأن الواحد منا لا يأمر بالشيء إلا ويريده، ومتى لم يرده لم يكن القول الواقع من جهته أمرًا. وكل ذلك يبين أنه سبحانه يريد من العباد كل ما أمرهم به فعلوه أم لم يفعلوه وأيضا أنه لو صح أن يريد القبائح لصح أن يفعل مثل المراد وينفرد به فكان هذا يؤدي إلى أن يجوز أن يكذب في إخباره ولا يفي بوعده ووعيده وأن يأمر بالقبيح وينهى عن الحسن وأن يريد إظهار المعجزات على الكذابين وفي ذلك الخروج من الدين. راجع المغني لعبد الجبار جـ 6 ص 218-255.
A Mu'tazili said to a Sunni: 'Glorified is He who is free from all indecency.' So the Sunni replied: 'Glorified is He in whose kingdom nothing happens contrary to what He wills.' And al-Sa'd said in Sharh al-'Aqa'id p. 96: the Mu'tazili is 'Abd al-Jabbar and the Sunni is Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. And Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar presents more than ten proofs on the conformity of command to will, for example: that it is established that God commanded acts of worship and encouraged their performance, and a command is not a command by its form nor by its other states; rather it is a command because of the commander's will for what he commands from the commanded. Therefore, what is not commanded equals it in form and other states. And a command is not a command because of the will to bring it about, nor because of the will for it to be a speech addressed to whom it is addressed, nor because of the world's knowledge of it, nor because of its object; rather it becomes a command only because of the will for the commanded thing. That is because none among us commands a thing unless he wills it, and when he does not will it, the utterance coming from him is not a command. And all that shows that He, exalted, wills from His servants everything He commands them, whether they do it or not. Also, if it were possible for Him to will ugly things, then it would be possible for Him to do the like of what is willed and be unique in it. That would lead to allowing that He could lie in His reports, not fulfill His promise and threat, command evil and forbid good, and will the manifestation of miracles upon liars — and that would be departing from religion. Refer to al-Mughni by 'Abd al-Jabbar, vol. 6, pp. 218-255.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This marginal note records a famous exchange: A Mu'tazili says 'Glorified is He who is free from all indecency,' implying that God cannot will evil. The Sunni responds 'Glorified is He in whose kingdom nothing happens contrary to what He wills,' affirming that everything that occurs is by God's will. The note then identifies the speakers as Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar (Mu'tazili) and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini (Sunni). It summarizes 'Abd al-Jabbar's argument that divine will must conform to divine command; otherwise, impossible consequences would follow such as God lying, breaking promises, commanding evil, etc. The argument is drawn from 'Abd al-Jabbar's work al-Mughni.

Key Terms
معتزلي
سني
موافقة الأمر للإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni response is often misinterpreted as fatalism, suggesting that God's will coerces human actions and eliminates moral responsibility.
Correction: The Sunni position affirms God's sovereignty over all events but does not deny human responsibility; rather, it emphasizes that nothing occurs outside God's will while human actions are still subject to divine command.

Checkpoint
In the marginal note, what is the Sunni's reply to the Mu'tazili?
A) God is free from all indecency
B) Nothing happens in God's kingdom contrary to His will
C) God does not will evil
D) God's will follows His command

Key Takeaway
The exchange and argument demonstrate the Sunni conviction that God's will is unconditionally sovereign, while the Mu'tazili insists on a necessary alignment between God's will and His commands to safeguard divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that God willed that sin to occur? How does this belief affect your repentance and your view of God's mercy?
?
If God's will is sovereign over every event, including your moments of disobedience, how does that reshape your understanding of His justice? Does it make you more grateful for His forgiveness or more fearful of His judgment?
?
The Mu'tazili tried to protect God's purity by denying He wills evil, but the Sunni affirmed His sovereignty over all things. Which view makes you more humble in your own actions? Why?

Mu'tazila Scriptural Objections Against Divine Will
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila's scriptural objections citing four Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four Quranic verses the Mu'tazila use to argue that Allah does not will evil or disbelief.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila use scripture to argue that Allah only wills good.

The Mu'tazila advanced several scriptural proofs against the Maturidi position that Allah creates and wills all actions, including evil. In this passage, Nasafi records four key verses they cite. Pay close attention to how each verse is interpreted by the Mu'tazila and how it challenges the Maturidi view.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural objections
Read the passage introducing the Mu'tazila's scriptural arguments. Notice the structure: each verse is presented with the Mu'tazila's interpretation.
There are four verses cited.
2
Identify each verse and its argument
List the four verses: 51:56 (created for worship), 2:185 (intends ease), 6:148 (polytheists' saying), 40:31 (no injustice). For each, note how the Mu'tazila use it to argue against Allah willing evil.
3
Consider the Maturidi response hinted at
Nasafi ends by saying the Mu'tazila assist the statement of those whom Allah belied. Reflect on how a Maturidi might respond to these scriptural objections.
Maturidis distinguish between will as decree and will as command.

فنقول: إن المعتزلة يتعلقون بشبه بعضها سمعية وبعضها عقلية. فأما السمعية فنحو قوله تعالى : ( وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الحنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ) أخبر الله تعالى أنه خلقهم جميعًا للعبادة، وأنتم تقولون إنه خلق الكفرة منهم والعصاة لا للعبادة بل للكفر والمعصية وقوله تعالى : يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ ) والكفر أعسر العسر، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا لَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مَا أَشْرَكْنَا (٤). فالله تعالى أخبر أنهم يقولون : لو شاء الله ما أشركنا ثم كذبهم في ذلك ورد قولهم بقوله : كَذَلِكَ كَذَّبَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ () () وقوله : كَذَلِكَ فَعَلَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ (1) وأنتم تساعدون في المقالة من كذبه الله تعالى بنص كتابه وهو باطل، وقوله: وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ ) () وعندكم يريد كل ظلم كان ويكون، وهو خلاف ما في الكتاب، وإكذاب الله تعالى في ما أخبر. [الشبه العقلية للمعتزلة]: وأما العقلية فكقولهم: مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارًا بالشاهد، ومريد الكفر كافر ومريد المعصية عاصي ويعتبر بالشاهد، ومريد شتم نفسه والافتراء عليه سفيه أيضا في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب إذ الشاهد دليل الغائب، ولأن العبد لا يمكنه
We say: Indeed the Mu'tazila cling to objections, some of them scriptural and some rational. As for the scriptural, such as His saying (And I created not the jinn and mankind except that they worship Me) – Allah informed that He created them all for worship, and you say that He created the disbelievers among them and the disobedient not for worship but for disbelief and disobedience; and His saying (Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship) and disbelief is the hardest hardship; and His saying (Those who associated partners will say: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners') – Allah informed that they say 'If Allah willed we would not have associated partners' then He belied them in that and rejected their saying by His saying (Thus did those before them belie) and His saying (Thus did those before them do) – and you assist in the statement of one whom Allah belied by the text of His Book, and that is false; and His saying (And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants) – and according to you He intends every injustice that was and will be, and that contradicts what is in the Book, and it is belying Allah in what He informed. [The rational objections of the Mu'tazila]: As for the rational, such as their saying: The one who wills foolishness is a fool, by analogy with the witnessed; and the one who wills disbelief is a disbeliever, and the one who wills disobedience is a disobedient, and it is considered by the witnessed; and the one who wills insult against himself and slander against himself is also foolish in the witnessed; so likewise in the unseen, for the witnessed is an evidence for the unseen; and because the servant cannot...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents the objections (shubah) of the Mu'tazila against the Maturidi view that Allah creates all actions, including evil, and that He wills them in the sense of creative decree. The objections are divided into two kinds: scriptural (sam'iyya) and rational ('aqliyya). The scriptural objections cite four Quranic verses. First, (51:56) 'I created jinn and humans only to worship Me' – the Mu'tazila argue that if Allah created all for worship, He could not have created disbelievers for disbelief. Second, (2:185) 'Allah intends ease for you, not hardship' – and disbelief is the greatest hardship. Third, (6:148) the polytheists say 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' – but Allah belies them, implying that if He had willed, they would not have associated; thus they argue that Allah does not will disbelief. Fourth, (40:31) 'Allah does not intend injustice for the servants' – but according to the Maturidis, He intends every injustice, which contradicts the verse. The rational objections use analogy from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). They argue: whoever wills foolishness is foolish; whoever wills disbelief is a disbeliever; whoever wills disobedience is disobedient. Furthermore, willing insult and slander against oneself is foolish. Therefore, if Allah willed evil, He would be foolish or evil, which is impossible. The passage ends abruptly with the beginning of another rational argument: 'because the servant cannot...' indicating that the text continues but is cut off in this window.

سمعية
العبادة
اليسر
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila interpret the verses as absolute proof that Allah does not will evil in any sense.
Correction: The Maturidis distinguish between different meanings of will (e.g., decree vs. command) and argue that these verses do not negate Allah's creative will of evil actions.

Checkpoint
How many Quranic verses are cited by the Mu'tazila in their scriptural objections?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila cite four Quranic verses to argue that Allah does not will disbelief or injustice, challenging the Maturidi view of divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazila's objection that Allah only intends ease, and that disbelief is the greatest hardship, does your heart find that argument compelling? Where do you feel the tension between Allah's mercy and His decree? Will you trust that His will is good even when it leads to hardship for you?
?
The Mu'tazila used the verse 'Allah does not intend injustice' to argue that He cannot will evil. Have you ever faced a situation that seemed unjust, and doubted whether Allah willed it? After this lesson, can you surrender that doubt to the truth that Allah's will is all-encompassing and wise, even when you cannot see it?
?
Nasafi says that the Mu'tazila's interpretation aligns them with the polytheists whom Allah belied. Are there any beliefs about Allah that you hold because they 'feel right' or satisfy your sense of justice, rather than submitting to what the Quran and sound scholarship teach? Will you examine your heart and repent from any such leanings?

Mu'tazila Rational Objections: Analogy from the Witnessed
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila's rational objection using analogy from the witnessed to the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila rational argument that if Allah willed evil, He would be considered foolish or evil, based on analogy from human behavior.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila judge the unseen by the witnessed: a will for evil makes one evil.

Beyond scriptural arguments, the Mu'tazila also employed rational arguments using analogy from the witnessed world (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). In this passage, Nasafi begins to present their reasoning: the one who wills foolishness is foolish, the one who wills disbelief is a disbeliever, and so on. Although the passage cuts off, the core analogy is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the rational objections. Notice the repeated pattern 'مريد السفه سفيه' and the reference to the witnessed as evidence for the unseen.
The passage ends abruptly; we only have the beginning of the rational arguments.
2
Identify the analogy from shahid to gha'ib
The Mu'tazila reason: in the witnessed world, a person who wills foolishness is called foolish; similarly, if Allah willed foolishness, He would be called foolish—which is impossible. Therefore, Allah cannot will evil.
This is an argument from analogy (qiyas).
3
Critically evaluate the analogy
Consider whether the analogy holds. Does the same moral judgment apply to the Creator as to creatures? Maturidis argue that God is not subject to human categories.

?

?

The Mu'tazila's Claim of Lam al-ʿĀqibah
Mu'tazila present their interpretation with analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that the lam in certain verses indicates consequence rather than purpose, and their use of analogies.

Core Idea
Lam al-ʿāqibah shifts divine will from purpose to mere consequence.

Before we can refute the Mu'tazila's interpretation, we need to grasp their claim. They argue that the lam in verses about divine will is like the lam in the story of Moses – it points to the outcome, not the intention. This lesson presents their reasoning and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's claim
Read the opening sentence where they assert that the lam is 'lam al-ʿāqibah' (consequence) rather than 'lam al-taʿlīl' (purpose).
Notice they refer to two verses (Q3:178 and Q7:179) but the immediate example is from Q28:8.
2
Examine the analogy of Pharaoh's family
They use the example of Pharaoh's family picking up Moses: they intended him as a son, but the consequence was enmity. This is presented as evidence that the lam can point to consequence.
The verse they cite is Q28:8.
3
Consider the common sayings
They give additional examples: 'They give birth for death' and 'they build for ruin' – where the intended purpose is different from the known consequence.
These illustrate that language allows expressing consequence as if it were purpose.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazila's logic
Ask yourself: does this analogy hold for God? The next lesson will address the rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت المعتزلة أن اللام في الآيتين جميعًا لام العاقبة لا لام التعليل كما في قوله تعالى: { فَالْتَقَطَهُ آلُ فِرْعَوْنَ لِيَكُونَ لَهُمْ عَدُوًّا وَحَزَنًا } وهم ما التقطوه لهذا وإنما التقطوه ليكون لهم ولدًا وقرة عين، ولكن لما كان عاقبة أمره أن صار لهم عدوا ذكر هذه اللفظة وكما يقال : ولدوا للموت وبنوا للخراب، وأحدٌ لا يلد للموت بل يطلب بقاء الولد ولا يبني للخراب ولكن للانتفاع بها، ولكن لما كان عاقبة أمر الولد الموت وعاقبة أمر الدار الخراب يقال : هذا لبيان العاقبة. والجواب إنما يتصور أن يريد أحد من الآخر ما لا يكون منه، ويفعل فعلاً له ولا يكون هو بل يكون غيره أو ضده ، إن كان الفاعل جاهلاً بالعواقب فيفعل فعلاً لغرض فلا يحصل ذلك بل يحصل غيره، فأما من لا يجهل العواقب كيف يريد عاقبة لفعل يعلم حقيقة أنها لا تكون على ما يريد؟ وهل يفعل هذا إلا سفيه؟ ومثله في الشاهد كمن يبذر بذرا في الأرض ويريد خروج الزرع وحصول النماء، علمه أنه لا ينبت ولا يخرج، وكذا استعمال لام العاقبة فمن يجوز عليه الجهل بالعواقب فيفعل فعلاً لقصد فيحصل له ضد تلك العاقبة، ولا يحصل له ما هو المقصود، فيقال على طريق تعريف عاقبة فعله الوخيمة الحاصلة لا على وفق قصده ليتنبه لذلك فلا يعود إلى العمل بمثله فيما يستأنف من عمره، فأما في حق من هو علام الغيوب فاستعماله فاسد والله الموفق. وقال تعالى : { فَمَن يُرِدِ اللهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَامِ وَمَن يُرِدْ أَن يُضِلَّهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَبًا } فجعل شرح الصدر سببًا يحصل به الإيمان، وضيق الصدر سببًا يحصل به الكفر.
And the Mu'tazila claimed that the lam in both verses is the lam of consequence (lam al-'āqibah), not the lam of purpose (lam al-ta'līl), as in His saying the Exalted: {Then the family of Pharaoh picked him up to be for them an enemy and a cause of grief.} And they did not pick him up for this; rather they picked him up so that he would be for them a son and a comfort of the eye, but when the consequence of his affair became that he was an enemy to them, this expression was mentioned. And as it is said: 'They give birth for death and build for ruin,' and no one gives birth for death, rather he seeks the survival of the child, and no one builds for ruin, but for benefit; but since the consequence of the affair of the child is death and the consequence of the affair of the house is ruin, it is said: this is to indicate the consequence. The answer: It can only be imagined that one intends from another what does not come from him, and does an action for him and it does not happen but rather something else or its opposite happens, if the agent is ignorant of consequences, so he does an action for a purpose and that does not occur but something else occurs. As for one who is not ignorant of consequences, how can he intend a consequence for an action while he knows in reality that it will not be as he intends? And does anyone do this except a fool? Its analogy in the witnessed world is like one who sows a seed in the ground and wants the crop to come forth and growth to occur, while he knows that it will not sprout nor come forth. Likewise, the use of the lam of consequence is such that one who is subject to ignorance of consequences does an action with a purpose, and the opposite of that consequence occurs for him, and what is intended does not occur for him, so it is said by way of informing of the evil consequence of his action happening not according to his intent, so that he may pay heed and not return to doing the like in the rest of his life. As for the case of the Knower of the Unseen, its use is corrupt, and Allah is the Grantor of success. And He said the Exalted: {And whomever Allah wills to guide, He expands his breast for Islam; and whomever He wills to lead astray, He makes his breast tight and constricted.} So He made the expansion of the breast a cause by which faith occurs, and the tightness of the breast a cause by which disbelief occurs.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's claim that the lam in the verses Q3:178 and Q7:179 is 'lam al-ʿāqibah' (lam of consequence) rather than 'lam al-taʿlīl' (lam of purpose). The Mu'tazila argue that just as in Q28:8, where Pharaoh's family picked up Moses with the intention of him becoming a son, but the consequence was enmity, so too the verses about divine will indicate consequence, not purpose. The author rebuts this by arguing that such usage is only valid for agents ignorant of outcomes. A person who does an action for a purpose may get a different result only if they are ignorant. But God is the Knower of the Unseen (ʿallām al-ghuyūb); He cannot be ignorant, so to say He acts with a consequence contrary to His will would be foolishness (safah). The author gives an analogy: a farmer who sows knowing no crop will grow is a fool; similarly, applying lam al-ʿāqibah to God is corrupt. The passage then cites Q6:125 as evidence that God's will directly causes guidance or misguidance through creating specific causes, not merely allowing consequences.

Key Terms
لام العاقبة
لام التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's interpretation of the lam in Q3:178 and Q7:179?
A) It is lam al-taʿlīl (purpose)
B) It is lam al-ʿāqibah (consequence)
C) It is ambiguous
D) It is a misreading

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use analogies from language and scripture to argue that the lam in certain verses indicates consequence, not purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses like 'He leads astray whom He wills', does it ever trouble your heart? How does the Mu'tazila's attempt to reinterpret this as mere consequence affect your own trust in God's absolute sovereignty? Are you willing to submit entirely, even if you cannot fully understand His wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazila used human analogies to argue that God does not directly intend misguidance. Are there areas in your life where you try to limit God's actions to fit your sense of justice? What would it mean for you to accept that God's will may include things you find difficult to reconcile?
?
You have just read how a single letter (lam) becomes the pivot of a theological debate. Examine your own heart: do you sometimes twist clear texts to avoid uncomfortable truths? Ask yourself honestly: where am I resisting God's decree because it doesn't align with my preferences?

Refutation: Lam al-ʿĀqibah Implies Ignorance
Author rebuts Mu'tazila and cites supporting verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why applying lam al-ʿāqibah to God is invalid, based on His complete knowledge of outcomes.

Core Idea
Only an ignorant agent can intend one outcome but get another; God knows all.

Now that we've seen the Mu'tazila's claim, we examine the refutation. The author argues that using 'lam al-ʿāqibah' for God is absurd because it implies ignorance. He supports this with a powerful analogy and a Qur'anic verse showing direct divine causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the rebuttal
Read from 'The answer' to the end of the passage. Notice how the author builds his argument against the Mu'tazila.
The rebuttal begins with 'والجواب'.
2
Understand the key premise
The author states that lam al-ʿāqibah is only used when the agent is ignorant of the consequence. He gives the example of a farmer sowing knowing no crop will grow – that would be foolish.
This is a crucial point: ignorance is a prerequisite for the mismatch between intention and outcome.
3
Apply to God
Since God is 'All-Knower of the Unseen' (ʿallām al-ghuyūb), He cannot be ignorant. Therefore, applying lam al-ʿāqibah to His actions is corrupt.
The author calls such an attribution 'safah' (foolishness).
4
Examine the supporting verse
The author cites Q6:125 to show that God directly creates causes for guidance and misguidance, not merely allowing consequences.
This verse reinforces the idea that God intends the outcome through direct causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت المعتزلة أن اللام في الآيتين جميعًا لام العاقبة لا لام التعليل كما في قوله تعالى: { فَالْتَقَطَهُ آلُ فِرْعَوْنَ لِيَكُونَ لَهُمْ عَدُوًّا وَحَزَنًا } وهم ما التقطوه لهذا وإنما التقطوه ليكون لهم ولدًا وقرة عين، ولكن لما كان عاقبة أمره أن صار لهم عدوا ذكر هذه اللفظة وكما يقال : ولدوا للموت وبنوا للخراب، وأحدٌ لا يلد للموت بل يطلب بقاء الولد ولا يبني للخراب ولكن للانتفاع بها، ولكن لما كان عاقبة أمر الولد الموت وعاقبة أمر الدار الخراب يقال : هذا لبيان العاقبة. والجواب إنما يتصور أن يريد أحد من الآخر ما لا يكون منه، ويفعل فعلاً له ولا يكون هو بل يكون غيره أو ضده ، إن كان الفاعل جاهلاً بالعواقب فيفعل فعلاً لغرض فلا يحصل ذلك بل يحصل غيره، فأما من لا يجهل العواقب كيف يريد عاقبة لفعل يعلم حقيقة أنها لا تكون على ما يريد؟ وهل يفعل هذا إلا سفيه؟ ومثله في الشاهد كمن يبذر بذرا في الأرض ويريد خروج الزرع وحصول النماء، علمه أنه لا ينبت ولا يخرج، وكذا استعمال لام العاقبة فمن يجوز عليه الجهل بالعواقب فيفعل فعلاً لقصد فيحصل له ضد تلك العاقبة، ولا يحصل له ما هو المقصود، فيقال على طريق تعريف عاقبة فعله الوخيمة الحاصلة لا على وفق قصده ليتنبه لذلك فلا يعود إلى العمل بمثله فيما يستأنف من عمره، فأما في حق من هو علام الغيوب فاستعماله فاسد والله الموفق. وقال تعالى : { فَمَن يُرِدِ اللهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَامِ وَمَن يُرِدْ أَن يُضِلَّهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَبًا } فجعل شرح الصدر سببًا يحصل به الإيمان، وضيق الصدر سببًا يحصل به الكفر.
And the Mu'tazila claimed that the lam in both verses is the lam of consequence (lam al-'āqibah), not the lam of purpose (lam al-ta'līl), as in His saying the Exalted: {Then the family of Pharaoh picked him up to be for them an enemy and a cause of grief.} And they did not pick him up for this; rather they picked him up so that he would be for them a son and a comfort of the eye, but when the consequence of his affair became that he was an enemy to them, this expression was mentioned. And as it is said: 'They give birth for death and build for ruin,' and no one gives birth for death, rather he seeks the survival of the child, and no one builds for ruin, but for benefit; but since the consequence of the affair of the child is death and the consequence of the affair of the house is ruin, it is said: this is to indicate the consequence. The answer: It can only be imagined that one intends from another what does not come from him, and does an action for him and it does not happen but rather something else or its opposite happens, if the agent is ignorant of consequences, so he does an action for a purpose and that does not occur but something else occurs. As for one who is not ignorant of consequences, how can he intend a consequence for an action while he knows in reality that it will not be as he intends? And does anyone do this except a fool? Its analogy in the witnessed world is like one who sows a seed in the ground and wants the crop to come forth and growth to occur, while he knows that it will not sprout nor come forth. Likewise, the use of the lam of consequence is such that one who is subject to ignorance of consequences does an action with a purpose, and the opposite of that consequence occurs for him, and what is intended does not occur for him, so it is said by way of informing of the evil consequence of his action happening not according to his intent, so that he may pay heed and not return to doing the like in the rest of his life. As for the case of the Knower of the Unseen, its use is corrupt, and Allah is the Grantor of success. And He said the Exalted: {And whomever Allah wills to guide, He expands his breast for Islam; and whomever He wills to lead astray, He makes his breast tight and constricted.} So He made the expansion of the breast a cause by which faith occurs, and the tightness of the breast a cause by which disbelief occurs.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's claim that the lam in the verses Q3:178 and Q7:179 is 'lam al-ʿāqibah' (lam of consequence) rather than 'lam al-taʿlīl' (lam of purpose). The Mu'tazila argue that just as in Q28:8, where Pharaoh's family picked up Moses with the intention of him becoming a son, but the consequence was enmity, so too the verses about divine will indicate consequence, not purpose. The author rebuts this by arguing that such usage is only valid for agents ignorant of outcomes. A person who does an action for a purpose may get a different result only if they are ignorant. But God is the Knower of the Unseen (ʿallām al-ghuyūb); He cannot be ignorant, so to say He acts with a consequence contrary to His will would be foolishness (safah). The author gives an analogy: a farmer who sows knowing no crop will grow is a fool; similarly, applying lam al-ʿāqibah to God is corrupt. The passage then cites Q6:125 as evidence that God's will directly causes guidance or misguidance through creating specific causes, not merely allowing consequences.

Key Terms
جهل بالعواقب
علام الغيوب
سفه
لام العاقبة
لام التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That lam al-ʿāqibah can be applied to God's actions like it is applied to human actions.
Correction: Lam al-ʿāqibah only applies when the agent is ignorant; God is omniscient, so it cannot apply to Him.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that lam al-ʿāqibah cannot be used for God?
A) Because God does not create consequences
B) Because God is not ignorant of consequences
C) Because the lam in the verses is always lam al-taʿlīl
D) Because the Mu'tazila are always wrong

Key Takeaway
Lam al-ʿāqibah is only valid for ignorant agents; since God knows all outcomes, attributing such lam to Him is irrational and corrupt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your carefully made plans fail and you feel frustrated, do you ever subconsciously think that God is unaware or indifferent? How can the truth that God is 'the Knower of the Unseen' reshape your reaction the next time you face disappointment?
?
If God's will and the actual outcome are always perfectly aligned, then what does that mean for a sin you committed yesterday? Are you willing to accept that God willed that sin for a wisdom you may not understand, and respond with humility rather than blame?
?
The farmer who sows seed knowing it won't grow is called a fool. In what areas of your life are you still expecting a result that contradicts what God has clearly revealed about His ways? How does this lesson expose your own foolishness and call you to realignment?

Refutation of Ja'far ibn Harb's Jabariyya Claim
Nasafi refutes Ja'far ibn Harb's claim about the verse addressing Jabariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that Ja'far ibn Harb's interpretation of the verse does not refute the Jabariyya and why a useful refutation must appeal to a point the opponent concedes.

Core Idea
A refutation only works if the opponent admits the implication is false.

In this passage, Nasafi engages with a rival theological claim about the verse of Noah. Ja'far ibn Harb had suggested that the verse was addressed to a group of Jabariyya who believed God wills corruption, and that Noah's response was a refutation. Nasafi dismantles this interpretation by showing it fails as a refutation. He teaches us a key principle of theological argument: a valid objection must lead to a consequence the opponent himself acknowledges as false.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ja'far ibn Harb's claim
Read the passage to see what Ja'far argued: that the verse was addressed to Jabariyya among Noah's people and that Noah's words were meant to alert them to the absurdity of their position.
Ja'far thought the verse was a direct refutation of Jabariyya.
2
Examine Nasafi's objection
Notice how Nasafi immediately questions: if Ja'far's interpretation holds, what notification or binding argument does it provide? He argues that simply denouncing a statement by its implications is useless if the opponent does not admit those implications are false.
3
Learn the principle of beneficial refutation
Nasafi explains that a refutation must lead to something the opponent himself acknowledges as corrupt. Only then can you argue: 'If your view were true, it would not lead to this false outcome.'
The principle: sound premises cannot produce false conclusions.
4
Conclusion: Ja'far's interpretation is corrupt
Since the Jabariyya do not admit that the futility of prophecy is a problem, Ja'far's argument has no force. Nasafi concludes that Ja'far's interpretation is itself corrupt, driven by confusion.
The Arabic uses word 'hayra' (حيرة) meaning confusion or perplexity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قاله جعفر بن حرب أن الآية تدل أنه كان في قوم نوح عليه السلام قوم مجبرة يقولون : إن الله تعالى يريد الفساد فخاطبهم منبها لهم على بطلان قولهم فقال: وَلَا يَنفَعُكُمْ نُصْحِي إِنْ أَرَدتُ أَنْ أَنصَحَ لَكُمْ فيما أدعوكم إن كان الأمر كما ذكرتم من أنه تعالى المريد لفسادكم ويخلق الكفر فيكم. فاسد، لأن الأمر لو كان كما زعم جعفر بن حرب أي تنبيه يحصل لأولئك بهذا القول؟ وأي إلزام حجة بذلك؟ وأكثر ما في الباب أنك تقول - يشنع عليهم بذلك: إن على مقالتكم هذه لا ينفع بعث الأنبياء مبشرين ومنذرين ولا الدعاء إلى الله تعالى، وأن أولئك كانوا منكرين بعث الأنبياء والدعاء على ألسنتهم إلى الله تعالى وتشنيع مقالة بما يفضي إليه المقالة، وأهل المقالة يعتقدون صحته لا يجدي نفعا إنما ينفع ذلك أن لو كانت المقالة تفضي إلى أمر يعترف صاحب المقالة بفساده ويقر ببطلانه فيستدل عليه فيقال: لو كانت مقالتك حقًا لما أفضت إلى هذا الفاسد، إذ الصحيح لا ينتج الفاسد، وإنما الفاسد هو الذي ينتج الفاسد، فأما إذا كان صاحب المقالة لا يقر بفساد ما أفضت إليه مقالته لم يكن للاحتجاج بذلك عليه منفعة ولا فيه حكمة، فدل أن هذا فاسد دفعته إليه الحيرة.
And what Ja'far ibn Harb said that the verse indicates that among Noah's people there were a group of Jabariyya who said: 'God, exalted, intends corruption.' So he addressed them, alerting them to the invalidity of their statement, saying: 'And my advice will not profit you if I intended to advise you...' if the matter is as you mentioned that He, exalted, is the willer of your corruption and creates disbelief in you. This is corrupt, because if the matter were as Ja'far ibn Harb claimed, what notification would those people obtain from this statement? And what binding argument is there in that? The most that can be said is that you say – you denounce them by that: 'According to your statement, the sending of prophets as bringers of good news and warners, nor the call to God, are of no benefit.' And those people were deniers of the sending of prophets and the call to God upon their tongues. And denouncing a statement by what the statement leads to, while the adherents of the statement believe it is sound, is of no benefit. That benefits only if the statement leads to something the adherent of the statement admits is corrupt and acknowledges its invalidity, so that it is used as evidence against him, and it is said: 'If your statement were true, it would not lead to this corrupt thing, for the sound does not produce the corrupt; only the corrupt produces the corrupt.' But if the adherent of the statement does not acknowledge the corruption of what his statement leads to, then there is no benefit in arguing against him by that, nor any wisdom in it. So this indicates that this is corrupt, driven to it by confusion.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the argument of Ja'far ibn Harb. Ja'far claimed that the verse was addressed to a group among Noah's people who held Jabariyya beliefs, i.e., that God wills evil and creates disbelief in them. According to Ja'far, Noah's response was meant to show the futility of their position by saying that if God wills their misguidance, then his advice is useless. The author of this work, Nasafi, declares this interpretation corrupt. He argues that if Ja'far's reading were correct, it would not serve as a valid refutation. The Jabariyya already deny the benefit of prophecy and calling to God, so pointing out that their view leads to the futility of prophecy is not a refutation they would accept; they already believe that. A valid refutation must lead to a conclusion that the opponent himself admits is false. For example, if a certain doctrine leads to a contradiction that the opponent acknowledges, then it is a powerful argument. But if the opponent does not accept the alleged consequence as false, the argument has no force. Since the Jabariyya do not see the futility of prophecy as a problem, the verse as interpreted by Ja'far would not convince them. Therefore, Ja'far's interpretation is itself corrupt and born of confusion.

Key Terms
مجبرة
تشنيع
إلزام
حيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that pointing out the implications of a belief is always a valid refutation, even if the opponent does not accept those implications as false.
Correction: Nasafi teaches that a refutation is only effective if it leads to a consequence the opponent himself admits is false. Otherwise, it is mere denunciation with no persuasive force.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is Ja'far ibn Harb's interpretation of the verse invalid as a refutation of the Jabariyya?
A) Because the Jabariyya already believe that prophecy is futile, so pointing out that their view leads to futility is not a refutation they accept.
B) Because the verse actually affirms that God does not will misguidance.
C) Because Noah was not addressing the Jabariyya but the disbelievers.
D) Because Ja'far ibn Harb was a known liar.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that a theological argument is only effective if it forces the opponent to confront a consequence they admit is false; otherwise, it is mere denunciation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you debate matters of faith, do you ever use arguments that your opponent doesn't even accept as valid? How does that affect the conversation? Will you commit to only using arguments that the other person can admit lead to a contradiction they reject?
?
Is there a belief you hold that, if you followed it to its logical end, would lead to something you find unacceptable? Are you willing to examine that belief honestly, or do you avoid the implication?
?
Nasafi says a refutation is useless if the opponent accepts the consequence. Think of someone you disagree with: have you been trying to 'refute' them with points they already accept? What would it look like to find a real point of leverage instead?

Nasafi's Critique of Abd al-Jabbar's Claim of Clarity
Nasafi criticizes Abd al-Jabbar's claim of clarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi deconstructs Abd al-Jabbar's claim of clarity, revealing its internal tension and the role of sectarian bias.

Core Idea
Love of one's doctrine can blind even a great scholar to obvious flaws.

Nasafi has just listed several Mu'tazili interpretations that he considers invalid. Now he turns to Abd al-Jabbar's assertion that these interpretations are 'clear'. In this passage, Nasafi unpacks the ambiguity of that claim, exposing a fundamental problem: either the clarity is of falsehood (which is useless) or of truth (which is shameful for a scholar of his stature). This sets up a powerful lesson on objectivity in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that Nasafi has been presenting and refuting various Mu'tazili interpretations. Now he addresses the author's own claim that these interpretations are 'clear'.
Abd al-Jabbar is the author of the interpretations.
2
Parse the two possibilities
Identify Nasafi's conditional reasoning: if Abd al-Jabbar means the falsehood is clear, that's acceptable; if he means the correctness is clear, that is shameful.
Note the stark contrast between the two interpretations.
3
Grasp the critique
Understand why claiming clear correctness is shameful: it shows a lack of objectivity and respect for truth, especially coming from a leading scholar.
Nasafi expresses amazement that such a scholar would accept flimsy interpretations.
4
Reflect on the proverb
Consider the saying 'Love of something blinds and deafens.' How does this apply to Abd al-Jabbar and to scholars in general?
This is a universal warning against bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر هذه الوجوه وحكى كل تأويل عن صاحبه عبد الجبار الرازي ثم قال : وكل هذا واضح. فإن أراد أنه واضح وجه بطلانه فهو كما أراد، وإن أراد أنه واضح وجه صحته فهو يقول بقول يجب به الاستحياء من الناس والاستغفار من الله تعالى، والتعجب من رجل يدعي الترأس على أهل نحلته والتبحر في علوم أصحابه. ثم يرضى بمثل هذه التأويلات مع وهائها وظهور فسادها لأول الفكرة، وما أصدق ما قيل: حبك الشيء يعمي ويصم.
He mentioned these aspects and narrated every interpretation from its author, Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi, then said: 'All of this is clear.' If he means that the aspect of its falsehood is clear, then it is as he intended; but if he means that the aspect of its correctness is clear, then he says something that requires shame before people and seeking forgiveness from Allah Almighty. And it is amazing that a man who claims leadership over the people of his sect and mastery in the sciences of his companions would then be satisfied with such interpretations, despite their flimsiness and the obviousness of their falsehood to even the first thought. How true is what was said: 'Your love of something blinds and deafens you.'
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage critiques Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi, a Mu'tazili leader, for claiming that all the mentioned interpretations are clear. Nasafi points out that if Abd al-Jabbar means the falsehood is clear, then he agrees with the refutation. But if he means the correctness is clear, then his statement is shameful. He expresses amazement that a man of his status would accept such flimsy interpretations, and quotes the proverb that love of something blinds and deafens a person.

Key Terms
تعجب
وهاء
حب الشيء يعمي ويصم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A scholar's authority makes his interpretations automatically plausible, even when they are weak.
Correction: Nasafi shows that even a leading scholar's claims must be evaluated critically; bias can lead to acceptance of flimsy arguments.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi imply about Abd al-Jabbar's claim that the interpretations are clear?
A) He agrees that they are clearly correct.
B) He thinks they are clearly false, and Abd al-Jabbar's love of his sect blinds him.
C) He says they are ambiguous.
D) He thinks Abd al-Jabbar is lying.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi exposes the ambiguity in Abd al-Jabbar's claim and uses it to highlight how bias can undermine a scholar's objectivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What specific belief or practice do you hold that you have never seriously questioned because of your love for a teacher, group, or tradition? How would you react if you discovered it was built on a 'flimsy' foundation like the interpretations Nasafi critiqued?
?
Nasafi says a scholar of his stature should be ashamed to make such weak claims. Are you more concerned about being right or about being seen as right by those whose approval matters to you? When was the last time you admitted error publicly?
?
The proverb 'love of something blinds and deafens'—what in your life might be currently blinding you from seeing a truth you need to accept? Will you take one concrete step today to confront that blind spot?
