Historical Recantations and Ja'far's Initial View on Lutf
Reports recantations and Ja'far's initial view on lutf and al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical reports of recantations within the Mu'tazilite school and Ja'far ibn Harb's initial position regarding the concept of lutf and al-aslah.

Core Idea
Ja'far initially believed God withholds a grace that would cause voluntary belief yet preserve reward hierarchy.

This lesson introduces two historical events within Mu'tazilite kalam: the repentance of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamar and Ja'far ibn Harb's original view on divine grace. Understanding these narratives helps see how key doctrines evolved through debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the recantation reports
Notice how al-Ka'bi transmits the reports of Bishr's and Ja'far's recantations, citing Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat and Abu Musa al-Murdar.
These reports show the chain of transmission within the Mu'tazilite school.
2
Examine Ja'far's initial view
Identify the components: a lutf (grace) exists with God; if given to disbelievers they would believe voluntarily; but they would deserve less reward than if they believed without it; therefore the most beneficial (al-aslah) is to withhold it.
Ja'far's view combines divine grace, human choice, and reward hierarchy.
3
Reflect on the notion of al-aslah
Ja'far defines al-aslah as God's act of withholding the lutf, because God aims to give servants the highest stations and greatest reward.
This definition differs from later definitions in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الكعبي في كتابه المقالات أنه تاب عن هذا ورجع إلى قول أصحابه قال: كتب إلى ذلك أبو الحسين الخياط، وحكاه عن بعض البصريين عن الشحام عن بشر قال : وذكر - يعني أبا الحسين - أنه بلغه عن أبي موسى المردار أنه كان يحكي التوبة عنه وكان جعفر بن حرب يقول : إن عند الله تعالى لطفا لو أعطاه الكافرين لأمنوا اختيارًا إيماناً لا يستحقون عليه من الثواب ما يستحقون به إذا آمنوا مع عدم ذلك اللطف، والأصلح لهم ما فعل بهم من تركه إعطائهم ذلك اللطف؛ لأن الله تعالى لا يعرض عباده إلا لأعلى المنازل وأشرفها وأفضل الثواب وأكثرها قال الكعبي : ثم ترك جعفر بن حرب هذا القول ورجع إلى قول أصحابه من أن ذلك محال؛ لأنه إذا كان الإيمان يقع منهم عند حدوث اللطف لا محالة فهو واقع ضرورة ولو لم يكن ضرورة جاز أن لا يقع ولا يوجد، إذا قال قائل: هو واقع لا يكن محالة ثم قال: هو اختيار فقد ناقض وجمع بين الاختيار والضرورة وذلك محال، قال الكعبي: كتب إلي توبة جعفر من هذا القول أبو الحسين، والأمر في ذلك مشهور. ثم الأصلح عند البغداديين منهم ما هو الأصلح في الحكمة والتدبير، وعند بعض البصريين منهم الصلاح هو النفع والأصلح هو الأنفع.
And al-Ka'bi mentioned in his book al-Maqalat that he (Bishr) repented from this (view) and returned to the statement of his companions. He said: Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat wrote to him about that. And he narrated from some Basrans from al-Shahham from Bishr, he said: and he mentioned—meaning Abu al-Husayn—that it reached him from Abu Musa al-Murdar that he used to narrate the repentance from him. And Ja'far ibn Harb used to say: Verily, with God Most High there is a grace (lutf) that if He gave it to the disbelievers, they would believe voluntarily, a faith for which they would not deserve the reward that they would deserve if they believed without that grace. And the most beneficial (al-aslah) for them is what He did with them of refraining from giving them that grace, because God Most High only exposes His servants to the highest and most noble stations and the best and most abundant reward. Al-Ka'bi said: Then Ja'far ibn Harb left this statement and returned to the statement of his companions, that that is impossible; because if belief occurs from them upon the occurrence of the grace inevitably, then it occurs necessarily, and if it were not a necessity, it would be possible that it does not occur and does not exist. If one says: it occurs inevitably, then says: it is voluntary, he has contradicted himself and combined choice and necessity, and that is impossible. Al-Ka'bi said: Abu al-Husayn wrote to me about the repentance of Ja'far from this statement, and the matter regarding that is well-known. Then al-aslah according to the Baghdadis among them is that which is most beneficial in terms of wisdom and administration; and according to some Basrans among them, salah is benefit and al-aslah is that which is more beneficial.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage reports historical developments within the Mu'tazilite school regarding the concept of lutf and al-aslah. First, al-Ka'bi mentions that Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamar repented from his earlier view and returned to the statement of his companions. Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat wrote to him about this repentance, and Abu Musa al-Murdar also narrated it. Then the passage presents the view of Ja'far ibn Harb, who initially held that there is a lutf with God that if given to disbelievers would cause them to believe voluntarily, but they would deserve less reward than if they believed without it. The most beneficial (al-aslah) for them is that God refrains from giving that lutf, because God only exposes His servants to the highest ranks and best reward. However, Ja'far later left this view and returned to the mainstream position that this is impossible, because if belief necessarily follows the lutf, it becomes compulsory, contradicting the claim of voluntary choice. One cannot say it both occurs inevitably and is voluntary. Al-Ka'bi reports that Abu al-Husayn wrote about Ja'far's repentance. Finally, the passage notes a difference in definitions: the Baghdad school defines al-aslah as what is most beneficial in terms of wisdom and administration, while some Basrans define salah as benefit and al-aslah as that which is more beneficial.

Key Terms
لطف (grace)
أصلح (most beneficial)
صلاح (benefit)
اختيار (choice)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ja'far's initial view, why is it most beneficial for God to withhold the lutf from disbelievers?
A) Because they would not believe anyway.
B) Because believing with the lutf would earn less reward than believing without it.
C) Because the lutf is only for believers.
D) Because God does not want them to believe.

Key Takeaway
Ja'far's initial view integrates divine grace with voluntary belief and a reward-based understanding of al-aslah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes wish God would make faith easier for you, or are you willing to struggle for the greater reward that comes from belief without special grace?
?
When you face difficulties in your faith, can you see them as God's wisdom to elevate your reward, or do you resent the absence of divine ease?
?
Ja'far teaches that God withholds a grace to give you a higher station. What does that reveal about your own desire for comfort versus your desire for closeness to God?

Ja'far's Recantation: The Contradiction of Choice and Necessity
Presents the logical argument that led Ja'far to recant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ja'far ibn Harb recanted his earlier view, specifically the logical contradiction between claiming belief is both inevitable (necessary) and voluntary.

Core Idea
Necessity and voluntary choice cannot coexist—a core principle of Mu'tazilite rationalism.

Ja'far ibn Harb originally held that a divine grace could cause voluntary belief, but later he and his school deemed this impossible. The argument rests on a logical contradiction: if belief necessarily follows the grace, it becomes compulsory, not voluntary. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the recantation argument
Focus on the reasoning: if belief occurs inevitably upon the lutf, it is necessary; if it is not necessary, it might not occur. One cannot have both inevitability and choice.
The Arabic phrase 'لا محالة' indicates inevitability.
2
Identify the logical contradiction
The passage states that claiming 'it occurs inevitably' and then 'it is voluntary' is a contradiction (naqid) and combines choice and necessity, which is impossible (muhal).
This is a standard Mu'tazilite argument against views that mix compulsion and free will.
3
Understand the implication
Ja'far's recantation shows that internal consistency is paramount in kalam. The school maintained that God's grace cannot override human choice without making belief compulsory.
This argument preserves human accountability for faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ja'far recanted because the Mu'tazilite school holds that necessity and free choice are mutually exclusive.

Defining al-Aslah: Baghdad vs. Basra
Provides the definitions of al-aslah among Baghdad and Basra schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Baghdadi and Basran definitions of al-aslah within the Mu'tazilite school.

Core Idea
Baghdad defines al-aslah by wisdom; Basra defines it by benefit.

The passage concludes by noting a terminological divergence among Mu'tazilites: the Baghdad school defines al-aslah in terms of wisdom and administration, while some Basrans define it simply as 'more beneficial.' This subtle difference reflects broader methodological emphases within the school.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Notice the two definitions: al-aslah according to Baghdadis is 'what is most beneficial in terms of wisdom and administration'; according to some Basrans, 'salah is benefit and al-aslah is that which is more beneficial.'
The key terms are حكمة (wisdom) and تدبير (administration) vs. نفع (benefit).
2
Compare the definitions
The Baghdadis add a qualifier (in wisdom and administration) whereas the Basrans give a simpler equation: salah = benefit, al-aslah = more beneficial.
This might reflect different views on whether 'most beneficial' is determined by God's wisdom or by maximal benefit.
3
Consider the significance
The difference shows that even within a school, there can be variations in how key terms are defined, affecting theological arguments about God's actions.
This is a common feature in kalam: precise definitions matter.

Ja'far ibn Harb's Lutf Doctrine and Its Retraction
Reports Ja'far ibn Harb's view on lutf and retraction, with later Mu'tazili differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ja'far ibn Harb proposed a specific form of divine grace that could cause voluntary faith, and why he later retracted this view due to its logical inconsistency with free will and reward.

Core Idea
A grace that forces belief negates free will and reward.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla recounts a fascinating episode of theological debate within the Mu'tazilite school. Ja'far ibn Harb proposed that Allah possesses a special grace (lutf) that, if given, would cause disbelievers to believe voluntarily but with lesser reward. However, he later retracted this view after realizing its logical inconsistency with free choice. As you read, notice how the narrative weaves together a reported view, its critique, and an authoritative retraction, ending with a note on later school divisions.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ja'far's initial position
Read the passage identifying Ja'far's claim about a specific lutf that would cause faith voluntarily but with less reward, and why withholding it is considered al-aslah.
Note the distinction between voluntary faith and reward.
2
Follow the logical critique and retraction
Al-Ka'bi explains why this view leads to contradiction: if lutf inevitably causes faith, it becomes necessary, not voluntary. See how Ja'far retracts to avoid combining choice and necessity.
This is a key argument in Mu'tazili theology.
3
Note the later diversity on al-aslah
The passage ends by noting that Baghdadi Mu'tazila define al-aslah as wisdom-optimal, while some Basrians define it as maximal benefit. This shows ongoing internal discussion.
Compare with earlier usage in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشر قال : وذكر - يعني أبا الحسين - أنه بلغه عن أبي موسى المردار أنه كان يحكي التوبة عنه وكان جعفر بن حرب يقول : إن عند الله تعالى لطفا لو أعطاه الكافرين لأمنوا اختيارًا إيماناً لا يستحقون عليه من الثواب ما يستحقون به إذا آمنوا مع عدم ذلك اللطف، والأصلح لهم ما فعل بهم من تركه إعطائهم ذلك اللطف؛ لأن الله تعالى لا يعرض عباده إلا لأعلى المنازل وأشرفها وأفضل الثواب وأكثرها قال الكعبي : ثم ترك جعفر بن حرب هذا القول ورجع إلى قول أصحابه من أن ذلك محال؛ لأنه إذا كان الإيمان يقع منهم عند حدوث اللطف لا محالة فهو واقع ضرورة ولو لم يكن ضرورة جاز أن لا يقع ولا يوجد، إذا قال قائل: هو واقع لا محالة ثم قال: هو اختيار فقد ناقض وجمع بين الاختيار والضرورة وذلك محال، قال الكعبي: كتب إلي توبة جعفر من هذا القول أبو الحسين، والأمر في ذلك مشهور. ثم الأصلح عند البغداديين منهم ما هو الأصلح في الحكمة والتدبير، وعند بعض البصريين منهم الصلاح هو النفع والأصلح هو الأنفع.
Bishr said: And he mentioned – meaning Abu al-Husayn – that it reached him from Abu Musa al-Murdar that he used to relate repentance from him [i.e., Ja'far], and Ja'far ibn Harb used to say: Indeed, with Allah Almighty there is a grace (lutf) such that if He gave it to the disbelievers, they would believe willingly, a faith for which they would not deserve the reward that they would deserve if they believed without that grace, and the most beneficial (al-aslah) for them is what He did with them by leaving off giving them that grace; because Allah Almighty only exposes His servants to the highest stations and the most noble and the best reward and the most of it. Al-Ka'bi said: Then Ja'far ibn Harb abandoned this saying and returned to the saying of his companions that that is impossible; because if faith occurs from them when the grace occurs inevitably, then it occurs necessarily, and if it were not necessary, it would be possible that it does not occur and does not exist. If someone says: it occurs inevitably, then says: it is voluntary, he has contradicted and combined between choice and necessity, and that is impossible. Al-Ka'bi said: Abu al-Husayn wrote to me about Ja'far's repentance from this saying, and the matter regarding that is well-known. Then al-aslah according to the Baghdadiyūn among them is what is most beneficial in terms of wisdom and administration, and according to some of the Basriyyūn, benefit? (salāh) is benefit (naf') and the most beneficial (al-aslah) is the most beneficial? (al-anfa').
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage reports a narration concerning the Mu'tazili figure Ja'far ibn Harb and his view on divine grace (lutf) and the doctrine of al-aslah. The narrator, Bishr, says that Abu al-Husayn mentioned that he heard from Abu Musa al-Murdar that he used to relate that Ja'far ibn Harb repented from a certain view. Ja'far had held that Allah has a specific grace such that if He gave it to disbelievers, they would believe voluntarily, but they would not deserve the same reward as those who believe without that grace; moreover, withholding this grace is more beneficial for them because Allah only exposes His servants to the highest rewards. Al-Ka'bi then comments that Ja'far later abandoned this position and returned to the mainstream Mu'tazili view that such a lutf would make faith necessary and thus contradict free choice. The reasoning is that if grace inevitably causes faith, faith becomes necessary, not voluntary, and calling it voluntary after claiming inevitability is contradictory. Al-Ka'bi further confirms that Abu al-Husayn informed him of Ja'far's repentance. The passage ends by noting differing interpretations of al-aslah among later Mu'tazila: the Baghdadi school defined it as what is wisdom-optimal, while some Basrians defined it as the most beneficial in terms of utility.

أصلح
اختيار
اضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume the Mu'tazili view on lutf and al-aslah is uniform; this passage shows internal disagreement and evolution of thought.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that even within Mu'tazilism, theologians like Ja'far ibn Harb proposed alternative views that were later rejected, and definitions of key terms like al-aslah varied between schools.

Checkpoint
Why did Ja'far ibn Harb retract his initial view on lutf?
A) Because he discovered it contradicted the Qur'an.
B) Because it implied faith becomes necessary and thus not voluntary.
C) Because al-Ash'ari refuted him.
D) Because the caliph forced him to repent.

Key Takeaway
Ja'far ibn Harb's retraction illustrates the Mu'tazili commitment to preserving free will, as any grace that ensures belief undermines the voluntary nature of faith and thus the basis for reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ja'far ibn Harb once believed that a grace from Allah could make disbelievers believe inevitably yet still willingly. In your own heart, do you ever secretly wish for a sign so clear that you would have no choice but to believe? Would such a belief feel like your own, and would it carry the same weight with you?
?
Al-Ka'bi exposed the contradiction: if faith is inevitable, it cannot be voluntary. Where in your life right now are you treating a habit or obligation as a free choice when it is really a compulsion? Are you willing to confront that dishonesty?
?
Ja'far had to retract his view when he saw it contradicted reason. Is there a comfortable belief you hold that, upon honest examination, contradicts a core truth of Islam? What would it cost you to retract it, and are you willing to pay that price?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
God's Actions with Disbelievers Contradict al-Aslah
Al-Nasafi presents two concrete examples to refute al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's treatment of disbelievers and apostates provides a decisive refutation of the Mu'tazilite principle that God must always do what is best for His creatures.

Core Idea
God's keeping disbelievers alive with intellect disproves the Mu'tazilite claim of al-aslah.

The Mu'tazilites asserted that God is obligated to do what is most beneficial for every individual. This lesson presents the first part of al-Nasafi's argument: by examining God's actual actions with those who disbelieve and apostatize, we see that God does not always do what is best for them in worldly terms. Instead, His wisdom includes trials and tests that ultimately serve a greater purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic segment carefully, noting the two examples of the disbeliever and the apostate. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: God keeping a disbeliever alive despite knowing his disbelief, and God keeping an apostate alive despite knowing his apostasy. In both cases, an earlier death would have been better for them.
Notice how each case ends with the same conclusion: this indicates that God's action is not based on al-aslah but on wisdom.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If al-aslah were an obligation, then God would have done the better action. Since He does not, the Mu'tazilite claim is false.
This argument relies on the premise that if a better option exists, God would choose it if obligated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بها ولا ينتفعون فلم يكن إيجادها مصلحة لهم فضلاً عن الأصلح، وهذه المسألة في الحقيقة فرع لتلك المسألة، ثم ما هو أظهر من هذا وأدل أن الله تعالى فعل بالكافر ما لا صلاح له فيه بل له فيه مضرة ومفسدة، فإن الله تعالى أبقاه إلى وقت بلوغه وركب فيه العقل مع علمه أنه لا يؤمن بل يكفر ويعادي الله تعالى، ولا شك أن الله تعالى إذا علم أنه يكفر عند بلوغه واعتدال عقله لو أماته في حال صغره وعدم تمييزه أو لم يركب فيه العقل عند بلوغه حتى لو بلغ مجنونا غير مخاطب لكان ذلك أصلح له، وحيث لم يمته بل أبقاه وركب فيه العقل والتمييز حتى دخل في حد التكليف والامتحان مع علمه أنه يكفر دل أنه لم يفعل به ما له فيه صلاح، وكذا من عاش مدة على الإسلام ثم ارتد بعد ذلك - نعوذ بالله - ولو كان الله تعالى قبض روحه وتوفاه قبل ارتداده بساعة حتى ختم له بالإسلام ولم يستحق التعذيب في النار خالدًا مخلدًا كان أصلح له، وحيث لم يفعل بل أبقاه مع علمه أنه يرتد عن الإسلام وكان ذلك مضرة له لا صلاحًا فقد فعل ذلك وهو تعالى حكيم، دل أن ذلك كان حكمة، ووقعت المعتزلة فيما وقعت لجهلهم بحقيقة الحكمة، ثم بعد تقرير فعل الله تعالى ذلك دعوى من زعم أن ذلك سفه وليس بحكمة وصف منه الله تعالى بذلك وهو كفر، بل ظهر بفعله أنه حكمة وإن جهلت المعتزلة بجهة الحكمة إذ الجهل عليهم جائز وخروج فعل الله تعالى عن الحكمة ممتنع.
يحققه أن الله تعالى أضاف بقاءهم على الكفر إلى إبقائه وإمهاله بقوله تعالى: {إنما نملي لهم ليزدادوا إثما}. ثم من جهل المعتزلة أنهم يزعمون أن إبقاء الطفل إلى أن يبلغ ويتركب العقل فيه عند البلوغ مع علمه أنه يكفر أصلح له، وكذا إبقاء من يعلم الله تعالى أنه يرتد لا محالة أصلح له من الإماتة قبل وقت الارتداد لما أنه تعالى ركب فيه العقل وأمره بالإيمان وهداه السبيل وبين حسن الإيمان وقبح الكفر، فكان إبقاؤه إياه مع هذه المعاني تعريضا له لأعلى المنزلتين وأسناهما، ولإحراز زيادة الثواب والتعريض لذلك مصلحة له، ومن المعقول الذي لا ريب فيه أن هذا الكلام لو تكلم به مبرسم أو هذى به مخبل لتعجب منه السامعون وضحك منه الحاضرون، ثم يقال له: لو أن رجلاً دفع مالاً إلى ولد له وأمره أن يذهب إلى بلده كذا ويتجر، وكان الأمر بحال لو وصل الابن إلى تلك البلدة وأتجر بها لوقعت له تجارات رابحة وحصلت له أموال نفيسة جمة، غير أن الأب يعلم يقينا أن ابنه لا يصل إلى تلك البلدة بل يقطع عليه الطريق ويغار على ما دفعه إليه من الأموال ويجز رقبته، أو يعلم يقينا أن ابنه لا يتجر بتلك الأموال بل يقامر بها ينفقها في ثمن الشراب وأجرة الزواني والقحاب ثم مع علمه بهذا يدفع إليه الأموال ويأمره بالذهاب ويحرضه على التجارة وينهاه عما يصده عنها ، أيكون هذا الرجل بهذا الصنيع والتعريض للتجارة الرابحة فاعلاً بهذا الولد ما هو الأصلح ومريدا لصلاحه وصلاح ماله مع علمه بما يؤول إليه عاقبة أمره؟ فإن قال: كان بذلك فاعلاً به الأصلح ومريدًا لصلاحه. نادى على نفسه بالحمق والمكابرة بل بعدم العقل ويسخر به كل من سمع ذلك منه، وإذا آل الأمر إلى ارتكاب مثل هذا المحال كان الإعراض عن مجادلته أصوب وتصوير عقيدته لعامة الناس ليعرفوا عناده أو غباوته أوجب، وإن قال: لم يكن بهذا التعريض فاعلاً به الأصلح ولا مريدًا
By it they do not benefit, so its creation was not a benefit for them, let alone the optimal. And this matter is in reality a branch of that matter. Then what is more apparent than this and more indicative is that God Almighty did to the disbeliever that which has no benefit for him, but rather in it is harm and corruption. For God Almighty kept him alive until the time of his maturity and placed in him intellect, knowing that he will not believe but rather will disbelieve and enmity God Almighty. And there is no doubt that if God Almighty knew that he would disbelieve at his maturity and the balance of his intellect, if He had caused him to die in his childhood and lack of discernment, or had not placed intellect in him at his maturity such that he reached maturity insane and not addressed by obligations, that would have been better (aslah) for him. And since He did not cause him to die but kept him alive and placed in him intellect and discernment until he entered the realm of obligation and trial, knowing that he will disbelieve, this indicates that He did not do for him what contains benefit for him. Similarly, one who lived a period upon Islam then apostatized after that—we seek refuge in God—and if God Almighty had taken his soul and caused him to die an hour before his apostasy so that he would be sealed with Islam and not deserve eternal punishment in the Fire, that would have been better for him. And since He did not do that but kept him alive knowing that he will apostatize from Islam, and that was harmful for him and not beneficial, thus He did that while He is Wise; this indicates that that was wisdom. And the Mu'tazilites fell into what they fell into due to their ignorance of the true nature of wisdom. Then, after establishing God's doing of that, the claim of one who claims that this is foolishness and not wisdom, and describes God Almighty thereby, is unbelief. Rather, it became apparent by His action that it is wisdom, even if the Mu'tazilites are ignorant of the aspect of wisdom, for ignorance of it is possible for them, but the departure of God's action from wisdom is impossible.
What confirms it is that God Almighty attributed their persistence in disbelief to His keeping them alive and giving them respite in His statement: 'We only give them respite that they may increase in sin' (Quran 3:178). Then, from the ignorance of the Mu'tazilites is that they claim that keeping the child alive until he reaches maturity and placing intellect in him at maturity, knowing that he will disbelieve, is better for him. Likewise, keeping alive one whom God Almighty knows will inevitably apostatize is better for him than causing him to die before the time of apostasy, because He placed in him intellect, commanded him to believe, guided him to the path, explained the goodness of faith and the ugliness of disbelief. So keeping him along with these meanings is an exposition to him for the highest of the two stations and the noblest, and for obtaining increase of reward, and the exposition to that is a benefit for him. And from the rational, of which there is no doubt, is that if this speech were uttered by a delirious person or mumbled by a madman, the listeners would be amazed and the attendees would laugh at him. Then it is said to him: Suppose a man gave money to his son and commanded him to go to such-and-such a town and trade, and the matter was such that if the son reached that town and traded there, profitable trades would occur and great precious wealth would be obtained for him, except that the father knows for certain that his son will not reach that town but rather will be intercepted on the road, robbed of the money given to him, and have his throat cut; or he knows for certain that his son will not trade with that money but rather will gamble it away, spending it on the price of wine and the wages of prostitutes and harlots; then, with his knowledge of this, he gives him the money, commands him to go, urges him to trade, and forbids him from what would turn him away from it. Is this man, by this action and exposition to profitable trade, doing what is optimal for this son and desiring his benefit and the benefit of his wealth, while knowing what the ultimate outcome of his affair will be? If he says: 'He was thereby doing the optimal for him and desiring his benefit,' then he proclaims his own foolishness and obstinacy, rather his lack of intellect, and everyone who hears that from him will mock him. And when the matter reaches the point of committing such an impossibility, then turning away from disputing him is more correct, and depicting his creed to the common people so that they may know his stubbornness or his stupidity is more obligatory. And if he says: 'He was not by this exposition doing the optimal for him nor desiring [his benefit]'
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the optimal), which holds that God must always do what is best for His creatures. The author argues from God's actual dealings with disbelievers.

The argument proceeds in several steps:
1. God creates the disbeliever, keeps him alive, and gives him intellect, even though He knows the disbeliever will use it to disbelieve and oppose God. If God had let him die in childhood or made him insane, that would have been better for him (aslah). Since God does not do so, this contradicts the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do the optimal.
2. Similarly, a Muslim who later apostatizes: if God had caused him to die before apostasy, that would have been better for him. But God keeps him alive, knowing he will apostatize, leading to harm. This again shows God's actions do not conform to the principle of al-aslah.
3. The author then asserts that this demonstrates true wisdom (hikma), which the Mu'tazilites fail to understand due to ignorance. To claim otherwise (that God's action is foolish) would be unbelief.
4. He supports this with the Quranic verse: 'We only give them respite that they may increase in sin' (3:178), indicating that God intentionally prolongs their disbelief.
5. Then he addresses a potential Mu'tazilite counterargument: they claim that giving the child intellect and guidance is a benefit—an exposition (ta'rid) to the highest rank (faith). The author dismisses this with a reductio ad absurdum analogy.
6. The analogy: A father sends his son on a business trip, knowing the son will be robbed or waste the money. If the father does this thinking it is optimal, he is foolish. If he admits it is not optimal, then the Mu'tazilite position collapses. The author argues that the Mu'tazilite claim about God is similarly absurd.
7. The conclusion (though cut off in the text) would be that the Mu'tazilite must either admit absurdity or concede that God is not bound by al-aslah.

The passage uses both rational argument and scriptural evidence to refute the Mu'tazilite view, emphasizing God's absolute wisdom which may appear contrary to human notions of benefit.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الإبقاء
الإماتة
البلوغ
التكليف
الارتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God must always do what is best for each person in this world.
Correction: The passage shows that God's wisdom sometimes involves allowing harm for a greater purpose, contradicting the Mu'tazilite view.

Checkpoint
What do the two examples in this passage demonstrate?
A) That God always does what is best for everyone.
B) That God's actions contradict the principle of al-aslah.
C) That the Mu'tazilites are correct.
D) That disbelievers deserve punishment.

Key Takeaway
God's treatment of disbelievers and apostates shows that He is not bound to do what is most beneficial for them in worldly terms; His wisdom transcends human notions of benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God could have caused you to die before you ever reached accountability or before you committed any sin. Instead, He gave you intellect and kept you alive. Does that make you fear how you are using that intellect? What will you change today to ensure you are not among those who disbelieve despite having intellect?
?
The passage describes a Muslim who later apostatizes. Is there any area of your faith where you feel uncertain or weak? If God knows you would turn away, why has He kept you alive? What commitment can you make right now to strengthen your iman?
?
You have likely experienced situations that seemed harmful or unfair. Do you believe that God's wisdom may be at work even when it doesn't seem 'best' from your perspective? Will you surrender your own judgment of what is beneficial and trust His wisdom in that specific difficulty you are facing?

The Wisdom Behind God's Actions and the Ignorance of the Mu'tazilites
Al-Nasafi discusses wisdom and presents the Mu'tazilite counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi asserts that the Mu'tazilite position stems from ignorance of the true nature of wisdom, and how the Quranic verse supports his argument.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites' ignorance of wisdom leads them to falsely accuse God of foolishness.

After presenting the factual counterexamples, al-Nasafi now addresses the underlying issue: the Mu'tazilites' misunderstanding of wisdom. He asserts that to claim God's actions are foolish is itself unbelief, and he supports his argument with a Quranic verse. He then presents a potential Mu'tazilite objection: that giving intellect and guidance is itself a benefit. This lesson unpacks these points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic segment which begins with the fall of the Mu'tazilites and includes the Quranic quote and the Mu'tazilite counterargument.
2
Analyze the claim about wisdom
Notice how al-Nasafi states that calling God's action foolish is unbelief. He then uses the Quranic verse to show that God intentionally prolongs disbelief.
The verse 'We only give them respite that they may increase in sin' (3:178) directly contradicts the idea that God is doing what is best for them.
3
Examine the Mu'tazilite counterclaim
The Mu'tazilites argue that keeping the child alive and giving him intellect is a benefit because it exposes him to the highest rank (faith). Al-Nasafi later refutes this with the analogy, but here he presents their argument.
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بها ولا ينتفعون فلم يكن إيجادها مصلحة لهم فضلاً عن الأصلح، وهذه المسألة في الحقيقة فرع لتلك المسألة، ثم ما هو أظهر من هذا وأدل أن الله تعالى فعل بالكافر ما لا صلاح له فيه بل له فيه مضرة ومفسدة، فإن الله تعالى أبقاه إلى وقت بلوغه وركب فيه العقل مع علمه أنه لا يؤمن بل يكفر ويعادي الله تعالى، ولا شك أن الله تعالى إذا علم أنه يكفر عند بلوغه واعتدال عقله لو أماته في حال صغره وعدم تمييزه أو لم يركب فيه العقل عند بلوغه حتى لو بلغ مجنونا غير مخاطب لكان ذلك أصلح له، وحيث لم يمته بل أبقاه وركب فيه العقل والتمييز حتى دخل في حد التكليف والامتحان مع علمه أنه يكفر دل أنه لم يفعل به ما له فيه صلاح، وكذا من عاش مدة على الإسلام ثم ارتد بعد ذلك - نعوذ بالله - ولو كان الله تعالى قبض روحه وتوفاه قبل ارتداده بساعة حتى ختم له بالإسلام ولم يستحق التعذيب في النار خالدًا مخلدًا كان أصلح له، وحيث لم يفعل بل أبقاه مع علمه أنه يرتد عن الإسلام وكان ذلك مضرة له لا صلاحًا فقد فعل ذلك وهو تعالى حكيم، دل أن ذلك كان حكمة، ووقعت المعتزلة فيما وقعت لجهلهم بحقيقة الحكمة، ثم بعد تقرير فعل الله تعالى ذلك دعوى من زعم أن ذلك سفه وليس بحكمة وصف منه الله تعالى بذلك وهو كفر، بل ظهر بفعله أنه حكمة وإن جهلت المعتزلة بجهة الحكمة إذ الجهل عليهم جائز وخروج فعل الله تعالى عن الحكمة ممتنع.
يحققه أن الله تعالى أضاف بقاءهم على الكفر إلى إبقائه وإمهاله بقوله تعالى: {إنما نملي لهم ليزدادوا إثما}. ثم من جهل المعتزلة أنهم يزعمون أن إبقاء الطفل إلى أن يبلغ ويتركب العقل فيه عند البلوغ مع علمه أنه يكفر أصلح له، وكذا إبقاء من يعلم الله تعالى أنه يرتد لا محالة أصلح له من الإماتة قبل وقت الارتداد لما أنه تعالى ركب فيه العقل وأمره بالإيمان وهداه السبيل وبين حسن الإيمان وقبح الكفر، فكان إبقاؤه إياه مع هذه المعاني تعريضا له لأعلى المنزلتين وأسناهما، ولإحراز زيادة الثواب والتعريض لذلك مصلحة له، ومن المعقول الذي لا ريب فيه أن هذا الكلام لو تكلم به مبرسم أو هذى به مخبل لتعجب منه السامعون وضحك منه الحاضرون، ثم يقال له: لو أن رجلاً دفع مالاً إلى ولد له وأمره أن يذهب إلى بلده كذا ويتجر، وكان الأمر بحال لو وصل الابن إلى تلك البلدة وأتجر بها لوقعت له تجارات رابحة وحصلت له أموال نفيسة جمة، غير أن الأب يعلم يقينا أن ابنه لا يصل إلى تلك البلدة بل يقطع عليه الطريق ويغار على ما دفعه إليه من الأموال ويجز رقبته، أو يعلم يقينا أن ابنه لا يتجر بتلك الأموال بل يقامر بها ينفقها في ثمن الشراب وأجرة الزواني والقحاب ثم مع علمه بهذا يدفع إليه الأموال ويأمره بالذهاب ويحرضه على التجارة وينهاه عما يصده عنها ، أيكون هذا الرجل بهذا الصنيع والتعريض للتجارة الرابحة فاعلاً بهذا الولد ما هو الأصلح ومريدا لصلاحه وصلاح ماله مع علمه بما يؤول إليه عاقبة أمره؟ فإن قال: كان بذلك فاعلاً به الأصلح ومريدًا لصلاحه. نادى على نفسه بالحمق والمكابرة بل بعدم العقل ويسخر به كل من سمع ذلك منه، وإذا آل الأمر إلى ارتكاب مثل هذا المحال كان الإعراض عن مجادلته أصوب وتصوير عقيدته لعامة الناس ليعرفوا عناده أو غباوته أوجب، وإن قال: لم يكن بهذا التعريض فاعلاً به الأصلح ولا مريدًا
By it they do not benefit, so its creation was not a benefit for them, let alone the optimal. And this matter is in reality a branch of that matter. Then what is more apparent than this and more indicative is that God Almighty did to the disbeliever that which has no benefit for him, but rather in it is harm and corruption. For God Almighty kept him alive until the time of his maturity and placed in him intellect, knowing that he will not believe but rather will disbelieve and enmity God Almighty. And there is no doubt that if God Almighty knew that he would disbelieve at his maturity and the balance of his intellect, if He had caused him to die in his childhood and lack of discernment, or had not placed intellect in him at his maturity such that he reached maturity insane and not addressed by obligations, that would have been better (aslah) for him. And since He did not cause him to die but kept him alive and placed in him intellect and discernment until he entered the realm of obligation and trial, knowing that he will disbelieve, this indicates that He did not do for him what contains benefit for him. Similarly, one who lived a period upon Islam then apostatized after that—we seek refuge in God—and if God Almighty had taken his soul and caused him to die an hour before his apostasy so that he would be sealed with Islam and not deserve eternal punishment in the Fire, that would have been better for him. And since He did not do that but kept him alive knowing that he will apostatize from Islam, and that was harmful for him and not beneficial, thus He did that while He is Wise; this indicates that that was wisdom. And the Mu'tazilites fell into what they fell into due to their ignorance of the true nature of wisdom. Then, after establishing God's doing of that, the claim of one who claims that this is foolishness and not wisdom, and describes God Almighty thereby, is unbelief. Rather, it became apparent by His action that it is wisdom, even if the Mu'tazilites are ignorant of the aspect of wisdom, for ignorance of it is possible for them, but the departure of God's action from wisdom is impossible.
What confirms it is that God Almighty attributed their persistence in disbelief to His keeping them alive and giving them respite in His statement: 'We only give them respite that they may increase in sin' (Quran 3:178). Then, from the ignorance of the Mu'tazilites is that they claim that keeping the child alive until he reaches maturity and placing intellect in him at maturity, knowing that he will disbelieve, is better for him. Likewise, keeping alive one whom God Almighty knows will inevitably apostatize is better for him than causing him to die before the time of apostasy, because He placed in him intellect, commanded him to believe, guided him to the path, explained the goodness of faith and the ugliness of disbelief. So keeping him along with these meanings is an exposition to him for the highest of the two stations and the noblest, and for obtaining increase of reward, and the exposition to that is a benefit for him. And from the rational, of which there is no doubt, is that if this speech were uttered by a delirious person or mumbled by a madman, the listeners would be amazed and the attendees would laugh at him. Then it is said to him: Suppose a man gave money to his son and commanded him to go to such-and-such a town and trade, and the matter was such that if the son reached that town and traded there, profitable trades would occur and great precious wealth would be obtained for him, except that the father knows for certain that his son will not reach that town but rather will be intercepted on the road, robbed of the money given to him, and have his throat cut; or he knows for certain that his son will not trade with that money but rather will gamble it away, spending it on the price of wine and the wages of prostitutes and harlots; then, with his knowledge of this, he gives him the money, commands him to go, urges him to trade, and forbids him from what would turn him away from it. Is this man, by this action and exposition to profitable trade, doing what is optimal for this son and desiring his benefit and the benefit of his wealth, while knowing what the ultimate outcome of his affair will be? If he says: 'He was thereby doing the optimal for him and desiring his benefit,' then he proclaims his own foolishness and obstinacy, rather his lack of intellect, and everyone who hears that from him will mock him. And when the matter reaches the point of committing such an impossibility, then turning away from disputing him is more correct, and depicting his creed to the common people so that they may know his stubbornness or his stupidity is more obligatory. And if he says: 'He was not by this exposition doing the optimal for him nor desiring [his benefit]'
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the optimal), which holds that God must always do what is best for His creatures. The author argues from God's actual dealings with disbelievers.

The argument proceeds in several steps:
1. God creates the disbeliever, keeps him alive, and gives him intellect, even though He knows the disbeliever will use it to disbelieve and oppose God. If God had let him die in childhood or made him insane, that would have been better for him (aslah). Since God does not do so, this contradicts the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do the optimal.
2. Similarly, a Muslim who later apostatizes: if God had caused him to die before apostasy, that would have been better for him. But God keeps him alive, knowing he will apostatize, leading to harm. This again shows God's actions do not conform to the principle of al-aslah.
3. The author then asserts that this demonstrates true wisdom (hikma), which the Mu'tazilites fail to understand due to ignorance. To claim otherwise (that God's action is foolish) would be unbelief.
4. He supports this with the Quranic verse: 'We only give them respite that they may increase in sin' (3:178), indicating that God intentionally prolongs their disbelief.
5. Then he addresses a potential Mu'tazilite counterargument: they claim that giving the child intellect and guidance is a benefit—an exposition (ta'rid) to the highest rank (faith). The author dismisses this with a reductio ad absurdum analogy.
6. The analogy: A father sends his son on a business trip, knowing the son will be robbed or waste the money. If the father does this thinking it is optimal, he is foolish. If he admits it is not optimal, then the Mu'tazilite position collapses. The author argues that the Mu'tazilite claim about God is similarly absurd.
7. The conclusion (though cut off in the text) would be that the Mu'tazilite must either admit absurdity or concede that God is not bound by al-aslah.

The passage uses both rational argument and scriptural evidence to refute the Mu'tazilite view, emphasizing God's absolute wisdom which may appear contrary to human notions of benefit.

Key Terms
الحكمة
المعتزلة
الإمهال
التعريض
المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's not doing the optimal means His actions are foolish.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that true wisdom may involve allowing harm for a greater purpose, and to call it foolish is unbelief.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the consequence of claiming that God's actions are foolish?
A) It is a minor error.
B) It is unbelief.
C) It is allowed if based on reason.
D) It shows wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Calling God's actions foolish is unbelief; true wisdom may involve allowing harm for a greater purpose, as shown by the Quranic verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or see a wrongdoer prosper, do you silently blame God or consider His action foolish? Repent and affirm that His action is wisdom, even if hidden from you.
?
Are you willing to trust that God's keeping you alive in a state of sin is not a mistake but a deliberate exposition for your benefit? How will you respond differently today?
?
Have you ever said or thought that something God did or allowed was pointless? That is the same attitude that the author calls unbelief. Confess it and seek forgiveness.

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
The Father-Son Analogy: A Reductio ad Absurdum of al-Aslah
Al-Nasafi uses an analogy to refute the Mu'tazilite counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of a father knowingly sending his son to ruin refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that exposition to faith is a benefit.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite defense of al-aslah leads to absurdity, as shown by a simple analogy.

The Mu'tazilites argued that even if a person disbelieves, giving them intellect and guidance is a benefit because it exposes them to the possibility of faith and reward. Al-Nasafi dismantles this claim with a powerful analogy: a father who sends his son on a dangerous journey, knowing disaster awaits, cannot be said to be acting in the son's best interest. This lesson works through that analogy and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Read the father-son analogy carefully. The father gives money and commands trade, but knows the son will be robbed or waste the money.
The analogy mirrors the Mu'tazilite scenario: God gives intellect and guidance knowing the person will disbelieve.
2
Understand the dilemma
Al-Nasafi presents a dilemma: if the father claims he is doing what is optimal, he is foolish. If he admits it is not optimal, the Mu'tazilite position collapses.
The analogy is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Apply to God
Transfer the logic to God: if the Mu'tazilite claims God is doing the optimal, then God would be foolish. Since that is impossible, the Mu'tazilite position is false.
The conclusion is cut off but the implication is clear: the Mu'tazilite must either accept absurdity or abandon al-aslah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يحققه أن الله تعالى أضاف بقاءهم على الكفر إلى إبقائه وإمهاله بقوله تعالى: {إنما نملي لهم ليزدادوا إثما}. ثم من جهل المعتزلة أنهم يزعمون أن إبقاء الطفل إلى أن يبلغ ويتركب العقل فيه عند البلوغ مع علمه أنه يكفر أصلح له، وكذا إبقاء من يعلم الله تعالى أنه يرتد لا محالة أصلح له من الإماتة قبل وقت الارتداد لما أنه تعالى ركب فيه العقل وأمره بالإيمان وهداه السبيل وبين حسن الإيمان وقبح الكفر، فكان إبقاؤه إياه مع هذه المعاني تعريضا له لأعلى المنزلتين وأسناهما، ولإحراز زيادة الثواب والتعريض لذلك مصلحة له، ومن المعقول الذي لا ريب فيه أن هذا الكلام لو تكلم به مبرسم أو هذى به مخبل لتعجب منه السامعون وضحك منه الحاضرون، ثم يقال له: لو أن رجلاً دفع مالاً إلى ولد له وأمره أن يذهب إلى بلده كذا ويتجر، وكان الأمر بحال لو وصل الابن إلى تلك البلدة وأتجر بها لوقعت له تجارات رابحة وحصلت له أموال نفيسة جمة، غير أن الأب يعلم يقينا أن ابنه لا يصل إلى تلك البلدة بل يقطع عليه الطريق ويغار على ما دفعه إليه من الأموال ويجز رقبته، أو يعلم يقينا أن ابنه لا يتجر بتلك الأموال بل يقامر بها ينفقها في ثمن الشراب وأجرة الزواني والقحاب ثم مع علمه بهذا يدفع إليه الأموال ويأمره بالذهاب ويحرضه على التجارة وينهاه عما يصده عنها ، أيكون هذا الرجل بهذا الصنيع والتعريض للتجارة الرابحة فاعلاً بهذا الولد ما هو الأصلح ومريدا لصلاحه وصلاح ماله مع علمه بما يؤول إليه عاقبة أمره؟ فإن قال: كان بذلك فاعلاً به الأصلح ومريدًا لصلاحه. نادى على نفسه بالحمق والمكابرة بل بعدم العقل ويسخر به كل من سمع ذلك منه، وإذا آل الأمر إلى ارتكاب مثل هذا المحال كان الإعراض عن مجادلته أصوب وتصوير عقيدته لعامة الناس ليعرفوا عناده أو غباوته أوجب، وإن قال: لم يكن بهذا التعريض فاعلاً به الأصلح ولا مريدًا
By it they do not benefit, so its creation was not a benefit for them, let alone the optimal. And this matter is in reality a branch of that matter. Then what is more apparent than this and more indicative is that God Almighty did to the disbeliever that which has no benefit for him, but rather in it is harm and corruption. For God Almighty kept him alive until the time of his maturity and placed in him intellect, knowing that he will not believe but rather will disbelieve and enmity God Almighty. And there is no doubt that if God Almighty knew that he would disbelieve at his maturity and the balance of his intellect, if He had caused him to die in his childhood and lack of discernment, or had not placed intellect in him at his maturity such that he reached maturity insane and not addressed by obligations, that would have been better (aslah) for him. And since He did not cause him to die but kept him alive and placed in him intellect and discernment until he entered the realm of obligation and trial, knowing that he will disbelieve, this indicates that He did not do for him what contains benefit for him. Similarly, one who lived a period upon Islam then apostatized after that—we seek refuge in God—and if God Almighty had taken his soul and caused him to die an hour before his apostasy so that he would be sealed with Islam and not deserve eternal punishment in the Fire, that would have been better for him. And since He did not do that but kept him alive knowing that he will apostatize from Islam, and that was harmful for him and not beneficial, thus He did that while He is Wise; this indicates that that was wisdom. And the Mu'tazilites fell into what they fell into due to their ignorance of the true nature of wisdom. Then, after establishing God's doing of that, the claim of one who claims that this is foolishness and not wisdom, and describes God Almighty thereby, is unbelief. Rather, it became apparent by His action that it is wisdom, even if the Mu'tazilites are ignorant of the aspect of wisdom, for ignorance of it is possible for them, but the departure of God's action from wisdom is impossible.
What confirms it is that God Almighty attributed their persistence in disbelief to His keeping them alive and giving them respite in His statement: 'We only give them respite that they may increase in sin' (Quran 3:178). Then, from the ignorance of the Mu'tazilites is that they claim that keeping the child alive until he reaches maturity and placing intellect in him at maturity, knowing that he will disbelieve, is better for him. Likewise, keeping alive one whom God Almighty knows will inevitably apostatize is better for him than causing him to die before the time of apostasy, because He placed in him intellect, commanded him to believe, guided him to the path, explained the goodness of faith and the ugliness of disbelief. So keeping him along with these meanings is an exposition to him for the highest of the two stations and the noblest, and for obtaining increase of reward, and the exposition to that is a benefit for him. And from the rational, of which there is no doubt, is that if this speech were uttered by a delirious person or mumbled by a madman, the listeners would be amazed and the attendees would laugh at him. Then it is said to him: Suppose a man gave money to his son and commanded him to go to such-and-such a town and trade, and the matter was such that if the son reached that town and traded there, profitable trades would occur and great precious wealth would be obtained for him, except that the father knows for certain that his son will not reach that town but rather will be intercepted on the road, robbed of the money given to him, and have his throat cut; or he knows for certain that his son will not trade with that money but rather will gamble it away, spending it on the price of wine and the wages of prostitutes and harlots; then, with his knowledge of this, he gives him the money, commands him to go, urges him to trade, and forbids him from what would turn him away from it. Is this man, by this action and exposition to profitable trade, doing what is optimal for this son and desiring his benefit and the benefit of his wealth, while knowing what the ultimate outcome of his affair will be? If he says: 'He was thereby doing the optimal for him and desiring his benefit,' then he proclaims his own foolishness and obstinacy, rather his lack of intellect, and everyone who hears that from him will mock him. And when the matter reaches the point of committing such an impossibility, then turning away from disputing him is more correct, and depicting his creed to the common people so that they may know his stubbornness or his stupidity is more obligatory. And if he says: 'He was not by this exposition doing the optimal for him nor desiring [his benefit]'
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the optimal), which holds that God must always do what is best for His creatures. The author argues from God's actual dealings with disbelievers.

The argument proceeds in several steps:
1. God creates the disbeliever, keeps him alive, and gives him intellect, even though He knows the disbeliever will use it to disbelieve and oppose God. If God had let him die in childhood or made him insane, that would have been better for him (aslah). Since God does not do so, this contradicts the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do the optimal.
2. Similarly, a Muslim who later apostatizes: if God had caused him to die before apostasy, that would have been better for him. But God keeps him alive, knowing he will apostatize, leading to harm. This again shows God's actions do not conform to the principle of al-aslah.
3. The author then asserts that this demonstrates true wisdom (hikma), which the Mu'tazilites fail to understand due to ignorance. To claim otherwise (that God's action is foolish) would be unbelief.
4. He supports this with the Quranic verse: 'We only give them respite that they may increase in sin' (3:178), indicating that God intentionally prolongs their disbelief.
5. Then he addresses a potential Mu'tazilite counterargument: they claim that giving the child intellect and guidance is a benefit—an exposition (ta'rid) to the highest rank (faith). The author dismisses this with a reductio ad absurdum analogy.
6. The analogy: A father sends his son on a business trip, knowing the son will be robbed or waste the money. If the father does this thinking it is optimal, he is foolish. If he admits it is not optimal, then the Mu'tazilite position collapses. The author argues that the Mu'tazilite claim about God is similarly absurd.
7. The conclusion (though cut off in the text) would be that the Mu'tazilite must either admit absurdity or concede that God is not bound by al-aslah.

The passage uses both rational argument and scriptural evidence to refute the Mu'tazilite view, emphasizing God's absolute wisdom which may appear contrary to human notions of benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that giving a person free will and guidance is always beneficial even if they misuse it.
Correction: The analogy shows that if the outcome is known to be disastrous, the action cannot be called beneficial; rather, it is harmful.

Checkpoint
What does the father-son analogy demonstrate?
A) That fathers should not send sons on dangerous journeys.
B) That the Mu'tazilite claim of al-aslah leads to absurd conclusions.
C) That God is like a human father.
D) That trading is risky.

Key Takeaway
The father-son analogy reveals that the Mu'tazilite defense of al-aslah is rationally untenable; God's wisdom transcends human calculations of benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted that a certain outcome was 'best' for someone, only to realize later that your knowledge was incomplete? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to judge what is optimal from a limited perspective?
?
The father in the analogy knows his son will fail, but still sends him. Are there areas in your life where you know something will lead to harm, yet you continue—excusing it as 'for the best'? What would it cost you to stop rationalizing and admit the truth?
?
If you truly believed that God's actions are based on a wisdom beyond your own calculations, which area of your life would you surrender to Him today? What fear holds you back from that trust?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Causing a Child's Death
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God kills children to prevent future disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes the logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazila argument about God killing a child to prevent future disbelief.

Core Idea
Killing a child to prevent future evil creates an unsolvable dilemma for the Mu'tazila.

The Mu'tazila argued that God may cause a child to die young because He knows the child would become a disbeliever and mislead others. In this passage, Nasafi systematically dismantles their reasoning by applying their own principles of al-aslah (the most beneficial) and wisdom (ḥikma). He shows that their position leads to contradictions and even implicit blasphemy regarding God's knowledge. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Begin by reading the Mu'tazila argument as quoted by Nasafi: that God kills a child to prevent future disbelief and harm to others.
Notice that this is a hypothetical scenario meant to justify God's actions in terms of benefit.
2
Identify Nasafi's first counter
Nasafi points out that this action prevents what is more beneficial for the child (the opportunity for highest station) and that this is injustice according to Mu'tazila principles.
Focus on the concept of 'ta'rīḍ li-a'lā al-manzilatayn' (exposure to the highest station).
3
Analyze the dilemma
The passage presents a dilemma: if the child dies, it benefits others but harms the child; if the child lives, it benefits the child but harms others. Either way there is corruption and loss of benefit.
This shows the inescapable trade-off the Mu'tazila cannot resolve.
4
Examine the historical examples
Nasafi asks about notorious disbelievers like Pharaoh, Mani, etc. If Mu'tazila say God knew they would disbelieve, why didn't He kill them for the benefit of others? If they deny God's knowledge, they commit kufr.
This punchline forces the Mu'tazila into a contradiction.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how Nasafi uses the Mu'tazila's own criteria (al-aslah, wisdom) to show that their argument is self-defeating.
This is a classic example of internal critique (ilzām).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعضهم على هذا وقال : إنه أمات الصغير في حال صغره (لأنه علم أنه) لو بلغ لكفر وأضل غيره وأكفره فأماته في حال صغره لما فيه من مصلحة الغير. قلنا هذا منكم إقرار أنه منع ما هو الأصح للصغير وهو التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين الذي أمهل ذلك البالغ لأجله، إلا أنه منع هذا الأصلح لما فيه من صلاح ذلك الغير، وعندكم منع النفع عن لا جناية له لإصلاح غيره ظلم على هذا. :يحققه أن عندكم إنما يجب على الله تعالى بذل الأصلح إذا كان بذله لا يخرجه من استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة، وإماتة هذا الصغير لما فيه من صلاح ذلك الغير منع لمصلحة التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين (في حق هذا الصغير، وهذا يخرجه عن استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة فينبغي أن لا يفوت على هذا الصغير مصلحة التعريض لأعلى (المنزلتين) لمكان مصلحة الصغير، ومن الحاصل أنتم بين طرفي نقيض فإنكم إن قلتم : إماتته في الصغر مصلحة لذلك الكبير فهو مفسدة ولو لم يمته حتى بلغ فله فيه مصلحة التعريض ومفسدة لذلك الغير، فلم يخل كيف ما كان عن مفسدة وترك مصلحة ، ثم هل كان الله تعالى علم أن ماني وزرادشت ومزدك ومسيلمة والمقنع وبابك وقبل ذلك فرعون لو بلغوا لكفروا وأكفروا الناس؟ فإن قالوا : لا . كفروا لتجهيلهم ربهم، وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا: فلم أمهلهم الله تعالى وقد علم أنهم يكفرون ويضلون الناس وهلا أماتهم مصلحة للناس، وهذا مما لا اعتراض لهم عليه
Some of them said regarding this: He (God) caused the small child to die in his childhood because He knew that if he reached maturity he would disbelieve and mislead others and cause them to disbelieve, so He caused him to die in his childhood due to the benefit of others. We said: This is from you an acknowledgement that He prevented what is more beneficial for the small child, which is exposure to the highest of the two stations for which that adult was granted respite. However, He prevented this more beneficial due to the benefit of that other. And according to you, preventing benefit from one who has no crime in order to reform another is an injustice. This is verified: that according to you, it is only obligatory upon God Almighty to provide the most beneficial if providing it does not take Him out of deserving the attribute of wisdom. And causing this small child to die for the benefit of that other is a prevention of the benefit of exposure to the highest of the two stations in the right of this small child, and this takes Him out of deserving the attribute of wisdom. So it ought not that this small child be deprived of the benefit of exposure to the highest of the two stations for the sake of the benefit of the small child. The result is that you are between two opposites: if you say his death in childhood is a benefit for that adult, it is a corruption; and if he had not died until maturity, he would have the benefit of exposure and corruption for that other. So it is not free, however it is, from corruption and abandonment of benefit. Then, did God Almighty know that Mani, Zoroaster, Mazdak, Musaylima, al-Muqanna', Babak, and before that Pharaoh, if they reached maturity, would disbelieve and cause the people to disbelieve? If they say 'No,' they disbelieve for ignorantly alleging their Lord. If they say 'Yes,' we say: Why did God grant them respite when He knew they would disbelieve and mislead people? Why did He not cause them to die as a benefit for the people? And this is something they have no objection to.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific Mu'tazila argument. They claim that God may cause a child to die young because He knows the child would disbelieve and mislead others, so killing the child prevents future evil. Nasafi responds by analyzing this claim according to Mu'tazila principles. First, he argues that this action prevents what is more beneficial for the child: the opportunity to reach the highest station of faith and good deeds. God's prevention of this for the benefit of another is considered injustice in their own system, because the child has no sin. Nasafi then shows that if providing the most beneficial is obligatory unless it conflicts with wisdom, then depriving the child of that highest station contradicts wisdom. He further points out a dilemma: if the child dies, it benefits the adult but harms the child; if the child lives, it benefits the child but harms the adult. Either way there is corruption and loss. Finally, he uses historical examples: if Mu'tazila deny that God knew the disbelievers would disbelieve, they insult God; if they affirm it, they must explain why God allowed them to live and cause harm, which they cannot consistently answer.

إضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God's actions must always maximize immediate benefit for others, even at the expense of an innocent individual.
Correction: Nasafi shows that such an action would violate the Mu'tazila's own criteria of al-aṣlaḥ and wisdom, and leads to an unresolvable dilemma.

Checkpoint
What is the main contradiction Nasafi points out in the Mu'tazila argument about the death of the child?
A) That it benefits the adult but harms the child
B) That God's knowledge is incomplete
C) That the child is punished without sin
D) That Mu'tazila deny God's wisdom

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their defense of killing a child leads to a dilemma that violates their own principles of al-aṣlaḥ and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God allowed Pharaoh and other tyrants to live despite knowing their evil, do you truly trust that His wisdom is higher than your sense of justice? Or do you secretly wish He would have removed them to make the world 'better'? What does that reveal about your view of God's sovereignty?
?
If you were the child in the Mu'tazila scenario—deprived of the chance to reach the highest station for the sake of others—would you accept God's decree without resentment? What would it take for you to surrender your own perceived 'right' to a fair chance?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God must always choose what seems most beneficial to us. Have you ever demanded that God explain a tragedy in your life—a death, a missed opportunity—as if He owed you an account? How can you train your heart to say 'God knows what I do not know' even when it hurts?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Claim of al-Aslah in All Divine Acts
Nasafi rebuts the Mu'tazila claim that all divine acts are al-aslah even if incomprehensible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila argument that everything God does is necessarily the most beneficial for His servants.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is not bound by human understanding of benefit.

The Mu'tazila, faced with the problem of evil and suffering, retreated to the claim that everything God does is al-ashlah (the most beneficial), even if we cannot perceive the benefit. In this passage, Imam Nasafi exposes the inconsistency of this position by turning their own reasoning against them. He demonstrates that if we accept incomprehensible benefit, we must also accept that creating disbelief could be for the servant's benefit, leading to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila assert that all divine acts are al-ashlah, even if humans don't grasp the benefit. Read the opening line where they state this.
They base this on the idea that God is obligated to do the best for His servants.
2
See Nasafi's initial refutation
Nasafi rejects their claim, stating that rational people can see corruption and harm in these acts, so the claim is false.
He says 'every rational person understands in it an aspect of corruption and harm.'
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi turns their logic against them: if we accept that God's acts can be beneficial without our understanding, then one cannot object to saying that creating disbelief is also most beneficial. He uses the example of keeping a person alive until they disbelieve.
He asks: why is keeping someone alive to become a disbeliever not considered a benefit?
4
Recognize the final consequence
Nasafi concludes that if human reason is the standard for benefit and harm, then it cannot judge divine wisdom. Therefore, we should not impose obligations or restrictions on God.
This refutes the Mu'tazila position that God must always do the most beneficial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما تحيروا في هذا زعم بعضهم: ثبت لنا أن الله تعالى لا يفعل إلا ما هو الأصلح فيكون جميع ما يفعل أصلح، وإن كنا لا نعقل نحن له وجه المصلحة . فيقال لهم: بل يعقل فيه كل عاقل وجه المفسدة والمضرة، وعلم أن ما قلتم باطل. ثم نقول لهم ما أنكرتم على من يقول لكم إذا ثبت بما بينا أنه تعالى يمنع أنه تعالى حكيم علم أن منع المصلحة والأصلح حكمة وإن كنا لا نعقل وجه الحكمة، ويقال لهم ما أنكرتم على هذا أن يكون تخليق الكفر أصلح للعبد؟ فإن قالوا: كيف يكون ذلك أصلح وقد يتضرر به العبد؟ قلنا: وكيف يكون الإبقاء إلى أن يكفر أو يرتد أصلح له وهو يتضرر به، وإن قالوا: لا يتصور من العبد الإيمان إذا خلق فيه الكفر . قلنا ولا يتصور منه الإيمان إذا علم أنه يكفر فإن كان هذا مصلحة فذاك أيضا مصلحة، وإن لم يكن ذاك مصلحة فهذا أيضا ليس بمصلحة وإن عقلا هذا مبلغ معرفته بالمصلحة والمفسدة لحقيق أن لا يعرف صاحبه به الحكمة والسفه ولحري أن لا يتحكم على الله بالإيجاب عليه تارة والحجر عليه أخرى، والله الموفق.
So when they were perplexed about this, some of them claimed: It is established for us that God does nothing but what is the most beneficial, so all that He does is most beneficial, even if we do not understand its aspect of benefit. So it is said to them: Rather, every rational person understands in it an aspect of corruption and harm, and they know that what you say is false. Then we say to them: What do you deny to one who says to you: If it is established by what we have shown that He prevents, that God is wise, knowing that preventing benefit and the most beneficial is wisdom, even if we do not understand the aspect of wisdom? And it is said to them: What do you deny to this: that the creation of disbelief is most beneficial for the servant? If they say: How can that be most beneficial when the servant is harmed by it? We say: How can keeping him alive until he disbelieves or apostatizes be most beneficial for him when he is harmed by it? And if they say: It is inconceivable from the servant to have faith if disbelief is created in him. We say: And it is inconceivable from him to have faith if He knows he will disbelieve. So if this is a benefit, that is also a benefit; and if that is not a benefit, then this also is not a benefit. And if a person's knowledge of benefit and corruption reaches this extent, it is worthy that its possessor not know wisdom and foolishness, and it is fitting that he not impose upon God, at one time obligation and at another time restriction. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila retreating to the claim that everything God does is al-aslah, even if we do not understand the benefit. Nasafi refutes this by stating that every rational person can see the harm in these cases, so the claim is false. He then turns their argument: if we accept that God's prevention of benefit can still be wisdom even if we don't understand it, then one cannot object to saying the creation of disbelief is also most beneficial. The Mu'tazila object that disbelief harms the servant, but Nasafi retorts that keeping him alive to become a disbeliever is equally harmful. When they say that creation of disbelief makes faith impossible, Nasafi counters that divine foreknowledge also makes faith impossible. Thus, if one is a benefit, the other is also; if one is not, the other is not. He concludes that if human reason is the standard for benefit and harm, it cannot judge divine wisdom, so we should not impose obligations or restrictions on God.

Misconception: God is obligated to do the most beneficial for everyone according to human understanding.
Correction: Nasafi shows that human reason cannot comprehend the full wisdom of divine acts, so we cannot insist that God must always do what appears beneficial to us.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, if the Mu'tazila claim that everything God does is al-aslah even if we don't understand it, what follows?
A) We must always know the benefit
B) The creation of disbelief could also be considered al-aslah
C) God is not wise
D) The Mu'tazila accept that God does nothing harmful

Key Takeaway
If we accept that God's acts are always beneficial beyond human comprehension, we cannot consistently deny that even apparent harms like creating disbelief are also beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that seems purely harmful, do you truly believe it is the most beneficial for you from God, or does your heart struggle with doubt? What concrete step can you take today to trust His wisdom even when you see only harm?
?
Are there areas in your life where you secretly think God should have acted differently for your benefit? How does this lesson challenge your assumptions about what is good for you?
?
If you fully internalized that God's wisdom surpasses your understanding even in the worst moments, how would your response to suffering change starting right now?

The Mu'tazila Contradiction on Grace (Lutf)
Nasafi highlights the internal contradiction in the Mu'tazila concept of lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal contradiction in the Mu'tazila concept of lutf as identified by Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claim empowerment is enough, then add grace—a clear contradiction.

Imam Nasafi’s critique cuts to the core of Mu'tazila theology. They insist that moral responsibility requires full empowerment, yet they introduce an additional factor called lutf that seems to determine the choice. This passage exposes the inconsistency: if empowerment is truly sufficient, lutf should be unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp Nasafi's argument.
Notice the two key terms: tamkin (empowerment) and lutf (grace).
2
Identify the two claims
Find where the Mu'tazila first assert that the mukallaf needs empowerment to act, and then where they add that beyond empowerment there is lutf.
The contradiction lies in the addition of a second requirement.
3
Understand the contradiction
Reflect on why Nasafi calls this a 'gross contradiction': if empowerment alone is sufficient for responsibility, then lutf is either redundant or it undermines the sufficiency of empowerment.
The Mu'tazila seem to want both full ability and divine assistance that influences choice.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this contradiction affects the Mu'tazila position on human free will and divine justice. Can a person be fully accountable if their choice is influenced by lutf?
This connects to broader debates about kasb (acquisition) and divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان تناقض المعتزلة ثم لهم تناقض فاحش فإنهم زعموا أن المكلف لا بد له من التمكن من الفعل وبثبوت التمكن يتوصل إلى الفعل، ثم وراء التمكن والقدرة معاني ويسمونها لطفا، ويفسرون اللطف أنه ما يختار عنده المكلف ما كلف من أخذ وترك لولاه لكان لا يختار أو يكون أقرب إلى هذا الاختيار فما هذا حاله يسمونه لطفا
[Section revealing the contradiction of the Mu'tazila.] Then they have a gross contradiction: They claimed that the morally responsible person inevitably needs empowerment for the act, and by establishing empowerment he arrives at the act. Then beyond empowerment and capability there are meanings which they call grace, and they interpret grace as that due to which the morally responsible person chooses what he is commanded to do or leave, such that without it he would not choose, or it brings him closer to this choice. So what is in this state they call grace.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage exposes a contradiction in Mu'tazila doctrine. The Mu'tazila hold that the mukallaf must have empowerment (tamkin) to perform an act, and that with this empowerment he can indeed act. However, they then add that there are additional elements called lutf (grace) that are necessary for the mukallaf to choose obedience or avoid disobedience. Without lutf, he would not choose rightly, or lutf brings him closer to the right choice. Nasafi declares this a gross contradiction because they first insist on full empowerment, which should be sufficient for responsibility, and then add a factor that seems to determine the choice, undermining the sufficiency of empowerment. The contradiction lies in claiming both that the person has full ability and that additional grace is needed.

Key Terms
تمكين
قدرة
لطف
اختيار
مكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila concept of lutf is logically consistent.
Correction: Nasafi shows that demanding both full empowerment and additional grace is contradictory because empowerment should be sufficient for responsibility.

Checkpoint
What is the contradiction Nasafi finds in the Mu'tazila concept of lutf?
A) They claim both that the mukallaf has full empowerment and that lutf is needed
B) They deny the existence of lutf
C) They say lutf is only for believers
D) They confuse lutf with rizq

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position that lutf is needed alongside empowerment is self-contradictory because full empowerment should suffice for moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever convinced yourself that you are fully capable of obeying Allah on your own, yet still found yourself relying on a 'grace' that you didn't fully acknowledge? What would you lose if you admitted that your ability alone is not enough?
?
If the Mu'tazila's contradiction shows that human reasoning can lead to inconsistency, where in your own beliefs might you be holding two positions that don't align? Are you willing to examine them with the same critical eye?
?
The Mu'tazila added lutf because they sensed empowerment was insufficient. In your daily struggle to obey Allah, do you truly believe that without His grace you would not choose what is right? How would that belief change your morning prayers or your reliance on Him?
