The Absurd Consequence of the Mu'tazili Doctrine
Al-Nasafi deduces the absurd consequence of the Mu'tazili view on God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi uses a reductio ad absurdum to expose the unacceptable implications of the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-aslah.

Core Idea
If God always does what is most beneficial, then prophets' early death would be better.

Al-Nasafi now takes the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah (the most beneficial) to its logical extreme. He shows that if consistently applied, it leads to the shocking conclusion that God should have taken the souls of prophets earlier, and that the survival of Iblis would be more beneficial. Read this passage carefully to see how this reductio works and why Al-Nasafi considers it so abhorrent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the emotional tone at the end.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Al-Nasafi deduces the ultimate result of the Mu'tazili position. Find the statement that begins with 'Then the ultimate consequence...' and understand what it claims.
3
Examine the extension to all prophets and Iblis
The passage extends the principle to all messengers, saints, and even Iblis. Notice the contrast: the survival of the righteous is corruption, while the survival of the wicked is beneficial.
This reversal is the core of the absurdity.
4
Reflect on the emotional conclusion
Al-Nasafi ends with a strong emotional statement. Consider why he uses such language and what it reveals about the gravity of the error.
The phrase 'the heart of a believer cannot bear to hear it' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مآل مذهبهم إلى أن الله تعالى لو بقي محمدًا عليه السلام لمحة بصر وراء الساعة التي قبض روحه فيها لكفر الله تعالى وعصاه وكان قبض روحه في تلك الساعة أصلح له، وكذا في كل رسول ونبي وولي وصديق، فكان بقاء الرسل والأخيار مفسدة لهم وللخلق وبقاء إبليس والأشرار أصلح لهم وللخلق، وهذا قول لا يحتمل قلب مؤمن سماعه لفظاعته في نفسه وبشاعته في ذاته، والله خصيم من هذا قوله في رسله وأنبيائه المصطفين الأخيار وأوليائه وعباده الأبرار، ونسأل الله العصمة من الضلال والتمادي في الغي والجهالة.
Then the ultimate consequence of their doctrine is that if God Most High had kept Muhammad peace be upon him for a blink of an eye beyond the hour in which He took his soul, then God would have disbelieved and disobeyed, and taking his soul at that hour would have been more beneficial for him. And similarly for every messenger, prophet, saint, and truthful one. Thus the survival of messengers and righteous ones would be a corruption for them and for creation, and the survival of Iblis and the wicked would be more beneficial for them and for creation. This is a statement that the heart of a believer cannot bear to hear due to its atrocity in itself and its ugliness in its essence. God is the adversary of the one who says this about His messengers, His chosen righteous prophets, His saints, and His pious servants. And we ask God for protection from misguidance and persistence in error and ignorance.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a reductio ad absurdum of the Mu'tazili position on divine wisdom and the principle of al-aslah (the most beneficial). Al-Nasafi shows that if the Mu'tazili principle—that God must always do what is most beneficial for His servants—is applied consistently, it leads to the outrageous conclusion that it would have been more beneficial for God to take the souls of prophets and righteous people earlier, because their continued existence would allegedly lead to evil. Conversely, the survival of Iblis (Satan) and the wicked would be more beneficial. This is because if God's keeping them alive is not the most beneficial for them (as the Mu'tazila claim that God must give all means to obedience, and if He doesn't, the person might disbelieve), then their death would be a greater benefit. Al-Nasafi then states that no believer can hear such a statement due to its atrocity, and he calls upon God to protect from such misguidance.

Key Terms
مآل مذهبهم
الأصلح
مفسدة
خصيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah is harmless and does not lead to absurd consequences.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that consistent application leads to the absurd conclusion that prophets' survival is corruption and Iblis' survival is beneficial, which is unacceptable to any believer.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what does the Mu'tazili doctrine lead to regarding the survival of prophets?
A) It is the most beneficial for them.
B) It is a corruption and their death would be more beneficial.
C) It is neutral.
D) It is obligatory for God.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili doctrine of al-aslah, when applied consistently, leads to the unacceptable conclusion that prophets' early death is more beneficial than their continued existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the Mu'tazili reasoning that the Prophet's early death was 'more beneficial,' what does it reveal about your own hidden assumptions regarding God's actions? Do you sometimes expect God's decisions to fit your own sense of what is best? How does this passage expose the danger of placing human logic above divine will?
?
Al-Nasafi says no believer can bear to hear such a statement. What is your visceral reaction when you read that the survival of Iblis would be 'more beneficial' than the survival of prophets? Does it move you to humble yourself before God's wisdom, or do you feel a temptation to rationalize?
?
If you truly internalized that God's actions are not bound by human concepts of benefit, how would that transform your response to a personal tragedy—like the loss of a loved one at an early age? Would you still be able to say 'God did what is best' without needing to explain it?

Refuting the Mu'tazili Argument from Wisdom
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili claim that a wise being must provide all means for obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili argument from wisdom by showing the fallacy of analogy and the inapplicability of human needs to God.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili argument fails because it wrongly equates God's wisdom with human needs.

In this passage, al-Nasafi tackles a key Mu'tazili claim: that a wise commander must provide the means for obedience. He dismantles this by questioning the analogy from the witnessed to the unseen and by highlighting God's self-sufficiency. Let's examine his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nasafi uses a very structured refutation with multiple aspects.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili claim
Find the statement that al-Nasafi is refuting. It is paraphrased at the beginning: the claim that a wise being who commands obedience cannot withhold the means.
This is the argument from wisdom.
3
Understand the first refutation
Al-Nasafi's first point is that the analogy from the witnessed realm is invalid without establishing parity. He gives a counterexample and shows the absurd consequences if the analogy were taken strictly.
The counterexample: a man who does not prevent his slave's adultery is considered a cuckold.
4
Understand the second refutation
The second point questions the nature of the 'wise person' in the argument. If the wise person is in need, the argument might hold, but that does not apply to God. If the wise person is self-sufficient, the argument does not follow.
God is self-sufficient and does not benefit from obedience or suffer from disobedience.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how al-Nasafi's refutation protects divine transcendence and rejects anthropomorphism in theological reasoning.
This connects to the broader Maturidi methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما اعتمدوا عليه من الشبه فنقول قولهم إنا وجدنا الحكيم إذا كان أمرًا بطاعة محبًا لها مريدًا فلن يجوز أن يمنع المأمور ما يصل به إلى طاعته إلى آخر ما حكينا. كلام باطل من وجوه. :أحدها: أنه استدل على هذا بالشاهد من غير إثبات التسوية فيقال له: هب أن الأمر في الشاهد كان على ما زعمت، ولكن لمَ ينبغي أن يكون الأمر كذلك في الغائب؟ وبأي معنى تجمع بينهما؟ فإن زعمت أن كل ما ثبت في الشاهد كان إثباته في الغائب واجبًا من غير اعتبار المعنى. فذلك باطل وقد بينا قبل هذا أن من رأى عبده يزني بأمته فلم يمنع مع القدرة على المنع بل اشتغل بتهيئة أسباب ذلك كان ديوثًا سفيها وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه، وكذا في الشاهد لا قدرة لفاعل ما على إيجاد الأجسام وإنما يقدر الفاعل في الشاهد على بعض الأعراض وعندكم لا يقدر أيضًا إلا على إثبات الأفعال في محال هذه الأجسام فلو قسم الغائب على الشاهد لبطل القول بخلق الأجسام وهو قول الدهرية. وإن أثبتم ذلك في الغائب وإن امتنع إثبات مثله في الشاهد أبطلتم كلامكم. ثم يقال: على أي حكيم يجب ما ذكرت من بذل جميع ما في مقدوره لمن دعاه إلى طاعته وطلب رجوعه عن معصيته؟ أعلى حكيم محتاج إلى طاعة المطيع، ورجوع العدو عن العداوة متعذر بكثرة أعوانه وأنصاره وتكاتف حزبه وأوليائه ذليل عند كثرة أعدائه؟ أم على حكيم مستغن عن طاعة غيره إياه عزيز في ذاته قوي في سلطانه لا يضعفه كثرة الأعداء ولا يقويه كثرة الأولياء؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فهو مسلم، ولكن لا وجه إلى تعديته إلى الغائب. وإن قالوا: بالثاني. فهو ممنوع ثم نقول لهم: لو كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم أن الأمر كان مريدًا لطاعة المأمور محبًا لها، وكذا لرجوع العدو لكان ما قلت مسلمًا ولكان يعطي لا محالة ويبذل ما يحتاج إليه المأمور للامتثال ولكن لم قلتم أن البار جل
As for what they relied upon of the doubts, we say: their statement 'We find that the wise person, when he commands obedience, loves it and wills it, it is not permissible that he prevent the commanded person from what enables him to obey him' — up to what we have reported — is false speech from multiple aspects. First: that they infer this from the witnessed realm without establishing parity (tasyīya). So it is said to him: Grant that the matter in the witnessed realm is as you claimed, but why should the matter be likewise in the unseen? And by what meaning do you combine the two? For if you claim that everything established in the witnessed realm must be established in the unseen without consideration of meaning, that is false. And we have shown before this that one who sees his slave committing adultery with his slave-girl and does not prevent him despite ability to prevent, but rather busies himself with preparing the means for that, is a cuckold (dayyūth) and foolish; but in the unseen the matter is contrary to that. Likewise, in the witnessed realm no agent has power to create bodies; rather, the agent in the witnessed realm has power only over some accidents, and according to you he also has power only to effect actions in the substrates of these bodies. So if the unseen is analogized to the witnessed, the statement of creating bodies would be invalid, and that is the statement of the materialists (dahriyya). And if you affirm that in the unseen while its like cannot be affirmed in the witnessed, you invalidate your statement. Then it is said: Upon which wise person does what you mentioned become obligatory — of expending all that is within his power for one who calls him to his obedience and seeks his return from disobedience? Is it upon a wise person who is in need of the obedience of the obedient, and the enemy's return from enmity is impeded by the multitude of his supporters and helpers and the solidarity of his party and allies, and who is humiliated by the multitude of his enemies? Or upon a wise person who is independent of the obedience of others, mighty in His essence, strong in His authority, whom the multitude of enemies does not weaken nor the multitude of allies strengthen? If they say the first, it is granted, but there is no way to transfer it to the unseen. And if they say the second, it is denied. Then we say to them: If the matter were as you mentioned—that the commander wills the obedience of the commanded and loves it, and likewise for the enemy's return—then what you said would be granted, and he would inevitably give and expend what the commanded needs for compliance. But why did you say that the Most High...
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Mu'tazili argument. The Mu'tazila claim that a wise being, when he commands obedience and truly desires it, cannot withhold the means necessary for that obedience. Al-Nasafi declares this statement false from multiple aspects. The first aspect is that the Mu'tazila rely on an analogy from the witnessed realm (al-shāhid) to the unseen (al-ghā'ib) without establishing the relevant parity (tasyīya). He points out that if we grant their claim about the witnessed realm, it does not necessarily apply to the unseen. The author gives a counterexample: in the witnessed realm, a man who sees his slave committing adultery and does not prevent him despite being able to is considered a cuckold and foolish; but in the unseen, such a situation does not apply to God because His wisdom is different. He further argues that if one insists on a strict analogy, it would lead to absurdities: in the witnessed realm, no one can create bodies, only cause accidents; so if the unseen is analogized, the doctrine of God creating bodies would be false—which is the position of atheists. Thus the analogy either fails or leads to self-contradiction. The second aspect is a question about the nature of the 'wise person' in the argument. If the Mu'tazila mean a wise person who is in need of obedience and is weak, then such a person might be compelled to provide all means; but this cannot be transferred to God. If they mean a wise person who is self-sufficient and mighty, then He is not obligated to provide in the same way, because He does not benefit from obedience nor suffer from disobedience. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the refutation is clear: the Mu'tazili claim does not apply to God.

Key Terms
شبهة
الشاهد
الغائب
التسوية
حكيم
محتاج
مستغن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A wise being must always provide the means for obedience because human wise people do.
Correction: This ignores God's self-sufficiency and the difference between the witnessed and unseen realms; the analogy is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of al-Nasafi's refutation of the Mu'tazili argument?
A) The argument is based on an invalid analogy from the witnessed to the unseen.
B) The Mu'tazila misinterpret the Qur'an.
C) God does not actually command obedience.
D) The commanded person does not need means.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazili argument from wisdom fails because it relies on an invalid analogy and misattributes human neediness to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about God's actions by comparing them to human expectations, which part of this lesson will you cling to in order to silence that tendency? Will you memorize the image of the cuckold to guard your tongue from attributing neediness to God?
?
Have you ever felt entitled to God's provision or guidance because you were 'obeying' Him? How does Al-Nasafi's distinction between the needy wise person and the self-sufficient Wise judge that entitlement in your heart? Write down one specific thing you feel entitled to and examine whether you are projecting your own neediness onto God.
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that God 'must' provide means because of His wisdom. What do you insist that God 'must' do for you? Name it. Now ask yourself: Is that insistence rooted in a true understanding of God's independence or in your own sense of deserving? What will you do to uproot that assumption?

Refuting Equal Treatment with Quranic Verses
Imam al-Nasafi refutes the claim that God must treat all equally by citing Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the doctrine of obligatory equal treatment (taswiya) by citing Quranic verses that demonstrate God's selective distinctions.

Core Idea
God's wisdom includes distinctions; the Quran itself proves He does not treat all equally.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabirat al-Adilla directly challenges the claim that God must treat all people identically. He argues that such a claim contradicts the explicit verses of the Quran where God distinguishes between groups. In this lesson, you will see how he uses multiple Quranic contrasts to defend divine wisdom and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice that the author begins with a definition of favoritism and then launches into a series of rhetorical questions.
The rhetorical questions are the core of the argument.
2
Identify the opening definition
The passage first defines partiality and favoritism as exceeding the bounds of wisdom. This sets the stage: God does not exceed wisdom, so His distinctions are not favoritism.
This is a key premise: favoritism is only blameworthy when it exceeds wisdom.
3
Examine the Quranic contrasts
The author lists several pairs of verses: one showing God's special selection (mercy, wisdom, prophets), another showing God's placing of barriers or punishment. Each pair contrasts two groups.
Focus on how each pair refutes the idea of equal treatment.
4
Understand the cumulative refutation
The overall argument is that the Quran itself contains numerous examples of differential treatment, so the claim that God must treat all equally is false. The verses are evidence, not just assertions.
The author is using revelation to refute a rational claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الميل والمحاباة يكون فيما تجاوز الفاعل حد الحكمة ويفضل من يفضل لهواه. ثم نقول لهم: مالكم توجبون التسوية وقد سمعتم قول الله تعالى: ﴿يَخْتَصُّ بِرَحْمَتِهِ مَن يَشَاءُ﴾ الآية، وقوله تعالى: ﴿يُؤْتِي الْحِكْمَةَ مَن يَشَاءُ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ اصْطَفَى آدَمَ وَنُوحًا وَآلَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَآلَ عِمْرَانَ﴾؟ ومالكم توجبون التسوية بين هؤلاء وبين من أخبر عنهم بقوله: ﴿وَجَعَلْنَا عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ أَكِنَّةً أَن يَفْقَهُوهُ﴾؟ وكيف تسوي بين من قال الله تعالى فيهم: ﴿إِنَّمَا يُرِيدُ اللهُ لِيُذْهِبَ عَنكُمُ الرِّجْسَ أَهْلَ الْبَيْتِ وَيُطَهِّرَكُمْ تَطْهِيرًا﴾ وبين من قال فيهم: ﴿كَذَلِكَ يَجْعَلُ اللَّهُ الرِّجْسَ عَلَى الَّذِينَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ﴾؟ أو بين من قال فيهم: ﴿فَأُولَئِكَ مَعَ الَّذِينَ أَنْعَمَ اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِم مِّنَ النَّبِيِّينَ وَالصِّدِّيقِينَ وَالشُّهَدَاءِ وَالصَّالِحِينَ﴾ وبين من قال فيهم: ﴿خَتَمَ اللَّهُ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ﴾؟ وبين من قال فيهم: ﴿وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلَكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطًا﴾ الآية وبين من قال: ﴿وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَا لِكُلِّ نَبِيٍّ عَدُوًّا مِّنَ الْمُجْرِمِينَ﴾.
Partisanship and favoritism occur when the agent exceeds the bounds of wisdom and favors whom he favors out of passion. Then we say to them: Why do you require equal treatment when you have heard the saying of God Most High: 'He selects for His mercy whom He wills' — the verse; and His saying: 'He gives wisdom to whom He wills'; and His saying: 'Indeed God chose Adam, Noah, the family of Abraham, and the family of Imran'? And why do you require equal treatment between these and those of whom He informed with His saying: 'And We placed covers upon their hearts lest they understand it'? And how can you make equal between those of whom God Most High said: 'God only intends to remove impurity from you, People of the House, and to purify you thoroughly' and those of whom He said: 'Thus does God place impurity upon those who do not believe'? Or between those of whom He said: 'Then they are with those whom God has blessed — of the prophets, the truthful, the martyrs, and the righteous' and those of whom He said: 'God has sealed their hearts'? And between those of whom He said: 'And thus We made you a middle community' — the verse — and those of whom He said: 'And thus We made for every prophet an enemy from among the criminals'?
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine that God must treat all people equally (taswiya) by citing Quranic verses that show divine distinction. The author begins by stating that partiality and favoritism occur when an agent exceeds wisdom, but God does not. He then directly challenges his opponents: why do you insist on equal treatment when the Quran testifies otherwise? He lists several verses that demonstrate God's selective mercy (choosing whom He wills for mercy and wisdom), His choosing specific prophets and families (Adam, Noah, Abraham, Imran). He then contrasts groups: those whom God placed covers on their hearts so they cannot understand, versus those He purified as Ahl al-Bayt; those upon whom He places impurity for disbelief versus those He blesses among prophets, truthful, martyrs, righteous; those whose hearts are sealed versus the Muslim community as a 'middle nation'; and finally the enemies of prophets. The point is that the Quran itself negates any obligation of identical treatment—God's actions are based on His wisdom and will.

Key Terms
التسوية (taswiya)
اختصاص (ikhtisas)
محاباة (muhabah)
فضلهوى (fadl hawa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's justice requires that He treat all humans identically, giving equal chances and rewards.
Correction: The Quran explicitly shows that God distinguishes between people based on His wisdom. He selects some for prophethood, purifies some, and seals the hearts of others. This differential treatment is not injustice but part of divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main problem with requiring God to treat all people equally?
A) God must be just, so He treats all equally.
B) The Quran clearly shows that God distinguishes between different groups of people.
C) Favoritism is an essential part of God's nature.
D) Human reason demands equality regardless of revelation.

Key Takeaway
God's differential treatment, as shown in the Quran, is based on His wisdom and will, and does not contradict justice; thus, the claim that God must treat all equally is refuted by revelation itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself demanding that God treat you exactly like someone else, forgetting that His wisdom distributes gifts and trials differently? What would change if you truly accepted His selective mercy?
?
When you see others being blessed in ways you are not, do you feel God is being unfair? How does this passage challenge that feeling and call you to trust His wisdom?
?
Are there areas in your life where you insist on 'equal treatment' from God, rather than trusting His wise distinctions? How can you surrender that expectation today and thank Him for His unique plan for you?

The First Mu'tazilite View on Children's Suffering and Its Refutation
Nasafi presents and refutes the first Mu'tazilite view on children's suffering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite attempt to explain children's suffering and Nasafi's two-pronged refutation based on their own principles of 'aslah'.

Core Idea
Children's suffering cannot be justified as a lesson for adults without contradicting Mu'tazilite theology.

The Mu'tazilites faced a challenge: if God must do what is best for everyone, why do innocent children suffer? One group proposed that children's pain serves as a warning to adults. In this passage, Nasafi dissects this claim and shows it contradicts the Mu'tazilites' own doctrine of 'aslah' and justice. Let's explore the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first Mu'tazilite answer
The passage begins by stating the problem: children suffer undeservedly. The Mu'tazilites answer that it is for adults' admonition. Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claim.
Notice that the Mu'tazilites assume God's action must be for someone's benefit.
2
Understand Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi argues the child gains nothing: he has no intellect now and won't remember later. He may die before puberty. So the suffering is harmful without benefit.
This shows the child is not compensated, violating the principle of 'aslah' for the child.
3
Identify the two contradictions
Nasafi points out two contradictions in the Mu'tazilite position: (1) the action is not best for the child, which is injustice; (2) it departs from wisdom, which they require for 'aslah'.
These are based on the Mu'tazilites' own criteria.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
See how Nasafi uses the Mu'tazilites' own doctrine of 'aslah' against them, showing internal inconsistency.
This is a classic refutation method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقيل لهم : إن الأطفال يتألمون ويتوجعون وتصيبهم الأمراض والأوجاع، ولم يستوجبوا شيئًا من ذلك بصنيعهم لما لا تصور للجريمة منهم وليس ذلك أصلح لهم في الدين إذ لا تكليف عليهم فتفرقت المعتزلة في الاعتراض على هذا. فزعمت طائفة منهم أنهم يؤلمون ليتعظ بذلك الآباء والأمهات ومن عاين ذلك من البالغين. قيل لهم : إن كان ذلك للبالغين أصلح فلا شك أن هذا الطفل يتضرر به في الدنيا، ولا ينتفع به في الآخرة لما لا يصير ذلك داعيًا له إلى فعل طاعة أو الامتناع عن معصية لانعدام عقله للحال وعدم تصور تذكره لذلك عند البلوغ، أو لأنه ربما يموت قبل جري التكليف عليه وإثبات الأصلح لغير الصغير مما مفسدة للصغير تناقض منكم لوجهين: أحدهما : أن ما فعل بالصغير ليس بأصلح له، فإذا فعل بشخص ما ليس بأصلح له فيكون هذا في حقه ظلما وجورًا عندكم. والآخر أن عندكم من شرط الأصلح أن لا يصير بإعطاء الأصلح خارجًا عن الحكمة، وفي هذا خروجه عن الحكمة.
It was said to them: Indeed children suffer and are pained, and illnesses and pains befall them, and they have not deserved any of that by their own action because there is no possibility of crime from them, and that is not better (aslah) for them in religion since there is no legal responsibility (taklif) upon them. So the Mu'tazila differed in objecting to this. A group of them claimed that they are pained so that fathers and mothers and those who witness that among adults may take admonition. It was said to them: If that is better for the adults, there is no doubt that this child is harmed by it in this world, and does not benefit from it in the Hereafter, because that does not become an incentive for him to perform an act of obedience or refrain from disobedience due to the absence of his intellect at present and the impossibility of his remembering it upon reaching puberty, or because he might die before the imposition of legal responsibility upon him. And affirming what is best (aslah) for someone other than the child, which is harmful to the child, is a contradiction from you in two respects: One: that what is done to the child is not best for him; so if something that is not best for a person is done to him, then that is injustice and tyranny in your view. The second: that according to you, one of the conditions of what is best (aslah) is that giving what is best should not result in departure from wisdom, and in this case it results in departure from wisdom.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The Mu'tazilites object that children suffer undeservedly. Nasafi first presents the objection: children suffer without having committed any crime, and since they are not legally responsible (mukallaf), it cannot be for their own good. The Mu'tazilites then give a first explanation: the suffering is for the admonition of adults (parents and observers). Nasafi refutes this by arguing that even if it benefits adults, the child is harmed in this world and gains nothing in the Hereafter, because he lacks intellect now and will not remember it when he becomes accountable, or may die before that. Then he points out a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite doctrine of 'aslah' (what is best): according to them, God must do what is best for each person. Here, what is done to the child is not best for him but for others, which contradicts their principle. He gives two aspects: First, doing what is not best for someone is injustice (zulm) in their view. Second, their condition for 'aslah' is that it does not depart from wisdom (hikma), but here it does depart because it harms the child without benefit to him.

Key Terms
أصلح
تكليف
ظلم
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone benefits from a child's suffering, God is just in causing it.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the child must also benefit or the act becomes unjust and unwise according to Mu'tazilite principles.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what are the two contradictions in the Mu'tazilite claim that children suffer for the admonition of adults?
A) The child is not harmed, and the adults do not learn.
B) The act is not best for the child (thus injustice), and it departs from wisdom.
C) The child will be compensated in the Hereafter, and God is just.
D) The child may die before adulthood, and the adults are not punished.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the first Mu'tazilite view by showing that using children's suffering for adults' admonition fails to benefit the child and contradicts their own doctrine of 'aslah'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a child suffering, do you ever rationalize it by thinking it might teach others a lesson? After reading this passage, how does Nasafi's argument challenge that rationalization? Will you now resist such justifications?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they were defending God's justice. Where in your own life do you try to defend God's actions by claiming they benefit others, while ignoring the direct harm? Can you name one such situation and commit to rethinking it?
?
If you believe God is perfectly just, how does the suffering of innocent children affect your trust? This passage shows a theological argument, but does it move your heart to accept God's wisdom beyond human reasoning? What would it take for you to surrender to God's justice even without understanding?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Argument from Compensation
Nasafi presents and refutes the second Mu'tazilite view on children's suffering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that children's suffering is compensated in the Hereafter does not solve the problem of theodicy, and how Nasafi refutes it using the child's fate and God's omnipotence.

Core Idea
Future compensation does not justify present injustice when the recipient cannot consent.

The second Mu'tazilite explanation attempts to justify children's suffering by appealing to future compensation in Paradise. Nasafi dismantles this argument by showing that many suffering children never receive that reward, and that the very analogy used by the Mu'tazilites actually undermines their position when God's omnipotence is considered.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the passage that presents the Mu'tazilite argument: children suffer so that God can compensate them with eternal reward, analogous to a father giving medical treatment.
Notice the key terms: compensation (عوض), best (أصلح), and wisdom (حكمة).
2
Examine Nasafi's first two objections
Nasafi points out that many children die as disbelievers and receive no compensation; God knew this beforehand. He also argues that injustice persists until the wronged party is satisfied, which cannot happen at the time of pain.
The second objection relies on the principle that injustice requires restitution with consent.
3
Analyze the medical analogy and its failure
The Mu'tazilites compare God to a father using painful treatments. Nasafi turns this around: the father is only justified because he lacks power to heal without pain. God is all-powerful and can heal without pain, making causing pain unnecessary and unjust.
This refutation highlights a key difference between human and divine action.

وزعم جمهورهم أن هذا أصلح لهذا الصغير، لأنه يعوضه بذلك ثوابًا مخلدًا في الآخرة ولا يجوز أن يعوضه ولو لم يعوضه لكان ظالما، والإيلام لمنفعة تحصل في العاقبة به حكمة، كالأب يحجم صبيه ويسقيه الأدوية الكريهة ويحسن ذلك لأن فيه من منفعة البرء في العاقبة. وهذا الكلام باطل من وجوه: أحدها: أن كثيرًا من الأطفال الذين تألموا في صغرهم ماتوا على الكفر ولا ينالون العوض في الآخرة، وكان الله تعالى عالمًا بعاقبة أمرهم عند إيلامهم فيكون الباري عز وجل على هذا ظالمًا على هذا الطفل حيث آلمه علمه أنه لا يعوضه، ولأن ما كان ظلمًا بغير عوض يعتقد ظلمًا وجورًا عند الوجود إلى أن يرضى من له الحق بالعوض، فينعقد فعل الله تعالى للحال ظلمًا ويصير هو ظالمًا إلى أن يزول أثر الظلم بإيصال العوض، وكذا ما يكون ظلمًا بغير عوض لا يزول عنه معنى الظلم، وإن كان الفاعل قصد التعويض ما لم يرض بذلك من له الحق ولا رضا من الصغير وقت الإيلام بالعوض، وما ضرب من المثال بفصل الحجامة وسقي الأدوية فهو الذي يدل على بطلان مذهبهم وفساد قولهم، فإن الأب إنما لم يخرج بذلك عن الحكمة وأطلق له ذلك الفعل إذا كان لا يقدر على تحصيل البرء والصحة بدون سابقة الإيلام بالحجامة والإضرار بسقي الأدوية الكريهة، ولو فعل القدرة على ذلك يعد ظالمًا سفيها جائراً، والله تعالى قادر على إيصال ذلك مع ذلك
And the majority of them claimed that this is best for this child, because He compensates him with eternal reward in the Hereafter, and it is not permissible for Him not to compensate him, for if He did not compensate him He would be unjust. And causing pain for a benefit that occurs in the end is wisdom, like a father cupping his son and giving him bitter medicine, and that is good because there is benefit of cure in the end. But this statement is false in several respects: One: that many children who suffered in their childhood died in disbelief and do not receive compensation in the Hereafter, and God Almighty knew the final outcome of their affair when He caused them pain. So the Creator, exalted, would on this view be unjust to this child where He caused him pain knowing that He will not compensate him. And because what is injustice without compensation is considered injustice and tyranny as long as it exists until the one who has the right is satisfied with compensation. Thus the act of God becomes at present unjust and He becomes unjust until the effect of injustice is removed by delivering compensation. Likewise, what is injustice without compensation does not have the meaning of injustice removed from it, even if the agent intends compensation, as long as the one who has the right is not satisfied with that, and there is no satisfaction from the child at the time of pain with compensation. And the example given of cupping and administering medicine actually indicates the falsity of their school and the corruption of their statement, because the father does not depart from wisdom by that and is allowed that action only if he is unable to achieve recovery and health without the prior pain of cupping and harm of bitter medicine. If he had the power to do that (i.e., heal without pain), he would be considered an unjust, foolish, tyrannical person. And God Almighty is able to bring about that (healing) without that.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The majority of Mu'tazilites give a second explanation: children suffer so that God can compensate them with eternal reward in Paradise, and this compensation makes the suffering just, analogous to a father giving his child medical treatment (cupping, bitter medicine) for the benefit of health. Nasafi refutes this in several ways. First, many children who suffered die as disbelievers (kuffar) and thus receive no compensation in the Hereafter—God knew this outcome beforehand, so on their view God would be unjust by causing them pain without compensation. Second, even if compensation is intended, injustice persists until the actual satisfaction of the wronged party; the child cannot consent at the time of pain. Third, the analogy with medical treatment backfires: a father is allowed to cause pain only because he lacks the power to heal without it; if he had such power, he would be unjust. Since God is all-powerful (qadir) and can heal without pain, causing pain without necessity would be unjust. Thus the Mu'tazilite position is invalid.

Key Terms
أصلح
عوض
ظلم
حكمة
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Children's suffering is justified if compensated in the afterlife, just as a father's painful medical treatment is justified by health benefit.
Correction: The analogy fails because a father is not all-powerful; God can give health without pain, making unnecessary suffering unjust. Furthermore, compensation does not erase the injustice of causing pain without prior consent.

Checkpoint
Why does the analogy of the father cupping his son or giving medicine fail to justify God causing pain?
A) The father is not all-powerful, but God can heal without pain.
B) The child does not benefit from cupping.
C) The father is unjust even if he heals.
D) Medicine is always harmful.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the second Mu'tazilite view by highlighting that children who die as disbelievers receive no compensation, and that an all-powerful God has no need to cause pain for benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a child suffering, do you find yourself demanding an explanation from God? How does this lesson's emphasis on God's omnipotence and the need for consent challenge you to trust His wisdom even without understanding?
?
Have you ever justified your own or others' suffering by pointing to a future reward, without considering whether you have truly consented to that pain? In what area of your life do you need to surrender your 'right' to an explanation and simply trust God's power and justice?
?
The father analogy breaks down because God could heal without pain. Is there a situation in your life where you believe you must suffer to achieve good, yet God could give it without the pain? How does this reframing affect your reliance on Him and your patience in hardship?

The Grandeur of the Giver Elevates the Gift
Al-Nasafi uses an analogy of kingly gifts to argue that God's favors cannot be burdensome.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the greatness of God as the Giver prevents His favors from ever being a burden or cause of resentment.

Core Idea
A gift from a great king is an honor; from God, infinitely more so.

Imam al-Nasafi begins with a powerful analogy: a subject who receives a favor from a mighty king counts it as his greatest honor, while a mere commercial transaction brings no such prestige. He then applies this to God, whose grandeur is incomparable, showing that any notion of obligation or burden attached to His favors is irrational. This passage sets the conceptual foundation for rejecting the Mu'tazila claim that God's grace is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Pay attention to the contrast between a gift from a king and a purchase. Notice how the recipient's perception changes based on the giver's status.
The analogy illustrates that the value of a favor depends on the giver's majesty.
2
Apply the analogy to God
Now read how al-Nasafi applies this reasoning to God. God's absolute sovereignty and power make any favor from Him infinitely honorable, never a burden.
The key phrase: 'how could it be imagined that His favors become unpleasant due to the attachment of obligation from the Almighty?'
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why al-Nasafi concludes that even the highest Paradise would not be tainted if God's gifts were obligatory. This directly challenges the Mu'tazila position.
This argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the Mu'tazila view.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المختص بمزية في السعة والميسرة والجاه والمقدرة اتخذ يدًا بمكان صاحبه وأدر الله عليه سوابغ نعمه، فيرى المنعم عليه نفسه تحت منته و رهن مباره وأياديه ورقبته كالمسترقة له مع أن تبدل الحال مترتب تحت الإمكان الأقرب والتصور الأيسر فتتنغص عليه النعمة بمقابلة ما يرى برقبته مسترقة لمن يساويه في الرتبة أو يقاربه في الدرجة والمنزلة حتى إن تبدل الحال لو كان من الممكنات الأبعدية والموهومات القصية بأن اختص ملك من الملوك في الأرض أو عظيم من عظماء المملكة بعض السوقة واحد من أمناء الناس وجعله أهلاً لأحبته ومباره واتخذه غيبة لأسراره أو ساق عليه السلطان الأعظم إلى حد من أكابر أبناء دولته وأشياع دعوته أموالاً خطيرة، وخلع عليه خلعًا نفيسة لعد المنعم عليه ذلك من أجل مفاخره وأعظم مآثره ومهما ازداد حظه من ذلك ازداد في نفسه ارتياحا وفي قلبه انفساحًا وانشراحا، ويمثله لو أن أحد من هؤلاء الأكابر والعظماء اشترى شيئًا من أحد وأوفاه ثمنه لم يحصل للبائع بذلك سبب فخر ولا يثبت له عند الناس منزلة وقدر، وكان ما نيل من السلطان بالامتنان والإنعام ألذ وأشهى وأهنأ عند كل العقلاء، وأمرًا هذا مع كون تقلب الحال وتبدل الشأن مترتبا تحت الإمكان وإن بعد وداخلاً في حيز التصور وإن تعذر فى العادات ونذر ، فكيف يتوهم أن تتنغص نعمه وإن صغرت في ذاتها وقلت في نفسها بلحوق منة من قبل الجبار المتكبر القهار المقتدر الذي كل شيء وإن عظم قدره وجل خطره داخل في ملكوته وخاضع لجبروته، يعز من يعز بإحسانه وإفضاله ويذل من يذل بإهانته وإذلاله، لا زوال لملكه وسلطانه ولا تبدل لتذلل العبد وادعائه بل التبدل والزوال يدخلان في حيز ثم لو كان الأمر على ما زعم لكانت الجنة العليا وما فيها من النعم منغصة على أولياء الله وأهل كرامته، لأن نيلهم ذلك كله كان بفضل من الله ونعمة منه، إذ لا أحد يستوجب بشيء من عباداته وطاعاته على الله تعالى شيئًا وإن زجي عمره في طاعته ولم يعصه طرفة عين، وذلك لأن العبد ما يأتيه من الطاعة يأتيه شكرًا لما أنعم عليه، ومن أدى إلى غيره حقا مستحقاً لا يستوجب بإزاء ما أدى شيئًا من العوض، على أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج في جميع عمره وإن طال عن شكر نعمة واحدة وإن قلت لما أنه لا يؤدي شكره إلا بتوفيق من ربه وما وصل إليه من أثر التوفيق نعمة مستأنفة يقتضى عليها شكرًا مستأنفا ثم لا يؤدي شكر هذا التوفيق إلا بتوفيق هكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى، وإذا كان لا يمكنه الخروج عن شكر نعمة واحدة فكيف عن شكر ما عليه من النعم الوافية الوافرة والمنن المتوالية المتظاهرة التي لا يمكنه إحصاؤها وعدها ويتعذر عليه حصرها وحدها على ما نطق به الكتاب بقوله تعالى : ( وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَةَ اللهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا ). وللمعتزلة في هذا تدبير عجيب فإنهم يزعمون أن جميع ما فعل الله تعالى بالعبد وأسدى إليه من النعم كل ذلك حق على الله واجب وجوبًا لو امتنع عن قضائه إلى مستحقه لصار ظالماً جائراً، ثم إذا أوفى هذا الحق إلى مستحقه استوجب عليه شكرًا لو امتنع العبد عن أدائه صار ظالمًاً جائراً سفيها مانعا حقا مستحقا ثم إذا أدى ذلك استوجب على الله تعالى بذلك ثوابًا على وجه لو لم يعرفه الله تعالى لكان جائرا ظالمًا. وهذا خروج عن المعقول، وكيف يستوجب من قضى ما عليه من الحق إلى مستحقه [؟]
The one singled out for distinction in abundance, ease, status, and power took a hand in place of his companion and God bestowed upon him abundant favors, so the favored one sees himself under his benefaction, bound by his gifts, favors, and obligations, as if they were stolen from him, even though the change of condition is contingent upon the nearest possibility and easiest conception. Thus the favor becomes unpleasant for him in comparison to what he sees as an obligation upon his neck from one equal to him in rank or close to him in degree and station, to the extent that if the change of condition were among the most remote possibilities and far-fetched imaginings—such as if a king of the kings on earth or a great one of the greats of the kingdom singled out one of the commoners, a trusted person, and made him worthy of his loved ones and gifts, took him as a confidant for his secrets, or if the supreme sultan drove to him from among the great ones of his state and followers of his call huge sums of money and bestowed upon him precious robes—the favored one would count that among his greatest honors and most magnificent deeds. The more his fortune increased from that, the more he felt joy in himself and expansion and delight in his heart. An example: if one of these great and mighty ones bought something from someone and paid its price, the seller would not obtain any cause for pride nor establish for himself any status or esteem among people. What is obtained from the sultan by grace and favor is more delicious, more pleasant, and more wholesome in the view of all rational people. This is the case even though the fluctuation of condition and change of state is contingent upon possibility, even if remote, and falls within the domain of conception, even if difficult in custom and rare. So how could it be imagined that His favors, even if small in themselves and few in themselves, become unpleasant due to the attachment of obligation from the Almighty, the Proud, the Subduer, the Omnipotent, to Whom everything, however great its magnitude and immense its importance, falls within His dominion and is subject to His compulsion? He honors whom He wills by His benevolence and bounty, and humiliates whom He wills by His humiliation and abasement. There is no cessation to His kingdom and authority, nor any change in the abasement of the servant and his claim; rather, change and cessation fall into the domain [of possibility]. Then, if the matter were as they claimed, the highest Paradise and what it contains of favors would be unpleasant for the friends of God and the people of His honor, because their attainment of all that was by God's bounty and grace, since no one deserves anything from God Almighty by any of his acts of worship and obedience, even if he spends his entire life in obedience and does not disobey Him for the blink of an eye. That is because the obedience that the servant performs is performed as gratitude for what He has bestowed upon him. One who renders to another a right that is due does not deserve any compensation in return for what he rendered. Moreover, the servant cannot, throughout his entire life, however long, escape from gratitude for a single favor, however small, because he does not perform his gratitude except by the enabling grace (tawfiq) from his Lord, and what reaches him from the effect of enabling grace is a renewed favor requiring renewed gratitude. Then he does not perform gratitude for this enabling grace except by enabling grace, and so on ad infinitum. If he cannot escape from gratitude for one favor, how can he escape from gratitude for the abundant and ample favors and the successive and successive blessings that he cannot enumerate or count, and for which it is impossible to limit or define them, as the Book has spoken with His saying: 'And if you count the favors of God, you cannot enumerate them.' And the Mu'tazila have a strange device in this matter, for they claim that all that God Almighty does to the servant and bestows upon him of favors is all a right incumbent upon God, an obligation such that if He refrained from fulfilling it to its due recipient, He would become an unjust tyrant. Then, when He fulfills this right to its due recipient, gratitude becomes incumbent upon the servant; if the servant refrains from performing it, he becomes an unjust tyrant, a fool, preventing a due right. Then, when he performs that, he deserves from God Almighty a reward in such a way that if God Almighty did not grant it to him, He would be an unjust tyrant. This is a departure from reason. How can one who fulfills what is due from him to its recipient deserve something?
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that God's favors are obligatory and that human obedience generates a claim on God. Imam al-Nasafi begins with an analogy: a person who receives a gift from a great king takes it as an honor and is grateful, but if the same item were purchased from a merchant, the merchant gains no special status. This illustrates that the value of a favor is enhanced by the giver's grandeur. Applying this to God, whose greatness is infinite, any favor from Him, no matter how small, cannot become burdensome due to a sense of obligation (minna), because God's sovereignty and power are absolute, and all things are subject to Him. Then al-Nasafi argues that if the Mu'tazila view were correct—that God's gifts generate obligation—then even Paradise would be tainted for the saints, since they receive it by God's grace, not by their own merit. He explains that no act of obedience can obligate God, because obedience is itself a form of gratitude for prior favors. Moreover, a person cannot fully thank God for even a single favor, because each act of gratitude requires God's enabling grace (tawfiq), which is itself a new favor requiring further gratitude, leading to an infinite regress. This is supported by the Quranic verse that humans cannot count God's favors. Al-Nasafi then criticizes the Mu'tazila doctrine: they claim that God's acts are obligatory (wajib) on Him, and that if He fails to bestow a favor, He would be unjust; then, after bestowing it, the servant owes gratitude; and after gratitude, God owes reward—otherwise He would be unjust again. This, al-Nasafi says, is irrational (khurūj 'an al-ma'qūl). He concludes with a rhetorical question: how can one who has merely fulfilled an obligation to its recipient deserve anything additional? The passage thus defends the Sunni view that God's grace is free, not obligatory.

Key Terms
منة
جبروت
ملكوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that receiving a favor from a higher power creates a negative sense of obligation, making the recipient resentful.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that when the giver is infinitely great, the favor is an honor, not a burden. This corrects the misconception by emphasizing the Giver's majesty.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy in the passage, why does a gift from a king bring more honor than a commercial transaction?
A) Because the king's gift is larger in value.
B) Because the giver's high status elevates the recipient's standing.
C) Because the recipient is forced to reciprocate.
D) Because the king expects gratitude in return.

Key Takeaway
God's infinite greatness ensures that His favors, no matter how small, are always a source of honor and never a burden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a blessing you received that felt more like an obligation than an honor. After reading this passage, do you realize that the feeling of burden came from focusing on yourself rather than on the Giver? Will you consciously remind yourself, starting today, that every favor from God is a royal gift?
?
When you look at your life, do you see 'small' blessings (like the air you breathe, food on your table) as ordinary—or do you treat them with the awe due a gift from the King of kings? What specific 'small' blessing will you thank Him for with full awareness of His majesty?
?
Al-Nasafi says a favor from an equal burdens the soul, but from a great king it delights. Is there someone in your life whose status or worldly position makes you feel small? How can the truth that you serve the Most High free you from envy and allow you to rejoice in whatever He gives you?

The Infinite Cycle of Gratitude
Al-Nasafi presents an infinite regress argument against the possibility of fully fulfilling gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human beings cannot fully thank God for even a single favor, due to the infinite regress of divine enabling grace (tawfiq).

Core Idea
Every act of gratitude requires God's help, creating an endless chain.

Imam al-Nasafi deepens his refutation by examining the nature of gratitude. He argues that human obedience is itself a form of thankfulness, and that thankfulness requires God's enabling grace (tawfiq). But that tawfiq is itself another favor, necessitating further gratitude—and so on, ad infinitum. This demonstrates the impossibility of ever fully fulfilling the duty of gratitude, and thus the absurdity of the Mu'tazila claim that human actions can obligate God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of obedience
Read the passage and note that al-Nasafi says every act of obedience is performed as gratitude for prior favors. Obedience is not a basis for demanding reward.
This reverses the common assumption: we obey to thank God, not to earn from Him.
2
Trace the infinite regress
Notice the logic: gratitude requires tawfiq, but tawfiq is a new favor requiring new gratitude, requiring new tawfiq, and so on without end.
This shows the impossibility of fully thanking God for even one favor.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Al-Nasafi cites the verse 'If you count the favors of God, you cannot enumerate them' to reinforce the point that human capacity is insufficient.
The verse is a scriptural proof for the logical argument.

خطيرة، وخلع عليه خلعًا نفيسة لعد المنعم عليه ذلك من أجل مفاخره وأعظم مآثره ومهما ازداد حظه من ذلك ازداد في نفسه ارتياحا وفي قلبه انفساحًا وانشراحا، ويمثله لو أن أحد من هؤلاء الأكابر والعظماء اشترى شيئًا من أحد وأوفاه ثمنه لم يحصل للبائع بذلك سبب فخر ولا يثبت له عند الناس منزلة وقدر، وكان ما نيل من السلطان بالامتنان والإنعام ألذ وأشهى وأهنأ عند كل العقلاء، وأمرًا هذا مع كون تقلب الحال وتبدل الشأن مترتبا تحت الإمكان وإن بعد وداخلاً في حيز التصور وإن تعذر فى العادات ونذر ، فكيف يتوهم أن تتنغص نعمه وإن صغرت في ذاتها وقلت في نفسها بلحوق منة من قبل الجبار المتكبر القهار المقتدر الذي كل شيء وإن عظم قدره وجل خطره داخل في ملكوته وخاضع لجبروته، يعز من يعز بإحسانه وإفضاله ويذل من يذل بإهانته وإذلاله، لا زوال لملكه وسلطانه ولا تبدل لتذلل العبد وادعائه بل التبدل والزوال يدخلان في حيز ثم لو كان الأمر على ما زعم لكانت الجنة العليا وما فيها من النعم منغصة على أولياء الله وأهل كرامته، لأن نيلهم ذلك كله كان بفضل من الله ونعمة منه، إذ لا أحد يستوجب بشيء من عباداته وطاعاته على الله تعالى شيئًا وإن زجي عمره في طاعته ولم يعصه طرفة عين، وذلك لأن العبد ما يأتيه من الطاعة يأتيه شكرًا لما أنعم عليه، ومن أدى إلى غيره حقا مستحقاً لا يستوجب بإزاء ما أدى شيئًا من العوض، على أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج في جميع عمره وإن طال عن شكر نعمة واحدة وإن قلت لما أنه لا يؤدي شكره إلا بتوفيق من ربه وما وصل إليه من أثر التوفيق نعمة مستأنفة يقتضى عليها شكرًا مستأنفا ثم لا يؤدي شكر هذا التوفيق إلا بتوفيق هكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى، وإذا كان لا يمكنه الخروج عن شكر نعمة واحدة فكيف عن شكر ما عليه من النعم الوافية الوافرة والمنن المتوالية المتظاهرة التي لا يمكنه إحصاؤها وعدها ويتعذر عليه حصرها وحدها على ما نطق به الكتاب بقوله تعالى : ( وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَةَ اللهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا ). وللمعتزلة في هذا تدبير عجيب فإنهم يزعمون أن جميع ما فعل الله تعالى بالعبد وأسدى إليه من النعم كل ذلك حق على الله واجب وجوبًا لو امتنع عن قضائه إلى مستحقه لصار ظالماً جائراً، ثم إذا أوفى هذا الحق إلى مستحقه استوجب عليه شكرًا لو امتنع العبد عن أدائه صار ظالمًاً جائراً سفيها مانعا حقا مستحقا ثم إذا أدى ذلك استوجب على الله تعالى بذلك ثوابًا على وجه لو لم يعرفه الله تعالى لكان جائرا ظالمًا. وهذا خروج عن المعقول، وكيف يستوجب من قضى ما عليه من الحق إلى مستحقه [؟]
The one singled out for distinction in abundance, ease, status, and power took a hand in place of his companion and God bestowed upon him abundant favors, so the favored one sees himself under his benefaction, bound by his gifts, favors, and obligations, as if they were stolen from him, even though the change of condition is contingent upon the nearest possibility and easiest conception. Thus the favor becomes unpleasant for him in comparison to what he sees as an obligation upon his neck from one equal to him in rank or close to him in degree and station, to the extent that if the change of condition were among the most remote possibilities and far-fetched imaginings—such as if a king of the kings on earth or a great one of the greats of the kingdom singled out one of the commoners, a trusted person, and made him worthy of his loved ones and gifts, took him as a confidant for his secrets, or if the supreme sultan drove to him from among the great ones of his state and followers of his call huge sums of money and bestowed upon him precious robes—the favored one would count that among his greatest honors and most magnificent deeds. The more his fortune increased from that, the more he felt joy in himself and expansion and delight in his heart. An example: if one of these great and mighty ones bought something from someone and paid its price, the seller would not obtain any cause for pride nor establish for himself any status or esteem among people. What is obtained from the sultan by grace and favor is more delicious, more pleasant, and more wholesome in the view of all rational people. This is the case even though the fluctuation of condition and change of state is contingent upon possibility, even if remote, and falls within the domain of conception, even if difficult in custom and rare. So how could it be imagined that His favors, even if small in themselves and few in themselves, become unpleasant due to the attachment of obligation from the Almighty, the Proud, the Subduer, the Omnipotent, to Whom everything, however great its magnitude and immense its importance, falls within His dominion and is subject to His compulsion? He honors whom He wills by His benevolence and bounty, and humiliates whom He wills by His humiliation and abasement. There is no cessation to His kingdom and authority, nor any change in the abasement of the servant and his claim; rather, change and cessation fall into the domain [of possibility]. Then, if the matter were as they claimed, the highest Paradise and what it contains of favors would be unpleasant for the friends of God and the people of His honor, because their attainment of all that was by God's bounty and grace, since no one deserves anything from God Almighty by any of his acts of worship and obedience, even if he spends his entire life in obedience and does not disobey Him for the blink of an eye. That is because the obedience that the servant performs is performed as gratitude for what He has bestowed upon him. One who renders to another a right that is due does not deserve any compensation in return for what he rendered. Moreover, the servant cannot, throughout his entire life, however long, escape from gratitude for a single favor, however small, because he does not perform his gratitude except by the enabling grace (tawfiq) from his Lord, and what reaches him from the effect of enabling grace is a renewed favor requiring renewed gratitude. Then he does not perform gratitude for this enabling grace except by enabling grace, and so on ad infinitum. If he cannot escape from gratitude for one favor, how can he escape from gratitude for the abundant and ample favors and the successive and successive blessings that he cannot enumerate or count, and for which it is impossible to limit or define them, as the Book has spoken with His saying: 'And if you count the favors of God, you cannot enumerate them.' And the Mu'tazila have a strange device in this matter, for they claim that all that God Almighty does to the servant and bestows upon him of favors is all a right incumbent upon God, an obligation such that if He refrained from fulfilling it to its due recipient, He would become an unjust tyrant. Then, when He fulfills this right to its due recipient, gratitude becomes incumbent upon the servant; if the servant refrains from performing it, he becomes an unjust tyrant, a fool, preventing a due right. Then, when he performs that, he deserves from God Almighty a reward in such a way that if God Almighty did not grant it to him, He would be an unjust tyrant. This is a departure from reason. How can one who fulfills what is due from him to its recipient deserve something?
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that God's favors are obligatory and that human obedience generates a claim on God. Imam al-Nasafi begins with an analogy: a person who receives a gift from a great king takes it as an honor and is grateful, but if the same item were purchased from a merchant, the merchant gains no special status. This illustrates that the value of a favor is enhanced by the giver's grandeur. Applying this to God, whose greatness is infinite, any favor from Him, no matter how small, cannot become burdensome due to a sense of obligation (minna), because God's sovereignty and power are absolute, and all things are subject to Him. Then al-Nasafi argues that if the Mu'tazila view were correct—that God's gifts generate obligation—then even Paradise would be tainted for the saints, since they receive it by God's grace, not by their own merit. He explains that no act of obedience can obligate God, because obedience is itself a form of gratitude for prior favors. Moreover, a person cannot fully thank God for even a single favor, because each act of gratitude requires God's enabling grace (tawfiq), which is itself a new favor requiring further gratitude, leading to an infinite regress. This is supported by the Quranic verse that humans cannot count God's favors. Al-Nasafi then criticizes the Mu'tazila doctrine: they claim that God's acts are obligatory (wajib) on Him, and that if He fails to bestow a favor, He would be unjust; then, after bestowing it, the servant owes gratitude; and after gratitude, God owes reward—otherwise He would be unjust again. This, al-Nasafi says, is irrational (khurūj 'an al-ma'qūl). He concludes with a rhetorical question: how can one who has merely fulfilled an obligation to its recipient deserve anything additional? The passage thus defends the Sunni view that God's grace is free, not obligatory.

Key Terms
شكر
توفيق
نعمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that by performing good deeds, they earn a right to reward from God.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that every good deed is itself a gift from God (tawfiq) and a form of gratitude, so it cannot be the basis of a claim.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why can a person never fully thank God for even a single favor?
A) Because the favor is too great to comprehend.
B) Because each act of gratitude requires another enabling grace from God, leading to infinite regress.
C) Because humans are naturally ungrateful.
D) Because gratitude is not required for small favors.

Key Takeaway
Human beings cannot fully discharge gratitude for any of God's favors because each attempt at thanks itself depends on a new favor from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even your deepest gratitude to Allah is itself a gift from Him, what does that reveal about your utter dependence on Him every single moment? Are you comfortable with that total dependence, or does it make you feel powerless?
?
Think of a specific blessing you are thankful for. Now realize that your ability to thank Allah for it is another blessing you haven't thanked Him for. How does this reality affect the way you approach your prayers and worship? Will you now ask Allah for the tawfiq to be grateful?
?
The impossibility of fully thanking Allah could lead to despair or to humility. Which path does your heart take? If humility, how will you express that humility in your actions today, knowing that every good deed is only possible by His grace?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate

Refuting the Mu'tazila Doctrine of Obligation
Al-Nasafi lays out and refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine of threefold obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila view that God's favors, human gratitude, and divine reward form a chain of obligations is irrational.

Core Idea
God cannot be obligated; His grace is free and unearned.

Imam al-Nasafi concludes his refutation by directly critiquing the Mu'tazila position. He outlines their three-step claim: God owes favors to humans, humans owe gratitude, and then God owes reward. He then dismisses this as a 'strange device' that departs from reason. The passage ends with a rhetorical question: how can one who merely fulfills an obligation to its recipient deserve something extra? This lesson examines the logical fallacy in the Mu'tazila reasoning and reaffirms the Sunni doctrine of God's absolute freedom and grace.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three-step Mu'tazila claim
Read the passage and outline the three obligations: God's obligation to bestow favors, the servant's obligation to thank, and God's obligation to reward.
This is the core of the Mu'tazila theory of justice.
2
Consider al-Nasafi's critique
He calls it a 'departure from reason'. Notice the rhetorical question at the end that exposes the flaw.
The flaw is that fulfilling an obligation does not create a new right to compensation.
3
Contrast with Sunni view
Reflect on how the previous lessons (analogy, infinite gratitude) support the conclusion that God's grace is free and not obligatory.
This lesson integrates and concludes the refutation.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that God's favors are obligatory and that human obedience generates a claim on God. Imam al-Nasafi begins with an analogy: a person who receives a gift from a great king takes it as an honor and is grateful, but if the same item were purchased from a merchant, the merchant gains no special status. This illustrates that the value of a favor is enhanced by the giver's grandeur. Applying this to God, whose greatness is infinite, any favor from Him, no matter how small, cannot become burdensome due to a sense of obligation (minna), because God's sovereignty and power are absolute, and all things are subject to Him. Then al-Nasafi argues that if the Mu'tazila view were correct—that God's gifts generate obligation—then even Paradise would be tainted for the saints, since they receive it by God's grace, not by their own merit. He explains that no act of obedience can obligate God, because obedience is itself a form of gratitude for prior favors. Moreover, a person cannot fully thank God for even a single favor, because each act of gratitude requires God's enabling grace (tawfiq), which is itself a new favor requiring further gratitude, leading to an infinite regress. This is supported by the Quranic verse that humans cannot count God's favors. Al-Nasafi then criticizes the Mu'tazila doctrine: they claim that God's acts are obligatory (wajib) on Him, and that if He fails to bestow a favor, He would be unjust; then, after bestowing it, the servant owes gratitude; and after gratitude, God owes reward—otherwise He would be unjust again. This, al-Nasafi says, is irrational (khurūj 'an al-ma'qūl). He concludes with a rhetorical question: how can one who has merely fulfilled an obligation to its recipient deserve anything additional? The passage thus defends the Sunni view that God's grace is free, not obligatory.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God, being just, is obliged to reward good deeds, as if humans have a claim on Him.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the very concept of obligation on God is irrational because God is the sovereign and His actions are free; fulfilling an obligation does not create a new right.

Checkpoint
What is the logical error in the Mu'tazila position according to al-Nasafi?
A) They assume that fulfilling an obligation entitles one to additional reward.
B) They deny that God is just.
C) They say God is not all-powerful.
D) They claim that humans can be completely grateful.

Key Takeaway
God's grace is not a debt; fulfilling an obligation cannot generate a further claim, so the Mu'tazila chain of obligations is irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly believe that your good deeds place God in your debt? How does this passage expose that belief as a form of arrogance? What would change in your worship if you truly accepted that God owes you nothing?
?
When you slip into thinking, 'I've done enough, God should reward me,' what is really at the root of that feeling? Is it trust in God's generosity—or a demand for payment? Which one honors God more, and which one do you actually live by?
?
If fulfilling an obligation cannot create a new right, then your gratitude to God is not a transaction but a response to unearned grace. What stops you from living every moment with that raw gratitude, free from the ledger of merit? Will you let go of that ledger today?

beginner

The Bakriyya Denial of Children's Pain
Refutes the extreme view that children do not feel pain even when mutilated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme rationalist position of the Bakriyya sect that denied the reality of children's pain, and how the author refutes it by appealing to observable reality.

Core Idea
Denying obvious pain is a desperate escape from theological difficulty.

This passage presents a striking example of how heretical sects can go to extreme lengths to avoid theological conclusions they find uncomfortable. The Bakriyya, to avoid attributing unjustified pain to God, claimed that children do not feel pain at all—even when their limbs are cut off. The author refutes this by pointing to the obvious fact of observation (mushahada).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the dramatic claim about children's pain and the names of the sects involved.
The passage includes footnote markers; ignore them for now.
2
Identify the absurd claim and its motivation
The author states that confusion (hayra) forced the Bakriyya to deny observation (mushahada) and resort to ignorance (tajahul). Find the specific claim that follows from this.
The claim is that children do not feel pain even if their limbs are cut off.
3
Understand the author's method of refutation
The author does not provide a lengthy argument; instead he points out that this view is a denial of what is plainly observed. Ask yourself why this is a powerful refutation.
This connects to the importance of empirical reality in theology.

ثم اضطرت الحيرة) (۳) لهم في هذه المسألة طائفة منهم (٤) إلى إنكار المشاهدة والخروج إلى التجاهل، فزعم أن الأطفال لا يتألمون ولو أن أوصالهم قطعت وجعلت أعضاؤهم إربا إربا لما تأملوا بذلك(۹) وهو قول بكر بن عبد عبد (1) ربه رئيس البكرية من أهل البدع وهو المعروف بابن أخت عبد الواحد بن زيد. وروى يحيى بن كامل البصري (9) المعروف بالحجدري هذه المقابلة أيضا عن النجدات (۸)
Then confusion forced a group among them in this matter to denial of observation and resorting to ignorance, so he claimed that children do not suffer pain, even if their joints were cut and their limbs made piece by piece, they would not feel that. And this is the saying of Bakr bin 'Abd 'Abd [?] Rabbih, the leader of the Bakriyya among the people of innovation, who is known as Ibn Ukht 'Abd al-Wahid bin Zaid. And Yahya bin Kamil al-Basri, known as al-Hajdari, also narrated this opposition from the Najadat.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an extreme position taken by some heretical groups (al-Bakriyya and possibly the Najadat) regarding the issue of children's pain. The author states that their confusion led them to deny obvious observation (mushahada) and resort to ignorance (tajahul). They claimed that children do not feel pain at all, even if their limbs are cut off—an absurd claim contradicted by evident reality. The author identifies the originator of this view as Bakr bin 'Abd Rabbih, the leader of the Bakriyya sect (a group of innovators). The passage also mentions that Yahya bin Kamil al-Basri (al-Hajdari) transmitted a similar view from the Najadat (a Kharijite subsect). The author presents this as a reductio ad absurdum of the opponent's position: to avoid the conclusion that God inflicts pain for no reason, they resorted to denying the observed reality of pain. This is a clear example of how heretical sects deviate from sound rational and empirical judgment.

تجاهل

نجدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Children do not feel pain, even when mutilated.
Correction: Pain is an observable reality; denying it contradicts sensory evidence and reason.

Checkpoint
What absurd claim did al-Bakriyya make about children?
A) Children cannot sin.
B) Children do not feel pain even if their limbs are cut off.
C) Children automatically enter Paradise.
D) Children are responsible for their actions.

Key Takeaway
The Bakriyya's denial of children's pain is a clear example of how heretical sects abandon empirical reality to preserve their doctrinal assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Bakriyya would rather believe that a child feels nothing while being dismembered than admit that their theology had a problem. Is there any area of your life—your relationships, your habits, your beliefs—where you are choosing comfortable falsehood over painful truth? Will you ask Allah to show it to you today?

The Bakriyya's Denial of Children's Pain
Al-Nasafi identifies the Bakriyya and Najdat sects who deny children's pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Bakriyya and Najdat sects denied the observable reality of children's pain.

Core Idea
Some sects denied children's pain so strongly they rejected observation itself.

Some theological debates can lead to extreme conclusions that defy common sense. In this passage, al-Nasafi exposes the astonishing claim of a group that children feel no pain at all, even under torture. This lesson introduces the background and identifies the proponents of this view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Bakriyya claim
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the extreme nature of the claim about children not feeling pain.
2
Identify the sects involved
Notice that al-Nasafi names two sects: the Bakriyya and the Najdat. Understand that this claim is attributed to specific historical figures.
Bakr ibn 'Abd Rabbih is the founder of the Bakriyya; Najda ibn 'Amir is a Kharijite leader.
3
Recognize the logical category
Al-Nasafi classifies this as 'denial of observation' (inkar al-mushahada). Reflect on what it means to deny something that is clearly observable.
This categorisation is important for the refutation that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اضطرت الحيرة لهم في هذه المسألة طائفة منهم إلى إنكار المشاهدة والخروج إلى التجاهل، فزعم أن الأطفال لا يتألمون ولو أن أوصالهم قطعت وجعلت أعضاؤهم إربا إربا لما تأملوا بذلك وهو قول بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية من أهل البدع وهو المعروف بابن أخت عبد الواحد بن زيد. وروى يحيى بن كامل البصري المعروف بالحجدري هذه المقابلة أيضا عن النجدات من الخوارج وهم أصحاب نجدة بن عامر الحنفي المعروف بنجدة الحروى أحد رؤساء الخوارج، وهذا من باب إنكار المشاهدة. فإنا نرى من الأطفال آثار السرور بالأسباب التي يسر أهلها، والحزن والبكاء لأجل أسباب الحزن، ونرى الهزال والضنا يظهر فيهم عند نزول مرض أو جراحة، ويسمع الصراخ والعويل عند ضربة أو صدمة حلت بهم، ثم لو كان الأمر على ما يزعمون لما أثم من ضرب صبيًا ضربا مبرحا أو جرحه جراحة مثخنة إذ لم يصر بذلك مضرابًا لصبي مرديًا له، ثم لو صورت هذه العقيدة لعوام الخليقة لقابلوا القائل بها بالهزء والسخرية، وحكموا عليه بالعناد والتجاهل، ثم كل عاقل يتذكر في نفسه آلاما ما لحقته في الأحوال التي هي قريبة من حالة الكلفة وتوجه المحنة عليه، وهذا لا معنى لتضييع الوقت بالاشتغال بما له من دلائل الإبطال لثبوت ذلك في البداهة وأوائل المعارف.
Then confusion forced a group among them in this matter to deny observation and to resort to feigned ignorance, so they claimed that children do not feel pain, even if their joints were cut and their limbs were made piece by piece, they would not perceive that. This is the statement of Bakr ibn 'Abd Rabbih, the head of the Bakriyya, a people of innovation, known as Ibn Ukht 'Abd al-Wahid ibn Zayd. And Yahya ibn Kamil al-Basri, known as al-Hajdari, also narrated this absurdity from the Najdat, a sect of the Kharijites, who are the followers of Najda ibn 'Amir al-Hanafi, known as Najda al-Haruri, one of the leaders of the Kharijites. This falls under the category of denying observation. For we see in children signs of joy due to causes that gladden their families, and sadness and crying due to causes of sorrow, and we see emaciation and thinness appear in them when illness or injury befalls them, and we hear screaming and wailing when a blow or shock occurs to them. Then if the matter were as they claim, no one would sin by severely beating a child or inflicting a serious wound, since by that he would not be harming the child or destroying him. Moreover, if this doctrine were presented to the common people, they would mock and ridicule the one who says it, and judge him to be obstinate and ignorant. Furthermore, every rational person recalls in himself pains that afflicted him in states close to a condition of hardship and trial. And it is pointless to waste time occupying oneself with something that has such obvious disproofs, since it is established by intuition and primary cognitions.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the extreme claim made by some sects (the Bakriyya and the Najdat) who denied that children feel pain, in order to avoid the implications of the doctrine of al-Aslah. Al-Nasafi begins by stating that confusion forced this group to deny observable reality and resort to willful ignorance. They claimed that even if children's limbs are cut, they do not feel pain. He identifies the proponent as Bakr ibn 'Abd Rabbih. Then he lists empirical evidence: we see children showing joy and sadness, we see physical effects like emaciation from illness, we hear screams of pain. He then argues that if pain were not real, then harming a child would not be sinful, which is absurd. He also notes that common sense would ridicule such a claim. Finally, he says every rational person recalls their own past pains. Therefore, this belief is so obviously false that it does not merit further time.

النجدات

Misconception: Children do not feel pain at all, even when tortured.
Correction: This contradicts direct observation and is considered a denial of evident reality.

B) They do not feel pain

D) They are not harmed

Key Takeaway
The Bakriyya and Najdat sects denied children's pain, a stance that al-Nasafi classifies as denial of observable evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's classification of the Bakriyya's view as 'denial of observation' challenge you to instead accept the full weight of such pain and then seek a coherent theological response? What would it cost you to honestly confront that pain rather than dismiss it?
?
Is there an area of your life where you are tempted to deny plain evidence because it would be easier than reconciling it with your beliefs? Think of a specific instance. What would it mean for you to accept that evidence and change your understanding accordingly?
?
The Bakriyya and Najdat were willing to contradict observable reality to maintain their theological system. Are you willing to let your theology be uncomfortable by confronting difficult truths, or do you choose comforting illusions? Examine your own heart: when a teaching of Islam seems to conflict with your experience, do you suppress the experience or seek a deeper understanding?
