Refuting the Kharijite Misinterpretation of the Disobedience Verse
Nasafi refutes the Kharijite proof from the Qur'anic threat against disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Kharijite proof from the Qur'anic threat against disobedience and defends the position that major sins do not nullify faith.

Core Idea
Disobedience does not automatically mean unbelief or eternal punishment.

The Kharijites argued that Qur'anic verses threatening punishment for disobedience prove that any major sin leads to eternal hellfire. In this passage, Imam Nasafi dismantles their proof using a powerful dilemma about the prophets' own slips, and then shows that the verse actually refers to disbelievers, not believers.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Kharijite proof
The Kharijites cite the Qur'anic verse that threatens anyone who disobeys Allah and His Messenger. Read the start of the passage to see how they used this as evidence that major sins lead to eternal punishment.
2
Follow the dilemma about prophets
Nasafi asks: were the slips of the prophets (like Adam and David) considered disobedience? If not, the Kharijites contradict the Qur'an. If yes, then did the prophets become disbelievers and deserve eternal fire? Either way, the Kharijite position is undermined.
Notice that both options lead to a contradiction for the Kharijites.
3
Analyze the alternative interpretation
Nasafi then offers his own reading: the verse is about those who make disobedience lawful (istihlal) and specifically applies to disbelievers, as shown by phrases like 'transgresses His limits' and 'prepared for the disbelievers'.
This interpretation aligns with the principle that faith is not nullified by sin.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
The lesson concludes by reaffirming that faith remains intact despite major sins, and that eternity in fire is only for disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة رأي الخوارج] وتعلق الخوارج بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَعْصِ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ ﴾ الآية قلنا لهم: ما تقولون في زلات الأنبياء عليهم السلام أنها هل كانت تسمى عصيانا؟ فإن قالوا: لا. كذبوا الله تعالى في قوله: ﴿ وَعَصَى عَادَمُ رَبَّهُ، فَغَوَى ﴾ ونسبوه إلى الظلم حيث عاتب داود وغيره من الأنبياء على ما وجد منهم من الزلات وأبطلوا مغفرته لداود بقوله : ﴿ فَغَفَرْنَا لَهُ ذَلِكَ ﴾ وذلك كفر . وإن قالوا: كان ذلك منهم عصيانًا قيل: فهل كفروا واستحقوا الخلود في النار ؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. كفروا وإن قالوا: لا. أبطلوا دليلهم. ثم الآية مصروفة إلى الاستحلال على أن في الآية دليلاً أنها وردت في الكافر لأنه قال : "ويتعد حدوده" والحدود اسم جمع والمؤمن لا يتعدى جميع حدود الله، وقوله: ﴿ لَا يَصْلَهَا إِلَّا الأَشْقَى ﴾ أي على طريق الإصالة، وكذا قوله: ﴿ أُعِدَّتْ للكفرين ﴾ على طريق الإصالة والله أعلم. على أنا بينا بما تلونا من الآيات وذكرنا من المعقول أن الإيمان لا ينعدم بالكبيرة، ولا بما دون التكذيب من العصيان، ولا خلود مع الإيمان، فكان ذلك دليلاً أن هذه الآية وردت في الكافر.
Discussion of the Kharijites' opinion] And the Kharijites clung to His statement the Exalted: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger...' the verse. We said to them: What do you say about the slips of the prophets, peace be upon them—were they called disobedience? If they say: No. They have belied Allah the Exalted in His statement: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord, and went astray,' and they have attributed injustice to Him where He reproached David and other prophets for the slips that occurred from them, and they have voided His forgiveness of David by His statement: 'So We forgave him that.' And that is unbelief. And if they say: That was disobedience from them, it is said: Did they then disbelieve and deserve eternity in the Fire? If they say: Yes. They have disbelieved. And if they say: No. They have voided their proof. Then the verse is turned to the meaning of making lawful disobedience; moreover, in the verse is evidence that it came regarding the disbeliever because He said: 'and transgresses His limits,' and 'limits' is a collective noun, and the believer does not transgress all the limits of Allah; and His statement: 'None shall enter it except the most wretched,' that is, in the primary sense; and likewise His statement: 'Prepared for the disbelievers' in the primary sense. And Allah knows best. Moreover, we have shown by what we recited of the verses and mentioned from reason that faith is not nullified by a major sin, nor by disobedience short of denial, and there is no eternity with faith. So that was evidence that this verse came regarding the disbeliever.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Kharijite argument that major sins entail eternal punishment. Nasafi begins by stating their proof from the Qur'anic threat against disobedience. He then poses a dilemma regarding the slips (zallat) of the prophets: if the Kharijites deny that those were disobedience, they contradict the Qur'an which describes Adam's act as 'disobedience' and attribute injustice to Allah for punishing prophets for slips; if they admit it was disobedience, then they must explain whether the prophets thereby became disbelievers deserving eternal fire. If they say yes, they commit kufr; if no, their proof is invalidated. Then Nasafi offers an alternative interpretation: the verse is about those who make disobedience lawful (istihlal) and is specifically for disbelievers, as indicated by the collective noun 'limits' (the believer does not transgress all limits) and by the phrase 'most wretched' and 'prepared for the disbelievers'. He concludes by reiterating the established rational and scriptural evidence that faith is not nullified by major sins, so the verse must refer to disbelievers.

Key Terms
المعصية
الكبيرة
الخلود
الاستحلال
الزلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Kharijites believed that any major sin (kabira) makes a person a disbeliever who will remain in Hell forever, based on verses like 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger...'
Correction: Nasafi shows that this verse applies only to disbelievers or those who make disobedience lawful, and that the prophets' own slips demonstrate that disobedience does not entail unbelief or eternal punishment for believers.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation of the Khawarij regarding the verse about disobedience?
A) The verse only applies to disbelievers or those who make disobedience lawful.
B) The verse shows that all sinners will be in hell forever.
C) The verse applies to prophets as well.
D) The verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Kharijite proof by showing that the Qur'anic threat verse applies only to disbelievers, not to believers who commit major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a single mistake or sin made you 'the most wretched' in God's sight, beyond forgiveness? How does the fact that even prophets had slips and were forgiven challenge that feeling? What will you do the next time such a thought comes?
?
When you see another Muslim committing a major sin, does your heart lean toward condemning them as 'outside the faith'? This lesson shows that faith remains even with sin. How can you actively combat that tendency in your heart starting this week?
?
The Kharijites used a verse to justify harsh judgment. Are there any verses or hadiths you are tempted to use to judge others or yourself harshly? What would it cost you to seek a fuller understanding before reaching a verdict?

Major Sin Does Not Make One a Polytheist or Hypocrite
Nasafi defines shirk and nifaq to refute equating major sinners with polytheists/hypocrites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that a major sinner is neither a polytheist nor a hypocrite, based on the precise definitions of shirk and nifaq.

Core Idea
Major sins do not change one's category to polytheism or hypocrisy; those are defined by belief, not action.

In this passage, Nasafi directly confronts the view that major sins expel one from faith, making one a polytheist or hypocrite. He begins by defining these terms precisely and shows that major sinners simply do not meet the criteria. The story of Joseph's brothers powerfully illustrates that even grave sins do not equate to hypocrisy.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of shirk and nifaq
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that Nasafi defines shirk as belief in a partner in worship or creation, and he defines hypocrisy as a distinct essence.
Shirk involves belief, not just action.
2
See why a major sinner is not a polytheist
The major sinner does not hold the belief in a partner, so he cannot be called a polytheist.
3
See why a major sinner is not a hypocrite
The essence of hypocrisy is something else; the major sinner is actually the opposite of a hypocrite. The hadith about signs of a hypocrite is about outward signs, not about being a hypocrite itself.
Hypocrisy is inward disbelief, not outward sin.
4
Examine the story of Joseph's brothers
Ata used this story to argue that even extreme sins (treachery, lying, breaking promises) did not make Joseph's brothers hypocrites. This convinced Hasan al-Basri to retract his view.
The story shows that sin does not inherently imply hypocrisy.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Internalize that major sins are destructive but do not change one's fundamental religious identity unless they involve denial or shirk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان قول من يقول إنه مشرك أو منافق]: وبتلك الآيات يبطل قول من يجعله مشركًا أو منافقا، فإن الله تعالى أبقى الإيمان وكذا الإشراك اعتقاد شريك الله تعالى إما في العبادة كمشركي العرب، وإما التخليق كالمجوس، وصاحب الكبيرة لا يعتقد ذلك ولا يشرك البتة بعبادة ربه أحدا، وقد بينا أيضا مائيَّة النفاق وأن صاحب الكبيرة ليس بداخل في حده بل هو على الضد من حال المنافق، وما ذكر من الحديث فذلك علامة المنافق في الأغلب فإما أن تكون هي بنفسها نفاقًا فلا، وقد روى أن عطاء لما سمع مذهب الحسن قال: قولوا له إن أخوة يوسف ائتمنوا فخانوا حيث ألقوه في غياهب الجب، وحدثوا فكذبوا بقولهم: أكله الذئب، ووعدوا بقولهم: وإنا له لحافظون فأخلفوا هل صاروا بذلك منافقين ؟ فقيل للحسن ذلك، فقال: صدق عطاء ورجع عن ذلك على أن الحديث يمكن حمله على الاستحلال، والله الموفق. ثم إن هذا الرجل كان مؤمنًا لما معه من التصديق وبالكبيرة ما انعدم، وما حدث له من الأسماء الخبيثة كالفاسق والظالم والفاجر وغيرها، فتلك أسماء يستحقها بأفعال ليست بمنافية للإيمان فثبوتها لا يكون منافيًا لكونه مؤمنًا، ثم لما لم يصر كافراً لانعدام التكذيب لم لم يبق مؤمنًا ومعه التصديق ولم يوجد منه ما يضاده وقد خرج الجواب عن تعلقهم بقوله تعالى : ﴿ الَّذِينَ يَأْكُلُونَ أَمْوَالَ الْيَتَامَى ظُلْمًا ﴾
Invalidation of the statement of one who says he is a polytheist or a hypocrite] And by those verses, the statement of one who makes him a polytheist or a hypocrite is invalidated. For Allah the Exalted preserved faith. And likewise polytheism is the belief in a partner with Allah the Exalted, either in worship like the polytheists of the Arabs, or in creation like the Magians. And the committer of a major sin does not believe that, nor does he associate anyone with his Lord in worship at all. And we have also explained the essence of hypocrisy, and that the committer of a major sin is not included in its definition; rather he is the opposite of the state of the hypocrite. And what is mentioned of the hadith, that is a sign of the hypocrite in most cases; but as for it being itself hypocrisy, no. And it has been narrated that when 'Ata' heard the opinion of al-Hasan, he said: 'Tell him: the brothers of Joseph were entrusted and they betrayed when they threw him into the bottom of the well, and they spoke and lied by saying: "The wolf ate him," and they promised by saying: "And indeed we are his protectors," and they broke their promise. Did they become hypocrites by that?' And al-Hasan was told that, and he said: 'Ata' spoke the truth,' and he retracted from that opinion. Moreover, the hadith can be interpreted as referring to making that behavior lawful. And Allah is the granter of success. Then, this man was a believer because of what he had of affirmation (tasdiq), and by the major sin it was not nullified. And what occurred to him of evil names, such as 'fasiq' (transgressor), 'zalim' (wrongdoer), 'fajir' (immoral), and others—these are names he deserves for actions that are not incompatible with faith. So their establishment is not incompatible with his being a believer. Then, since he did not become a disbeliever due to the absence of denial, why should he not remain a believer while he has affirmation, and nothing that opposes it has occurred from him? And the answer to their clinging to His statement the Exalted: 'Those who consume the wealth of orphans unjustly...' has been given.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage invalidates the view that a major sinner is a polytheist or a hypocrite. Nasafi first defines polytheism (shirk) as belief in a partner, whether in worship (like Arab polytheists) or in creation (like Magians). The major sinner does not hold such belief. He then explains the essence (ma'iyya) of hypocrisy (nifaq) and shows that the major sinner is the opposite of a hypocrite. He addresses a hadith that mentions signs of a hypocrite, clarifying that those signs are typical but not themselves the actual hypocrisy. He relates a story where 'Ata' (Wassil bin 'Ata') challenged al-Hasan al-Basri using the example of Joseph's brothers: they acted treacherously, lied, and broke promises—did that make them hypocrites? Al-Hasan agreed and retracted his earlier view. Nasafi then argues that the major sinner remains a believer due to his inward tasdiq (affirmation); major sins do not nullify faith. The evil names like fasiq, zalim, fajir are earned through actions that are not incompatible with faith itself. Finally, he notes that the answer to their proof from the verse about consuming orphans' wealth has been given, implying that such verses refer to disbelievers or those who make it lawful.

Key Terms
الإشراك
النفاق
الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Committing major sins makes one a polytheist or a hypocrite.
Correction: Polytheism requires belief in a partner for Allah, and hypocrisy requires inner denial of faith. Major sins alone do not meet these definitions.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is a major sinner not a polytheist?
A) Because he still performs prayers
B) Because he does not believe in a partner with Allah
C) Because he repents after sinning
D) Because major sins are minor compared to shirk

Key Takeaway
Major sins do not make one a polytheist or a hypocrite, as these categories are defined by specific beliefs, not by sinful actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you ever secretly doubt that you are still a believer? How does Nasafi’s clear definition of shirk and nifaq confront that fear? What will you do today to anchor your identity in your belief rather than your mistakes?
?
The brothers of Joseph betrayed trust, lied, and broke promises—yet they were not hypocrites. Think of a sin you have fallen into repeatedly. Does knowing that your belief in your heart saves you from the label of hypocrisy bring you hope or complacency? What specific step will you take this week to turn back to Allah without despairing of His mercy?
?
If the signs of a hypocrite—lying, breaking promises, betraying trust—can appear in a believer, what does that mean for your own character? Which of these signs have you excused in yourself? How will you use the lesson of Joseph’s brothers to hold yourself accountable without losing your identity as a believer?

5 min
The Major Sinner Remains a Believer
Nasafi argues that major sinners remain believers due to tasdiq and that evil names do not negate faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that a major sinner remains a believer because inward affirmation (tasdiq) is not nullified by sin, and that evil names like fasiq are not incompatible with faith.

Core Idea
Faith is based on inward affirmation; major sins do not destroy faith unless they involve denial.

After refuting the labels of polytheism and hypocrisy, Nasafi turns to the positive case: the major sinner retains faith because the core of faith—tasdiq (affirmation)—remains intact. He addresses that evil names like fasiq and zalim are earned through actions that are not incompatible with faith itself. He then responds to a Quranic verse used by opponents, noting that it refers to those who make sin lawful, not to believers who sin.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the central role of tasdiq
Nasafi states that the major sinner was a believer because of his tasdiq (affirmation). The major sin did not nullify this affirmation.
Tasdiq is the heart of faith.
2
Understand evil names are not faith-canceling
Names like fasiq, zalim, and fajir are earned by sinful actions, but these actions are not incompatible with faith; thus, these names do not negate being a believer.
One can be a believer and still be a transgressor.
3
Examine the logical argument
Nasafi argues: since the major sinner did not become a disbeliever (because he did not deny), he remains a believer because he still has tasdiq and nothing opposes it.
4
Consider the answer to the Quranic verse
The verse about consuming orphans' wealth unjustly was used by opponents. Nasafi says the answer has already been given, implying it refers to those who make it lawful, not to believers who sin while knowing it is wrong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Invalidation of the statement of one who says he is a polytheist or a hypocrite] And by those verses, the statement of one who makes him a polytheist or a hypocrite is invalidated. And the committer of a major sin does not believe that, nor does he associate anyone with his Lord in worship at all. Did they become hypocrites by that?' And al-Hasan was told that, and he said: 'Ata' spoke the truth,' and he retracted from that opinion. Moreover, the hadith can be interpreted as referring to making that behavior lawful. And Allah is the granter of success. Then, this man was a believer because of what he had of affirmation (tasdiq), and by the major sin it was not nullified. And what occurred to him of evil names, such as 'fasiq' (transgressor), 'zalim' (wrongdoer), 'fajir' (immoral), and others—these are names he deserves for actions that are not incompatible with faith. So their establishment is not incompatible with his being a believer. Then, since he did not become a disbeliever due to the absence of denial, why should he not remain a believer while he has affirmation, and nothing that opposes it has occurred from him? And the answer to their clinging to His statement the Exalted: 'Those who consume the wealth of orphans unjustly...' has been given.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage invalidates the view that a major sinner is a polytheist or a hypocrite. Nasafi first defines polytheism (shirk) as belief in a partner, whether in worship (like Arab polytheists) or in creation (like Magians). The major sinner does not hold such belief. He then explains the essence (ma'iyya) of hypocrisy (nifaq) and shows that the major sinner is the opposite of a hypocrite. He addresses a hadith that mentions signs of a hypocrite, clarifying that those signs are typical but not themselves the actual hypocrisy. He relates a story where 'Ata' (Wassil bin 'Ata') challenged al-Hasan al-Basri using the example of Joseph's brothers: they acted treacherously, lied, and broke promises—did that make them hypocrites? Al-Hasan agreed and retracted his earlier view. Nasafi then argues that the major sinner remains a believer due to his inward tasdiq (affirmation); major sins do not nullify faith. The evil names like fasiq, zalim, fajir are earned through actions that are not incompatible with faith itself. Finally, he notes that the answer to their proof from the verse about consuming orphans' wealth has been given, implying that such verses refer to disbelievers or those who make it lawful.

Key Terms
التصديق
الفاسق
الظالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling someone a fasiq or zalim means they are no longer a believer.
Correction: These names are for sinful actions, which are not incompatible with faith. A person can be a believer and still be a fasiq.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the reason a major sinner remains a believer?
A) Because he performs good deeds
B) Because he still has tasdiq (affirmation)
C) Because he never committed major sins deliberately
D) Because he repents immediately

Key Takeaway
A major sinner remains a believer as long as he retains inward affirmation (tasdiq); sinful acts earn blameworthy names but do not negate faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone committing a major sin, do you ever doubt the sincerity of their faith? Nasafi insists they remain believers. How does that challenge your tendency to judge others’ hearts, and what change will you make in your thoughts or actions starting today?
?
Think of a major sin you have committed or struggle with. Did you feel your faith was weakened or lost? This lesson says tasdiq keeps you a believer. But does that give you comfort or make you complacent? What does this knowledge demand of you in terms of repentance and drawing closer to Allah?
?
The passage mentions evil names like 'fasiq' and 'zalim' that apply to sinners. Are there any of these names that describe your own actions? How will you reconcile the fact that you remain a believer with the urgency to abandon that sin and seek forgiveness, rather than taking the security of faith for granted?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Generality and the Dispute on Wa'id
Describes the disagreement between Mu'tazila and early Hanafis on generality's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between the Mu'tazila and early Hanafis regarding the necessity of believing in generality and its connection to the issue of wa'id.

Core Idea
Generality is not always obligatory to believe; linguistic usage allows specificity.

This passage delves into a key methodological disagreement between the Mu'tazila and early Hanafi scholars. At issue is whether a general statement must always be interpreted as fully general, or whether it is permissible to intend a specific meaning. The debate ties directly to the theological question of wa'id — whether divine threats of punishment are necessarily fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrasting positions of the two groups.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents the Mu'tazila and the early Hanafis. Identify what each group holds regarding the interpretation of general words.
The Mu'tazila insist on the literal general meaning; the Hanafis allow intended specificity.
3
Understand the linguistic argument
The Hanafis argue that Arabic speakers often use a general word intending specific. This undercuts the obligation to believe in literal generality.
This is a key linguistic point.
4
Note the disagreement on delayed specification
The Mu'tazila consider delayed proof of specification as abrogation (naskh), while the Hanafis see it as clarification (bayan). This affects how they view the original intent.
This is a methodological difference.
5
Connect to wa'id
Both groups link this principle to wa'id. The Hanafi position leaves room for pardon; the Mu'tazila position requires actualization of threats.
Thus, the debate is not merely linguistic but theological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يزل المعتزلة يتمسكون بهذا الكلام. وكان أوائل أصحابنا يتنازعونهم في هذا الأصل أشد المنازعة، ولا يسلمون لهم أن القول بالعموم واجب الاعتقاد لما أن التكلم بلفظة العموم يراد بها الخصوص سائغ فيما بين أهل اللسان حتى كان ذلك يغلب على إرادة الحقيقة وهو العموم، وكذا اللفظ العاري عن القيد يجوز أن يكون المراد به القيد عند مشايخنا، وهم يأبون ذلك أشد الإباء ويجعلون دليل التخصيص أو القيد إذا كان متأخرا عن العام أو المطلق نسخاً لا بيانًا أن المتكلم أراد به من أول ما تكلم ما وراء ما دخل تحت القيد ودليل الخصوص، وأصحابنا كانوا يجعلون ذلك بيانًا وكان الفريقان جميعًا يجعلان هذا الأصل من نتائج مسألة الوعيد، وكان كل من تكلم من أوائلنا وأوائلهم في مسألة العام والمطلق يذكرون أن القول بها يؤدي إلى القول بتحقيق الوعيد أو بجواز العفو.
The Mu'tazila continued to cling to this statement. And the early ones of our companions disputed with them over this principle with the most intense dispute, and they did not grant them that the statement of generality is obligatory to believe, because speaking a general word intending specificity is permissible among the people of the language, so much so that it may override the intention of the reality, which is generality. Likewise, the word bare of a qualification may be intended with a qualification according to our mashayikh, and they strongly refused that and considered the proof of specification or qualification, if it comes after the general or absolute, to be abrogation, not clarification—that the speaker intended from the beginning what is beyond what falls under the qualification and the proof of specification. And our companions considered that to be clarification. And both groups all considered this principle to be a result of the issue of wa'id. And everyone who spoke from our early ones and their early ones in the issue of the general and absolute mentioned that the statement on it leads to the statement of actualizing the wa'id or permissibility of pardon.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage explains the prolonged dispute between the Mu'tazila and the early Hanafi scholars (ashabuna) regarding the principle of generality. The Mu'tazila insisted on the previous argument. The Hanafis strongly contested them, refusing to concede that belief in generality is obligatory. Their reasoning is that Arabic speakers often use a general word while intending a specific meaning, and this is common; thus they do not consider a general form as always indicating its literal scope. Similarly, an unqualified word may be intended as qualified. The Mu'tazila objected, considering a delayed proof of specification or qualification as abrogation, not clarification, because they believe the speaker originally intended the general meaning. The Hanafis saw it as clarification (bayan). Both groups, however, recognized that this whole issue is a result of the question of wa'id (the threat of punishment for sinners). Whoever discusses the general and absolute inevitably addresses whether it leads to affirming the actualization of the threat or the possibility of pardon (al-'afw).

Key Terms
عموم
خصوص
وعيد
عفو
إباء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hanafis completely rejected the concept of generality itself.
Correction: They only disputed the necessity of believing in the literal general meaning as obligatory, not the linguistic validity of generality. They acknowledged that general words can be used, but argued that it is permissible to intend specific meanings, so one cannot automatically assume the general scope without evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the early Hanafis, why did they not consider belief in generality as obligatory?
A) Because general words always intend specific.
B) Because the Mu'tazila said so.
C) Because Arabic speakers often intend specific from general.
D) Because it leads to abrogation.

Key Takeaway
The debate over generality is not merely linguistic; it directly affects the theological question of whether divine threats of punishment must be fulfilled or whether pardon is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a verse like 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the fire of Hell' (72:23), do you instinctively believe it applies to you personally, or do you assume it only applies to others? What does your reaction reveal about your hidden assumptions about God's mercy and justice?
?
The early Hanafis defended the possibility of divine pardon by arguing that general statements can intend specific meanings. Are you willing to 'allow' God that linguistic freedom, or do you insist He must fulfill every literal threat? Which position gives you more genuine hope, and why?
?
This debate was not just academic for the early scholars—it determined whether they lived in fear or hope. Which camp do you find your heart leaning toward, and what does that say about your relationship with Allah's promises and warnings? Will you consciously embrace the position that leads you to greater taqwa and trust?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner

Al-Jassas: Murji'ah Rejected Generality to Avoid Wa'id
Al-Jassas quotes himself on the Murji'ah's rejection of generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jassas linked the Murji'ah's rejection of generality to their doctrine of irja'.

Core Idea
The Murji'ah rejected generality to escape the Qur'anic threat against sinners.

Al-Jassas, a Hanafi usuli, argues that the principle of generality is rooted in the Arabic language and unanimously accepted by the Salaf. The only opposition came from a later faction of Murji'ah who found that the apparent verses imply a definite punishment for sinners. To avoid this conclusion, they abandoned generality entirely. This passage shows how theological commitments can influence methodological principles.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Jassas's argument
Read the quoted passage from al-Jassas. Notice how he frames generality as a linguistic and consensus-based principle.
2
Identify the Murji'ah's motive
Al-Jassas states the Murji'ah rejected generality because they felt constrained by the doctrine of irja' and wanted to avoid being compelled to affirm wa'id.
Wa'id means threat of punishment for sinners.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: it shows that methodological choices in usul can be driven by prior theological positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى إن أبا بكر الجصاص قال بعد ما استشهد لصحة قوله فبان بما وصفنا أن العموم من مفهوم لسان العرب، وأن ذلك من مذهب السلف من غير خلاف بينهم وما خالف من هذا أحد من السلف ومن بعدهم إلى أن نشأت فرقة من المرجئة ضاق عليهم المذهب في القول بالإرجاء فلجأت إلى دفع القول بالعموم رأسًا لئلا يلزمها لخصومها القول بوعيد الفساق بظاهر الآي المقتضية لذلك. هذا كله لفظه ذكره في كتابه المصنف في أصول الفقه وقال أيضا في أول هذا الباب وحكى لي أبو الطيب بن شهاب عن أبي الحسن الكرخي أنه قال : إني أقف في عموم الأخبار وأقول بالعموم في الأمر والنهي، فقلت لأبي الطيب فهذا يدل على أن مذهبه كان الوقف في وعيد فساق أهل الملة، فقال : هكذا كان مذهبه، قال الجصاص وأبو الطيب هذا غير مهم عندي فيما يحكيه، وقد جالس أبا سعيد البردعي وشيوخنا القدماء ولم أسمع أن أبا الحسن الكرخي يفرق بين الخير والأمر والنهي في ذلك بل كان يقول بالعموم والإطلاق.
Until Abu Bakr al-Jassas said, after he cited evidence for the correctness of his statement: 'So it became clear by what we described that generality is from the concept of the Arabic language, and that it is from the way of the Salaf without any disagreement among them, and no one from the Salaf or after them opposed this until a faction of the Murji'ah arose, and the doctrine of irja' became constrained for them, so they resorted to rejecting the statement of generality entirely, lest their opponents compel them to the statement of wa'id against sinners by the apparent verses necessitating that.' This is all his wording; he mentioned it in his book 'al-Musannaf fi Usul al-Fiqh'. And he also said at the beginning of this chapter: 'Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab narrated to me from Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi that he said: I stop (waqif) regarding the generality of reports, and I say generality in command and prohibition.' So I said to Abu al-Tayyib: 'This indicates that his school was stopping regarding the wa'id of sinful people of the religion.' He said: 'Thus was his school.' Al-Jassas said: 'And Abu al-Tayyib is not important to me in what he narrates. He accompanied Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i and our early shaykhs, and I did not hear that Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi differentiated between report and command and prohibition in that; rather he used to say generality and absoluteness.'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents a direct quote from Abu Bakr al-Jassas. He argues that the principle of generality (al-'umum) is a well-established concept in the Arabic language and the way of the Salaf, without any early disagreement. The only opposition came from a faction of the Murji'ah, who found the doctrine of irja' (postponement of judgment) problematic because the apparent verses imply a definite threat (wa'id) against sinners. To avoid this conclusion, they rejected generality outright. Al-Jassas then reports an anecdote: Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab narrated that Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi said he stops (waqf) regarding the generality of reports (akhbar) but affirms generality in commands and prohibitions. Al-Jassas interprets this as al-Karkhi stopping regarding the wa'id of sinful believers. However, al-Jassas doubts this narration, noting that Abu al-Tayyib's reliability is not significant to him, and he never heard al-Karkhi differentiate between reports and commands; rather, al-Karkhi affirmed generality in all cases.

Key Terms
مرجئة
إرجاء
وعيد
عموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the rejection of generality is a valid methodological position independent of theology.
Correction: Al-Jassas argues that the Murji'ah rejected generality precisely to avoid a theological implication, not based on linguistic or usul reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jassas, why did the Murji'ah reject the principle of generality?
A) Because they had a different linguistic theory.
B) To avoid being compelled to affirm the threat (wa'id) against sinners.
C) Because they followed the Salaf.
D) Because they rejected all Qur'anic verses.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jassas links the Murji'ah's rejection of generality to their attempt to avoid the Qur'anic threat against sinners, showing theology can drive usul positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a clear command in the Qur'an or Sunnah that you find difficult to fully obey. How have you rationalized your shortcomings? Are you unknowingly doing what the Murji'ah did—twisting principles to avoid the weight of Allah's words? Repent and resolve to submit fully.
?
When you read verses of punishment (wa'id), do you feel a personal urgency to change your ways, or do you mentally distance yourself, thinking 'that's for extreme sinners'? What does your response reveal about the state of your iman? Let this lesson be a mirror.
?
The Murji'ah allowed their doctrine to override the apparent meaning of the Qur'an. Is there any belief or habit you hold so dear that you would twist the meaning of Allah's revelation to preserve it? Pray for sincerity and courage to embrace the truth even when it hurts.
